Prophethood
beginner
4 min
Maturidi's Emphasis on Prophethood
The passage notes Maturidi's greater interest in prophethood compared to other mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Maturidi showed a distinctive emphasis on prophethood compared to other mutakallimun, and that this interest was continued by later scholars.

Core Idea
Maturidi is the theologian with the greatest interest in prophethood.

This passage from al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid highlights his unique focus on prophethood (nubuwwa). While the Ash'ari tradition relied heavily on al-Baqillani for this topic, Maturidi himself dedicated significant attention to it, and his approach was adopted by later scholars like Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The passage sets the stage for understanding Maturidi's broader theological project.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the key point about Maturidi's emphasis.
The passage is incomplete but conveys a clear historical claim.
2
Identify the scholars mentioned
Note that al-Baqillani is associated with the Ash'ari school on this topic, while al-Maturidi is highlighted as the one with greatest interest. Also note that later scholars like Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi continued his approach.
3
Reflect on the historical context
Consider why opposition to prophethood arose after Islam's strength and why this topic mattered in early kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل النبوات إن موضوع النبوات في الجناح الأشعري من الكلام السني قد تعرض فيه خصوصا الإمام الباقلاني الذي يعتبر مكملاً لتأسيس المذهب بعد مؤسسه الأول. غير أن الإمام الماتريدي الذي عاش قبل الباقلانى زمنا قريبًا من القرن، هو المتكلم الذي أبدى الاهتمام الأكبر لمسائل النبوات. ولعل المعارضة على النبوة قد بدأت مباشرة عقب إثبات الإسلام قوته بالمنطقة، فظهر الموقف الإسلامي في الساحة وقدم أدلته في الموضوع. وأما عن الطريق الذي فتحه الماتريدي في الاهتمام بموضوع النبوات، فقد استمر فيه المؤلفون فيما بعد. فأبو المعين النسفي
Matters of prophethood. The topic of prophethood in the Ash‘ari wing of Sunni kalam was especially addressed by Imam al-Baqillani, who is considered the completer of the school after its first founder. However, Imam al-Maturidi, who lived before al-Baqillani at a time close to the century, is the mutakallim who showed the greatest interest in matters of prophethood. And perhaps opposition to prophethood began immediately after Islam established its strength in the region, so the Islamic position appeared in the arena and presented its proofs on the subject. As for the path that al-Maturidi opened in interest in the topic of prophethood, later authors continued in it. So Abu al-Mu‘in al-Nasafi...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage introduces Maturidi's treatment of prophethood (nubuwwat). It notes that in the Ash‘ari tradition, al-Baqillani is a key figure in this topic, but Maturidi, who lived earlier, showed even greater interest. The passage suggests that opposition to prophethood arose early in Islamic history, and Muslim scholars presented proofs. Maturidi's path of interest in prophethood was continued by later scholars such as Abu al-Mu‘in al-Nasafi. The passage is incomplete but highlights Maturidi's pioneering role.

Key Terms
النبوة
مسائل النبوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi was primarily concerned with divine attributes and had little to say about prophethood.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Maturidi showed the greatest interest in matters of prophethood among the mutakallimun, demonstrating his significant emphasis on this topic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholar is said to have shown the greatest interest in matters of prophethood among the early theologians?
A) Al-Baqillani
B) Al-Ash‘ari
C) Al-Maturidi
D) Abu al-Mu‘in al-Nasafi

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi is distinguished by his exceptional focus on prophethood, a topic he addressed more extensively than other early mutakallimun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi made prophethood his greatest concern. When you study Islam, which topics draw your deepest attention? Does your priority align with the centrality of prophethood in your faith?
?
The passage notes that opposition to prophethood arose when Islam became strong. In your own life, have you ever felt pressure to downplay or soften the role of prophethood due to external circumstances? How will you consciously uphold its importance starting today?

Prophethood
beginner
6 min
The Five Groups Denying Prophethood
Enumeration of five groups rejecting prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five groups that deny prophethood according to Imam al-Maturidi and understand their core positions.

Core Idea
Five denials of prophethood: from atheism to rationalism to skepticism about miracles.

In this lesson, we explore Maturidi's classification of those who reject prophethood. He identifies five distinct groups, each with a different basis for denial. Understanding these categories is essential for recognizing the variety of objections to divine revelation throughout history.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the five groups
Read the Arabic passage that lists the five groups. Notice how each group is introduced with 'منهم من' (among them are those who).
The passage comes from a larger discussion of prophethood in Kitab al-Tawhid.
2
Identify each group's position
For each group, identify the specific reason they deny prophethood. The first group denies God entirely; the second acknowledges God but rejects His commands; the third relies on reason alone; the fourth claims miracles can be matched by sorcery; the fifth doubts the inability of opponents.
3
Reflect on the variety of objections
Consider how these five groups represent different levels of opposition: from atheism to deism to rationalism to skepticism. Maturidi's classification shows he addressed a wide spectrum of views.
This variety helps us understand why Maturidi devoted so much space to prophethood.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Pause and summarize in your own words the five denials. This reinforces the core idea that prophethood is denied on multiple fronts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل النبوات إن موضوع النبوات في الجناح الأشعري من الكلام السني قد تعرض فيه خصوصا الإمام الباقلاني الذي يعتبر مكملاً لتأسيس المذهب بعد مؤسسه الأول. غير أن الإمام الماتريدي الذي عاش قبل الباقلانى زمنا قريبًا من القرن ، هو المتكلم الذي أبدى الاهتمام الأكبر لمسائل النبوات. ولعل المعارضة على النبوة قد بدأت مباشرة عقب إثبات الإسلام قوته بالمنطقة، فظهر الموقف الإسلامي في الساحة وقدم أدلته في الموضوع . وأما عن الطريق الذي فتحه الماتريدي في الاهتمام بموضوع النبوات، فقد استمر فيه المؤلفون فيما بعد. فأبو المعين النسفي الذي يعتبر شارحًا لمؤلفات الماتريدي في الكلام والتفسير ، نراه قد استقل بموضوعات النبوة في تبصرة الأدلة في قسم يقرب حجمه من ١٠٠ صفحة، وطلب ألا يؤآخذ عليه لهذا الحجم الكبير المخالف لتخطيطه العام الذي وضعه للكتاب، وذلك بسبب وقوع هجمات التيارات المنكرة للنبوة . وقد تناول الماتريدي في مواضع عديدة من تأويلات القرآن موضوعات متعددة تتعلق بالنبوة وعلى رأسها موضوع إثبات نبوة الرسول عليه السلام وفي كتاب التوحيد هناك قسم حجمه سبعون صفحة يتناول في القسم الأكثر من نصف الحجم المذكور موضوع الحاجة إلى مؤسسة النبوة. فقد قال في هذا القسم بأن الرسالة قد أثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر، وأنكرها خمس فرق آتية منهم من جهل بالصانع وأنكره، ومنهم من أنكر الأمر الإلهي ونهيه رغم الإقرار به سبحانه، ومنهم من أقر بذلك كله وزعم أن العقل مستقل بدوره ومستغن عن الرسالة، ومنهم من ادعى إمكان مقابلة معجزات مدعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة، ومنهم من قال باحتمال ظهور عجز الناس الذين يتوجه إليهم صاحب الرسالة بها عبر التاريخ فلم يكن لهم في تلك الموضوعات علم ولا خبرة ولم يمتحن قواهم في ذلك، فلعله لو امتحن لوجد من يقدر على مثله ويعارضه في صنيعه وقد أجاب الماتريدي للاعتراض الأول من تلك الاعتراضات الموجهة إلى النبوة في القسم الذي سبق مسائل النبوات. وقد حاول أيضًا أن يتناول الباقي منها على حدة بتقديم أدلة متعددة في الموضوع. ففي تلك الاستدلالات يشير إلى أن الله تعالى حكمة في أفعاله، وأنه لم يخلق الإنسان الذي كرمه من بين مخلوقاته ولا العالم الذي أوجده من أجله لهدف يقصد به وجودهما الأبدي، وأنه لن يكون هناك نظام ولا حياة دون الإرشاد من قبل الوحي الإلهي، وأن العقل لن يستطيع مواصلة سيره الفكري إلى عالم الأبد منفردًا بقدراته؛ فيحاول الماتريدي تقديم تلك الأفكار لكي يبيّن من ورائها ما يعتمد فيها من العقل وطبيعة الإنسان والحقائق الاجتماعية وما هو متعارف عبر تاريخ البشر .
Issues of prophethood. Indeed, the topic of prophethood in the Ash'ari wing of Sunni theology was particularly addressed by Imam al-Baqillani, who is considered the completer of the founding of the school after its first founder. However, Imam al-Maturidi, who lived before al-Baqillani by nearly a century, is the theologian who showed the greatest interest in issues of prophethood. Perhaps opposition to prophethood began immediately after Islam established its power in the region, so the Islamic position appeared on the scene and presented its proofs on the subject. As for the path that al-Maturidi opened in interest in the topic of prophethood, later authors continued it. Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, who is considered an expounder of al-Maturidi's works in theology and exegesis, we see that he treated prophethood topics independently in his 'Tabṣirat al-Adilla' in a section nearly 100 pages long, and he asked not to be blamed for this large size contrary to his general plan for the book, because of the occurrence of attacks from currents denying prophethood. Al-Maturidi addressed in many places of his 'Ta'wilat al-Qur'an' various topics related to prophethood, foremost of which is the topic of establishing the prophethood of the Messenger, peace be upon him. And in the 'Kitab al-Tawhid' there is a section seventy pages long, more than half of which deals with the need for the institution of prophethood. He said in this section that the message has been confirmed by the imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity, and it was denied by five groups: among them are those who are ignorant of the Maker and deny Him; among them are those who deny the divine command and prohibition despite acknowledging Him, glorified; among them are those who acknowledge all that and claim that reason is independent in its role and independent of the message; among them are those who claim the possibility of matching the miracles of the claimant of prophethood with the work of priests, sorcerers, and conjurers; and among them are those who say that it is possible that the people to whom the claimant of prophethood addresses his message have lacked ability throughout history and have no knowledge or experience in those matters, and have not tested their powers therein, perhaps if they were tested, someone would be found who is able to do the like and oppose him in his deed. And al-Maturidi answered the first of those objections directed against prophethood in the section preceding the issues of prophethood. He also attempted to treat the rest individually by presenting multiple proofs on the subject. In those arguments, he points out that God, exalted, is wise in His actions, and that He did not create man, whom He honored among His creatures, nor the world which He created for his sake, for a purpose aiming at their eternal existence, and that there will be no order nor life without guidance from divine revelation, and that reason cannot continue its intellectual journey to the eternal world alone with its capacities; thus al-Maturidi presents these ideas in order to show behind them what he relies on from reason, human nature, social realities, and what is customary throughout human history.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a description of Maturidi's treatment of prophethood. It begins by contrasting his work with that of al-Baqillani in Ash'arism, noting Maturidi's greater emphasis on this topic. Then it mentions later scholars like al-Nasafi who expanded on Maturidi's approach. The core of the passage identifies five groups that deny prophethood: (1) those who deny God, (2) those who acknowledge God but reject His commands, (3) those who affirm God and commands but claim reason suffices without revelation, (4) those who equate prophets' miracles with sorcery, and (5) those who argue that people might have been able to produce similar miracles if they had tried. Maturidi answered the first objection earlier, and for the rest he provided proofs: he argues that God is wise and created humans for an eternal purpose, that society needs divine guidance for order, and that reason alone cannot reach eternal truths. The passage thus presents Maturidi's rational and social arguments for the necessity of prophethood.

Key Terms
النبوة
الرسالة
العقل
معجزات
الكهنة
السحرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi only lists three groups or that the objections are purely philosophical.
Correction: Maturidi carefully identifies five distinct groups, each with a concrete basis for denial, including atheism, deism, rationalism, and skepticism about miracles.

Checkpoint
How many groups denying prophethood does Maturidi list, and which group claims reason is independent of revelation?
A) Four groups; the third group
B) Five groups; the third group
C) Five groups; the fourth group
D) Six groups; the second group

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi identifies five distinct groups that deny prophethood, ranging from atheism to rationalism to skepticism about miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these five attitudes toward prophethood do you find hiding in your own heart? Have you ever acted as if your reason is sufficient without needing divine guidance? What would change in your daily life if you truly submitted to the prophetic message not just theoretically but practically?
?
The second group acknowledges God but denies His commands. Where in your life do you acknowledge God's existence but resist His commands? What specific command are you most reluctant to obey?
?
The fifth group suggests that if people had tried, they could have matched the prophet's miracle. Have you ever downplayed a miracle in your life, attributing it to coincidence or natural causes? How does this lesson call you to recognize the unique divine signature in prophethood?

Prophethood
intermediate
8 min
Maturidi's Rational Arguments for Prophethood
Summarizes Maturidi's rational proofs for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key rational arguments Imam al-Maturidi uses to prove the necessity of prophethood against the five denying groups.

Core Idea
Prophethood is necessary because God is wise, creation has purpose, society needs guidance, and reason alone is insufficient.

After cataloging the five groups that deny prophethood, Maturidi presents multiple rational proofs to affirm its necessity. He argues from God's wisdom, the purpose of creation, the need for social order, and the limitations of human reason. These arguments form a comprehensive defense of prophethood grounded in reason and human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Maturidi's arguments
Read the Arabic passage that describes Maturidi's rational responses. Identify the key points he makes: God's wisdom, purpose of creation, need for divine guidance, and reason's incapacity for eternal matters.
This passage follows immediately after the enumeration of the five groups in the source text.
2
Analyze each argument individually
Break down each argument: 1) God's actions are wise and purposeful; 2) creation has an eternal purpose; 3) order and life require revelation; 4) reason cannot independently reach eternal truths.
Notice how Maturidi combines theological, teleological, and epistemological arguments.
3
Reflect on the refutation of the five groups
Consider how each argument addresses the objections of the five groups. For example, the argument from God's wisdom refutes the atheist group; the argument from reason's limitation refutes the rationalist group.
This shows the systematic nature of Maturidi's response.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Summarize in one sentence why Maturidi believes prophethood is necessary. This reinforces the core idea that prophethood is essential for human guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل النبوات إن موضوع النبوات في الجناح الأشعري من الكلام السني قد تعرض فيه خصوصا الإمام الباقلاني الذي يعتبر مكملاً لتأسيس المذهب بعد مؤسسه الأول. غير أن الإمام الماتريدي الذي عاش قبل الباقلانى زمنا Issues of prophethood. Indeed, the topic of prophethood in the Ash'ari wing of Sunni theology was particularly addressed by Imam al-Baqillani, who is considered the completer of the founding of the school after its first founder. However, Imam al-Maturidi, who lived before al-Baqillani by nearly a century, is the theologian who showed the greatest interest in issues of prophethood. Perhaps opposition to prophethood began immediately after Islam established its power in the region, so the Islamic position appeared on the scene and presented its proofs on the subject. As for the path that al-Maturidi opened in interest in the topic of prophethood, later authors continued it. Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, who is considered an expounder of al-Maturidi's works in theology and exegesis, we see that he treated prophethood topics independently in his 'Tabṣirat al-Adilla' in a section nearly 100 pages long, and he asked not to be blamed for this large size contrary to his general plan for the book, because of the occurrence of attacks from currents denying prophethood. Al-Maturidi addressed in many places of his 'Ta'wilat al-Qur'an' various topics related to prophethood, foremost of which is the topic of establishing the prophethood of the Messenger, peace be upon him. And in the 'Kitab al-Tawhid' there is a section seventy pages long, more than half of which deals with the need for the institution of prophethood. He said in this section that the message has been confirmed by the imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity, and it was denied by five groups: among them are those who are ignorant of the Maker and deny Him; among them are those who deny the divine command and prohibition despite acknowledging Him, glorified; among them are those who acknowledge all that and claim that reason is independent in its role and independent of the message; among them are those who claim the possibility of matching the miracles of the claimant of prophethood with the work of priests, sorcerers, and conjurers; and among them are those who say that it is possible that the people to whom the claimant of prophethood addresses his message have lacked ability throughout history and have no knowledge or experience in those matters, and have not tested their powers therein, perhaps if they were tested, someone would be found who is able to do the like and oppose him in his deed. And al-Maturidi answered the first of those objections directed against prophethood in the section preceding the issues of prophethood. He also attempted to treat the rest individually by presenting multiple proofs on the subject. In those arguments, he points out that God, exalted, is wise in His actions, and that He did not create man, whom He honored among His creatures, nor the world which He created for his sake, for a purpose aiming at their eternal existence, and that there will be no order nor life without guidance from divine revelation, and that reason cannot continue its intellectual journey to the eternal world alone with its capacities; thus al-Maturidi presents these ideas in order to show behind them what he relies on from reason, human nature, social realities, and what is customary throughout human history.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a description of Maturidi's treatment of prophethood. It begins by contrasting his work with that of al-Baqillani in Ash'arism, noting Maturidi's greater emphasis on this topic. Then it mentions later scholars like al-Nasafi who expanded on Maturidi's approach. The core of the passage identifies five groups that deny prophethood: (1) those who deny God, (2) those who acknowledge God but reject His commands, (3) those who affirm God and commands but claim reason suffices without revelation, (4) those who equate prophets' miracles with sorcery, and (5) those who argue that people might have been able to produce similar miracles if they had tried. Maturidi answered the first objection earlier, and for the rest he provided proofs: he argues that God is wise and created humans for an eternal purpose, that society needs divine guidance for order, and that reason alone cannot reach eternal truths. The passage thus presents Maturidi's rational and social arguments for the necessity of prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's arguments in the passage, which of the following is NOT a reason for the necessity of prophethood?
A) God's wisdom in His actions
B) The eternal purpose of creation
C) The ability of reason to reach eternal truths independently
D) The need for divine guidance for social order

Key Takeaway
Maturidi provides four rational arguments—from divine wisdom, purpose of creation, social order, and limitation of reason—to demonstrate the necessity of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live each day with the conviction that this life is not the final end, or do your actions betray a love for this world that contradicts the eternal purpose God created you for? What one habit will you change today to align with that purpose?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on divine guidance from revelation? How might your life look different if you honestly sought and submitted to the Qur'an and Sunnah before trusting your intellect alone?
?
If you believe that without prophethood there is no order or meaningful life, how does your community reflect that belief? Are you actively working to spread the guidance of revelation, or do you assume society can manage without it? What will you do this week to prioritize divine guidance in your home or circle?

Prophethood

Refuting the Claim that Miracles Can Be Countered by Sorcery
Maturidi refutes al-Warraq by redirecting focus to deeper distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that prophets' miracles are just like sorcerers' tricks by focusing on deeper distinctions of character, influence, message, and societal impact.

Core Idea
Prophets are distinguished not by miracle type but by character, influence, message, and societal impact.

Al-Warraq, a skeptic, argued that the miracles claimed by prophets could be matched by the tricks of priests and sorcerers. Maturidi's response does not deny the external similarity; instead, he shifts the ground to the profound differences in the person of the prophet, the effect on people, the content of the message, and its lasting stability. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Al-Warraq claimed that a prophet's miracles can be countered or matched by the tricks of priests, sorcerers, and conjurers. This objection challenges the distinctiveness of prophethood.
This sets up the problem Maturidi addresses.
2
Read Maturidi's Response
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that Maturidi does not argue against the possibility of external similarity but instead points to deeper differences.
The passage is a single sentence that summarizes his approach.
3
Identify the Four Distinguishing Aspects
From the commentary, identify the four elements that Maturidi uses to distinguish prophets: (1) personal character, (2) ability to influence people, (3) meanings contained in the message, (4) stability of the message in society.
These are the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the Significance
Ask yourself why these four aspects are more fundamental than the miracles themselves. How do they prove that prophethood is genuine and not mere sorcery?
This connects the argument to the broader proof of prophethood.
5
Test Your Understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasped the basis of Maturidi's distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذهب إليه الورّاق حول إمكان مقابلة معجزات مدعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة، فقد استدل عليه الماتريدي بتوجيه انتقادات لكي يفرق بين هؤلاء الأشخاص وبين شخصية الرسل وقدرة تأثيرههم في الناس والمعاني التي تحتويها رسالتهم وثباتها بالمجتمع .
As for what al-Warraq went to regarding the possibility of matching the miracles of the claimant of prophethood with the work of priests, sorcerers, and conjurers, al-Maturidi argued against it by directing criticisms in order to distinguish between these individuals and the character of the messengers, their ability to influence people, the meanings contained in their message, and its stability in society.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's refutation of a specific objection raised by al-Warraq, who claimed that a prophet's miracles could be matched by the tricks of priests and sorcerers. Maturidi's response is to direct criticisms that distinguish prophets from such figures. The distinction is based on three aspects: (1) the personal character of the messengers, (2) the powerful impact they have on people, (3) the profound meanings contained in their message, and (4) the lasting stability of their message in society. This shows that Maturidi rejects the equivalence by appealing to the overall quality and effect of prophethood, not just the external form of miracles.

Key Terms
الورّاق
معجزات
الكهنة
السحرة
المشعبذة
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies any similarity between miracles and sorcery.
Correction: Maturidi acknowledges the external similarity but focuses on deeper distinctions of character, influence, message, and societal impact.

Checkpoint
On what basis does Maturidi distinguish prophets from sorcerers according to this passage?
A) Prophets perform greater miracles in size
B) Prophets have better character, influence, message content, and societal stability
C) Prophets never perform miracles that look like sorcery
D) Sorcerers cannot perform any miracles at all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the equivalence of miracles and sorcery by highlighting that prophets are distinguished by their character, influence, message, and societal stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who claims to have a divine message, do you tend to focus on the external 'signs' and 'miracles' they show, or do you carefully examine their character, their influence on others, the depth of their message, and its lasting impact? Which of these four aspects have you neglected most in your own judgments? Commit to changing that this week.
?
Think of a time when you were impressed or misled by someone's persuasive abilities or apparent supernatural feats. Did you pause to consider whether they possessed the moral character and the timeless truth of a prophet? If you had applied Maturidi's criteria, how would your judgment have been different? What will you do differently next time you are faced with a persuasive claimant?
?
If you aspire to call others to truth, which of the four distinguishing aspects—character, influence, message meaning, or societal stability—do you most lack? What is one concrete step you can take today to strengthen that aspect in your own life so that your call is more aligned with the prophetic model?

The Five Groups Denying Prophethood According to Maturidi
Lists five groups denying prophethood and notes Maturidi's initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct positions that reject prophethood as categorized by Imam Maturidi, and how these positions arise from different underlying assumptions about God, reason, and miracles.

Core Idea
Prophethood is denied from five distinct angles — not just one.

Imam Maturidi begins his treatment of prophethood by noting that it has been affirmed by righteous leaders and wise men throughout history. However, he identifies five groups that reject it, each for a different reason. This lesson maps out these five positions and sets the stage for his responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the translation. Identify the five groups as they are listed. Notice the progression from outright denial of God to more subtle arguments.
2
Understand the rationale of each group
For each group, ask: What is their core argument against prophethood? Are they denying God, denying revelation, or claiming self-sufficiency?
The groups are not merely labels; each has a distinct theological position.
3
Reflect on the order
Notice that the groups are arranged from the most extreme (denial of God) to the most subtle (historical skepticism). This order shows the range of objections Maturidi addresses.
4
Connect to Maturidi's response
The passage ends by noting that Maturidi already answered the first objection earlier. Think about how the remaining four objections might be answered using arguments from intellect and revelation.
This lesson only introduces the groups; the responses are covered in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد قال في هذا القسم بأن الرسالة قد أثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر، وأنكرها خمس فرق آتية منهم من جهل بالصانع وأنكره، ومنهم من أنكر الأمر الإلهي ونهيه رغم الإقرار به سبحانه، ومنهم من أقر بذلك كله وزعم أن العقل مستقل بدوره ومستغن عن الرسالة، ومنهم من ادعى إمكان مقابلة معجزات مدعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة، ومنهم من قال باحتمال ظهور عجز الناس الذين يتوجه إليهم صاحب الرسالة بها عبر التاريخ فلم يكن لهم في تلك الموضوعات علم ولا خبرة ولم يمتحن قواهم في ذلك، فلعله لو امتحن لوجد من يقدر على مثله ويعارضه في صنيعه وقد أجاب الماتريدي للاعتراض الأول من تلك الاعتراضات الموجهة إلى النبوة في القسم الذي سبق مسائل النبوات.
For he said in this section that the message (al-risāla) has been affirmed by the imams of guidance, the leaders of good, and the wise of mankind, and it has been denied by five groups: among them are those who were ignorant of the Maker and denied Him; among them are those who denied the divine command and prohibition despite acknowledging Him, exalted is He; among them are those who acknowledged all of that but claimed that the intellect is independent in its role and self-sufficient from the message; among them are those who claimed the possibility of matching the miracles of the claimant to prophethood with the practices of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers; among them are those who said it is possible that the inability of the people to whom the claimant of prophethood addressed himself through history appeared, and they had no knowledge or experience in those matters and did not test their powers in that, so perhaps if he had tested, he would have found someone able to do the like and oppose him in his work. And al-Maturidi answered the first of those objections directed at prophethood in the section that preceded the topics of prophethoods.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five groups that deny prophethood according to Maturidi and notes his initial response. It first states that prophethood has been affirmed by the righteous leaders and wise, but denied by five groups. The first group denies the existence of the Creator entirely. The second group acknowledges God but denies His command and prohibition. The third group acknowledges everything but claims the intellect is sufficient without revelation. The fourth group claims that the miracles of a prophet can be matched by sorcery or trickery. The fifth group argues that the inability of people to oppose a prophet throughout history might be due to lack of opportunity or testing, not divine support. Maturidi answered the first objection in a previous section.

Key Terms
رسالة (message/prophethood)
فرق (groups)
عقل (intellect)
معجزة (miracle)
كهانة (soothsaying)
صانع (Maker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five groups are mutually exclusive and cover all possible denials of prophethood.
Correction: The groups are not necessarily mutually exclusive; some individuals or sects may combine aspects of multiple groups. Maturidi lists them as distinct categories for analytical clarity.

Checkpoint
How many groups does Maturidi list as deniers of prophethood?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Maturidi identifies five distinct arguments against prophethood, ranging from denial of God to skepticism about human capacity to challenge miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the five groups' reasoning resonates most with your own heart? Do you ever rely solely on your intellect and feel you don't need divine guidance through the Prophet ﷺ? Examine yourself honestly — where have you made your intellect independent of revelation?
?
The second group acknowledges God but disobeys His commands. Where in your life do you say 'I believe in God' but then ignore or downplay a clear command from the Qur'an or Sunnah? That is a dangerous inconsistency. Will you repent and realign your actions with your claim of faith?
?
The fifth group doubts because they think people could have challenged the Prophet's miracles if they had the chance. Are you waiting for a miracle to submit fully? Do you require a sign before you obey Allah's Messenger? Your faith must be based on the established proof of prophethood, not hypothetical objections. Will you surrender today without demanding more evidence?

Maturidi's Rational Proofs for Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates rational proofs for prophethood's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational arguments for the necessity of prophethood based on divine wisdom, human nature, and the limitations of the intellect.

Core Idea
Prophethood is rationally necessary because intellect alone cannot guide to eternal truth.

Imam al-Maturidi, a foundational figure in Sunni theology, argues that prophethood is not merely a historical accident but a rational necessity. In this passage, he presents multiple proofs grounded in God's wisdom, the purpose of creation, and the inherent limits of human reason. Let's explore these arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the provided Arabic text and its English translation to grasp Maturidi's main points.
Pay attention to the logical flow: he builds from God's wisdom to the need for revelation.
2
Identify the key arguments
List the specific arguments Maturidi offers: divine wisdom in actions, purpose of creation, necessity of revelation, and intellect's inability alone.
Each argument supports the same conclusion: prophethood is essential.
3
Reflect on the role of the intellect
Consider Maturidi's view that intellect has limits and cannot reach the eternal world by itself. How does this justify the need for prophets?
This is a key point distinguishing Maturidi from some rationalist thinkers.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think about how these arguments fit into Maturidi's overall system, which balances reason and revelation.
This lesson lays groundwork for understanding prophethood in Sunni Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد حاول أيضًا أن يتناول الباقي منها على حدة بتقديم أدلة متعددة في الموضوع. ففي تلك الاستدلالات يشير إلى أن الله تعالى حكمة في أفعاله، وأنه لم يخلق الإنسان الذي كرمه من بين مخلوقاته ولا العالم الذي أوجده من أجله لهدف يقصد به وجودهما الأبدي، وأنه لن يكون هناك نظام ولا حياة دون الإرشاد من قبل الوحي الإلهي، وأن العقل لن يستطيع مواصلة سيره الفكري إلى عالم الأبد منفردًا بقدراته؛ فيحاول الماتريدي تقديم تلك الأفكار لكي يبيّن من ورائها ما يعتمد فيها من العقل وطبيعة الإنسان والحقائق الاجتماعية وما هو متعارف عبر تاريخ البشر . وأما ما ذهب إليه الورّاق حول إمكان مقابلة معجزات مدعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة، فقد استدل عليه الماتريدي بتوجيه انتقادات لكي يفرق بين هؤلاء الأشخاص وبين شخصية الرسل وقدرة تأثيرههم في الناس والمعاني التي تحتويها رسالتهم وثباتها بالمجتمع . وأما عن الاعتراضات التي وجهها الورّاق حول إعجاز القرآن الكريم ، فقد أجاب عليها الماتريدي معتمدًا فيها . ابن الراوندي وموقفه تجاه الموضوع . وعمقها
And he also attempted to treat the rest of them separately by presenting multiple proofs on the subject. In those arguments, he indicates that God Almighty has wisdom in His actions, and that He did not create man, whom He honored among His creatures, nor the world, which He created for his sake, for a purpose intended for their eternal existence, and that there will be no order nor life without guidance from divine revelation, and that the intellect cannot continue its intellectual journey to the eternal world alone by its own abilities; thus al-Maturidi tries to present these ideas in order to show through them what he relies upon from the intellect, human nature, social realities, and what is customary throughout human history. As for what al-Warraq held regarding the possibility of matching the miracles of the claimant to prophethood with the practices of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers, al-Maturidi argued against him by directing criticisms in order to distinguish between these persons and the character of the messengers, their power of influence over people, the meanings contained in their message, and its stability in society. And as for the objections that al-Warraq directed concerning the inimitability (iʿjāz) of the Noble Quran, al-Maturidi answered them relying on [؟] Ibn al-Rawandi and his stance on the subject. And its depth.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's rational arguments for the necessity of prophethood and his response to al-Warraq. First, Maturidi attempts to address the remaining objections individually by providing multiple proofs. He argues that God's actions are based on wisdom; He did not create humans and the world for a temporary purpose but for an eternal one. Therefore, divine guidance (prophethood) is necessary for order and life. The intellect alone cannot reach the eternal realm. Maturidi thus combines rational, human, social, and historical evidence. Then, regarding al-Warraq's claim that miracles can be matched by sorcery, Maturidi distinguishes between prophets and soothsayers/sorcerers based on their character, influence, message content, and lasting societal impact. Finally, it mentions that al-Warraq also objected to the Quranic inimitability, and Maturidi answered him, with reference to Ibn al-Rawandi, though the text is fragmented.

Key Terms
حكمة (wisdom)
وحي (revelation)
عقل (intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the intellect insufficient for reaching the eternal world?
A) The intellect is inherently flawed
B) The intellect cannot continue alone
C) The intellect is not needed
D) The intellect is superior to revelation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proofs show that prophethood is required because intellect alone cannot secure order, life, or eternal guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect cannot independently guide you to eternal truth, where do you currently place your ultimate trust in matters of faith and salvation? Are you living in reliance on revelation or on your own reasoning?
?
Maturidi argues that without divine revelation, there is no order or life. In which area of your life are you most tempted to seek order apart from God's guidance? What step will you take today to realign that area with revelation?
?
The passage says creation has an eternal purpose. How does this truth challenge the way you spend your time, energy, and resources? What one change in your daily routine would reflect that eternal perspective?

Maturidi Refutes Al-Warraq: Miracles vs. Sorcery
Maturidi distinguishes prophets from sorcerers and answers al-Warraq on miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes genuine prophethood from sorcery and soothsaying, and his response to al-Warraq's objections about miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles of prophets are unique in character, message, and lasting impact, unlike sorcery.

Al-Warraq, a critic of prophethood, argued that miracles could be matched by sorcery. In this passage, Maturidi refutes this by highlighting fundamental differences between prophets and sorcerers. He also addresses objections about the Quranic inimitability. Let's examine his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the text to understand the context: al-Warraq's claim and Maturidi's response.
Focus on the criteria Maturidi uses to distinguish prophets from sorcerers.
2
Identify the distinguishing criteria
List the specific points Maturidi mentions: character of persons, influence, message content, and societal stability.
These are not arbitrary but grounded in observable differences.
3
Analyze the refutation of al-Warraq
Consider why Maturidi's distinctions are effective. How do they undermine the comparison to sorcery?
Think about the qualitative differences between miracles and magic.
4
Reflect on Quranic inimitability
Note that Maturidi also responded to al-Warraq's objections about the Quran's inimitability, though the text is fragmentary. Reflect on its importance.
This lesson shows the comprehensive defense of prophethood.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT a criterion for distinguishing prophets from sorcerers?
A) The character of the persons
B) Their power of influence over people
C) The number of followers
D) The stability of the message in society

Maturidi refutes al-Warraq by demonstrating that prophetic miracles are unique in their moral character, transformative influence, meaningful message, and enduring impact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four criteria mentioned by Maturidi do you rely on most when assessing a religious teacher? Is your evaluation based on their character, the impact of their message, or something else? Will you honestly apply all four today to someone you currently follow?
?
When you hear a speaker who is eloquent but later learn their lifestyle contradicts their message, how does this lesson challenge your willingness to follow them? What specific step will you take—starting today—to guard your heart from being swayed by mere presentation without substance?
?
Al-Warraq questioned whether miracles were anything more than tricks. Has doubt ever crept into your heart about the truth of prophethood? Which of Maturidi's distinctions can you now hold onto as a shield when that doubt returns? Will you commit to memorizing those four criteria and reciting them when your faith is tested?

Maturidi's Proof of Prophethood: Miracles and Social Context
Maturidi's proof of prophethood through miracles, Quran, and social context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument for Muhammad's prophethood based on the classification of miracles and the social need for revelation.

Core Idea
Miracles are sensory, rational, and scriptural—Muhammad's prophecy is proven by all three.

Maturidi presents a systematic proof for the prophethood of Muhammad. He first classifies miracles into three types, then supports his argument with Quranic verses, the Prophet's illiteracy, and the social context of revelation. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three miracle types
Read the passage and locate where Maturidi divides miracles into sensory, rational, and scriptural. Notice how he presents this classification as foundational.
This classification became a model for later Maturidi scholars.
2
Understand the illiteracy argument
Maturidi points out that despite the Prophet's illiteracy, the message is comprehensive and logical. Reflect on why this strengthens the proof.
3
Analyze the social context
The passage describes the society's desperate need for new revelation. Consider why Maturidi emphasizes the debates on tawhid and resurrection.
The Prophet's success required divine support.
4
Examine the objection and response
Maturidi refutes the objection that Muhammad was sent only to non-scriptural communities. See how he argues that acceptance of previous revelation implies acceptance of the next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن إثبات رسالة محمد الله فقد لجأ فيه أبو منصور الماتريدي إلى الاستدلال بأنواع المعجزة وأنها تنقسم إلى المعجزات الحسية والعقلية والسمعية ، فهذا التصنيف للمعجزات قد أصبح مثالاً ونموذجا لعلماء الماتريدية فيما بعد . وبجانب تلك المعجزات قد أيد فكرته بآيات قرآنية تناول فيها الصورة المعنوية للرسول عليه السلام . وقد نبه إلى شمول الرسالة ومنطقيتها رغم أن الرسول الله الله ، كان أميًا، كما أشار إلى النص القرآني وتعبيراته الفنية وتأثيره البالغ بالمجتمع. وكذلك نرى الماتريدي يتعرض إلى الموضوع عن وجهة اجتماعية يبين فيها بأن الأوساط القريبة والبعيدة للرسول عمله الا الله حينئذ كانت في حاجة ماسة إلى وحي إلهي جديد، ثم ما كان بينهم من المحاجة في توحيد الرب وأدلة البعث مما لم يكن يومئذ على وجه الأرض من يدعى ذلك، وأن الرسول الله لن ينجح إلا بالتأييد الإلهي الذي يظهر دعوته، ثم يدعى الإمام بأن تحقق ذلك كله بالواقع يعتبر دليله بالموضوع . وقد اعترض الماتريدي على من بأن محمدا عليه السلام أرسل إلى مجتمعات خارج أهل الكتاب، فذهب إلى أن خط ادعى الوحي مستقيم في ذلك، لأن قبول الوحي الأول يستدعى قبول الآخر أيضا. إن المبدأ الأساسي الذي أسست عليه الأديان السماوية هو توحيد الرب، وأنه ليس له شريك ولا ولد؛ غير أن اليهود قالت بأن عزيرًا ابن الله ، وقالت النصارى المسيح ابن الله، فهم بذلك أفسدوا عقيدة التوحيد . فالأوصاف التي تعلو طبيعة البشر وإضافتها إلى الأنبياء أو القادة الدينية تستلزم إخراج القدسية من حالتها الأصلية إلى حالة الطبيعة والمادية، وهذا يستدعى بالتالي تشويه الدين والماتريدي الذي يعطي اهتماما بالغا بالمقام العالي للألوهية، نراه ...
As for proving the message of Muhammad, may Allah [bless him], Abu Mansur al-Maturidi resorted to inference by the types of miracles, and that they are divided into sensory, rational, and scriptural miracles. This classification of miracles became an example and model for later Maturidi scholars. Alongside these miracles, he supported his idea with Quranic verses in which he addressed the moral image of the Messenger, peace be upon him. He pointed to the comprehensiveness and logic of the message even though the Messenger of Allah, may Allah [bless him], was illiterate, as he also referred to the Quranic text, its artistic expressions, and its profound impact on society. Likewise, we see al-Maturidi approaching the subject from a social perspective, in which he shows that the near and far circles of the Messenger, may Allah [bless him], at that time were in dire need of a new divine revelation, and then what was among them of debate concerning the tawḥīd of the Lord and the proofs of resurrection, of which there was no one on earth at that time claiming that, and that the Messenger of Allah would not succeed except by divine support that makes his call manifest. Then the imam claims that the realization of all that in reality is considered his proof on the subject. And al-Maturidi objected to those who claimed that Muhammad, peace be upon him, was sent to communities outside the People of the Book, so he held that the line of revelation is straight in that regard, because acceptance of the first revelation necessitates acceptance of the other as well. The fundamental principle upon which the heavenly religions are established is tawḥīd of the Lord, and that He has no partner or child; however, the Jews said that Ezra is the son of God, and the Christians said that the Messiah is the son of God, and thus they corrupted the doctrine of tawḥīd. For attributing descriptions that transcend human nature to prophets or religious leaders necessitates removing sanctity from its original state to a natural and material state, and this consequently entails distortion of religion. And al-Maturidi, who gives great attention to the exalted station of divinity, we see him...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's proof of Muhammad's prophethood and his critique of deviations from tawhid. First, Maturidi uses a classification of miracles: sensory, rational, and scriptural, which became a model for later Maturidis. He also uses Quranic verses highlighting the moral image of the Prophet. Despite the Prophet's illiteracy, the message is comprehensive and logical, and the Quran's artistic expression had great societal impact. From a social perspective, Maturidi argues that the society at the time was in desperate need of new divine revelation, and debates about tawhid and resurrection were occurring with no other claimant. The Prophet's success required divine support, and its actual occurrence is the proof. Then Maturidi responds to an objection that Muhammad was sent to non-scriptural communities; he argues that acceptance of previous revelation logically implies acceptance of subsequent revelation. Finally, Maturidi identifies the corruption of tawhid by Jews and Christians: Jews claim Ezra is son of God, Christians claim Christ is son of God. Attributing transcendent descriptions to prophets or leaders debases sanctity and distorts religion. Maturidi emphasizes the exalted station of divinity.

Key Terms
معجزة
حسية
عقلية
سمعية
أمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi only relies on scriptural miracles for proof.
Correction: He explicitly includes sensory and rational miracles as equally important.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the three types of miracles mentioned?
A) Visible, invisible, and verbal
B) Sensory, rational, and scriptural
C) Prophetic, angelic, and demonic
D) Past, present, and future

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves Muhammad's prophethood through a threefold classification of miracles and the social need for revelation, underscoring the consistency of divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you sense its profound impact on society as Maturidi described? Have you ever let that impact change your own life, or do you take it for granted? What would it take for you to truly submit to its message as the final revelation?
?
Maturidi argued that society was in desperate need of divine guidance, debating tawhid and resurrection. Are you aware of your own deep need for guidance in these matters? Do you actively seek answers, or do you assume you already know? Will you let this lesson drive you to examine your own beliefs about God and the afterlife?
?
The consistency of revelation means that accepting earlier scriptures logically demands accepting the final one. Have you ever questioned whether you truly accept the Quran as the final word? If you have doubts, what are you doing to resolve them? Will you commit to studying the proofs until certainty settles in your heart?

Maturidi's Critique of Tawhid Deviations
Maturidi's critique of Jewish and Christian corruptions of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Jewish and Christian doctrines that corrupt the principle of tawhid.

Core Idea
Attributing divine sonship to prophets distorts the sanctity of divinity.

After establishing the proof of prophethood, Maturidi addresses deviations from monotheism. He identifies specific corruptions by Jews and Christians and warns against attributing transcendent descriptions to prophets. This lesson explains his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fundamental principle
Maturidi states that all heavenly religions are founded on tawhid: no partner or child. Read carefully how he phrases this.
2
Recognize the deviations
He cites two specific corruptions: Jews saying Ezra is son of God, Christians saying Christ is son of God. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the consequence
Maturidi explains that attributing transcendent descriptions to prophets takes sanctity from its original state to a natural/material state, distorting religion.
4
Note Maturidi's emphasis
The passage ends by highlighting Maturidi's great attention to the exalted station of divinity. Reflect on why this matters.
This connects to his overall theological project.

Maturidi's Classification of Miracles
Maturidi's classification of miracles into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold classification of miracles—sensory, intellectual, and scriptural—and how it serves as a proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Miracles are divided into sensory, intellectual, and scriptural types.

Maturidi begins his proof of prophethood by establishing a clear classification of miracles. This systematization became a model for later Maturidi scholars. Let's explore these three categories and their role in confirming the prophetic message.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi introduces the three types of miracles.
The passage is concise but foundational.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the terms for the three miracle types: sensory (ḥissiyya), intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and scriptural (samʿiyya). Consider what each might refer to.
Sensory miracles are physically witnessed; intellectual miracles appeal to reason; scriptural miracles are based on revealed texts.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this classification is important for proving prophethood. It provides a comprehensive framework for evaluating the truth of a prophet's claim.
This classification became standard among Maturidis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن إثبات رسالة محمد الله فقد لجأ فيه أبو منصور الماتريدي إلى الاستدلال بأنواع المعجزة وأنها تنقسم إلى المعجزات الحسية والعقلية والسمعية ، فهذا التصنيف للمعجزات قد أصبح مثالاً ونموذجا لعلماء الماتريدية فيما بعد . وبجانب تلك المعجزات قد أيد فكرته بآيات قرآنية تناول فيها الصورة المعنوية للرسول عليه السلام . وقد نبه إلى شمول الرسالة ومنطقيتها رغم أن الرسول الله الله ، كان أميًا، كما أشار إلى النص القرآني وتعبيراته الفنية وتأثيره البالغ بالمجتمع. وكذلك نرى الماتريدي يتعرض إلى الموضوع عن وجهة اجتماعية يبين فيها بأن الأوساط القريبة والبعيدة للرسول عمله الا الله حينئذ كانت في حاجة ماسة إلى وحي إلهي جديد، ثم ما كان بينهم من المحاجة في توحيد الرب وأدلة البعث مما لم يكن يومئذ على وجه الأرض من يدعى ذلك ، وأن الرسول الله لن ينجح إلا بالتأييد الإلهي الذي يظهر دعوته، ثم يدعى الإمام بأن تحقق ذلك كله بالواقع يعتبر دليله بالموضوع . وقد اعترض الماتريدي على من بأن محمدا عليه السلام أرسل إلى مجتمعات خارج أهل الكتاب، فذهب إلى أن خط الوحي مستقيم في ذلك، لأن قبول الوحي الأول يستدعى قبول الآخر أيضا.
And as for the proof of the message of Muhammad الله, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi resorted to reasoning by the types of miracles and that they are divided into sensory, intellectual, and scriptural miracles, so this classification of miracles became an example and model for Maturidi scholars afterwards. Alongside those miracles, he supported his idea with Quranic verses that dealt with the moral image of the Messenger, peace be upon him. He alerted to the comprehensiveness and logicality of the message despite the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, being illiterate, and he also pointed to the Quranic text and its artistic expressions and its profound impact on society. Similarly, we see al-Maturidi approaching the subject from a social perspective in which he explains that the near and far circles of the Messenger, peace be upon him, at that time were in dire need of a new divine revelation, and then what was between them of disputation in the tawhid of the Lord and proofs of resurrection, which at that time there was no one on the face of the earth who claimed that, and that the Messenger of Allah would not succeed except with divine support that manifests his call, then the Imam claims that the realization of all that in reality is considered his evidence on the subject. And al-Maturidi objected to those who claimed that Muhammad, peace be upon him, was sent to societies outside the People of the Book, so he went that the line of revelation is straight in that, because acceptance of the first revelation necessitates acceptance of the other as well.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage describes Maturidi's approach to proving the prophethood of Muhammad through miracles. It begins by stating that Maturidi used reasoning based on the types of miracles, dividing them into three: sensory (ḥissiyya), intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and scriptural (samʿiyya). This classification became a model for later Maturidi scholars. Next, it mentions that Maturidi supported his argument with Quranic verses that address the moral image of the Prophet. He emphasized the comprehensiveness and logicality of the message despite the Prophet being illiterate, and highlighted the artistic expression of the Quran and its impact on society. The passage then notes that Maturidi approached the subject from a social perspective, explaining that the society around the Prophet was in need of a new divine revelation, especially given their debates on tawhid and resurrection which no one else claimed. He argued that the Prophet would only succeed with divine support. Then Maturidi addressed an objection: some claimed that Muhammad was sent only to communities outside the People of the Book. Maturidi responded that the line of revelation is consistent, because acceptance of the first revelation (given to previous prophets) necessitates acceptance of the subsequent one. This defends the universality of Muhammad's prophethood.

المعجزات الحسية
المعجزات العقلية
المعجزات السمعية

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's threefold classification of miracles—sensory, intellectual, and scriptural—provides a structured proof for prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three types of miracles—sensory, intellectual, or scriptural—has the strongest impact on your personal conviction in prophethood? Are you neglecting any? Commit to studying one type you have overlooked.
?
When you recite the Quran, do you primarily experience it as a scriptural miracle, or do you also reflect on its intellectual proof? How can you deepen your appreciation of its inimitability starting today?
?
If you had to explain to a skeptic why prophethood is true using only one type of miracle, which would you choose, and why? Does this lesson reveal a weakness in how you approach dawah? What will you change?

Maturidi's Social Argument and Refutation of an Objection
Social argument for prophethood and refutation of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's social argument for the need of revelation and his response to the claim that Muhammad was sent only to non-ahl al-kitab.

Core Idea
The need for revelation is universal and the line of revelation is continuous.

After establishing the miracle framework, Maturidi presents a social argument: the society around the Prophet was in dire need of a new revelation, and the Prophet's success required divine support. He then refutes an objection that Muhammad was sent only to communities outside the People of the Book, arguing that the line of revelation is straight and consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the two main parts: the social argument and the refutation of the objection.
The objection is stated as a claim by others, and Maturidi responds.
2
Analyze the social argument
Identify the key points: the near and far circles needed revelation, debates on tawhid and resurrection existed nowhere else, and divine support was necessary for success.
Maturidi argues that the historical context validates the prophecy.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection claims Muhammad was sent only to non-ahl al-kitab. Maturidi replies that acceptance of the first revelation (to the People of the Book) implies acceptance of the subsequent revelation to Muhammad.
This defends the universal scope of Muhammad's prophethood.

Commentary
This passage describes Maturidi's approach to proving the prophethood of Muhammad through miracles. It begins by stating that Maturidi used reasoning based on the types of miracles, dividing them into three: sensory (ḥissiyya), intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and scriptural (samʿiyya). This classification became a model for later Maturidi scholars. Next, it mentions that Maturidi supported his argument with Quranic verses that address the moral image of the Prophet. He emphasized the comprehensiveness and logicality of the message despite the Prophet being illiterate, and highlighted the artistic expression of the Quran and its impact on society. The passage then notes that Maturidi approached the subject from a social perspective, explaining that the society around the Prophet was in need of a new divine revelation, especially given their debates on tawhid and resurrection which no one else claimed. He argued that the Prophet would only succeed with divine support. Then Maturidi addressed an objection: some claimed that Muhammad was sent only to communities outside the People of the Book. Maturidi responded that the line of revelation is consistent, because acceptance of the first revelation (given to previous prophets) necessitates acceptance of the subsequent one. This defends the universality of Muhammad's prophethood.

Key Terms
أهل الكتاب
الوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhammad's prophethood was limited to non-ahl al-kitab communities.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the line of revelation is straight: accepting the first revelation (to the People of the Book) necessitates accepting the subsequent revelation to Muhammad, implying universality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the objection that Muhammad was sent only to non-ahl al-kitab fail?
A) Because Muhammad performed miracles
B) Because the line of revelation is straight and acceptance of the first revelation necessitates acceptance of the other
C) Because non-ahl al-kitab rejected the message
D) Because the Quran was revealed in Arabic

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's social argument and refutation establish the universal need for revelation and the consistency of the prophetic line.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in a time filled with debates about God and the afterlife, just as the society Maturidi describes. What are the areas in your own life where you are still disputing or uncertain about tawhid and resurrection? Have you truly turned to divine revelation for answers, or are you relying on your own reasoning alone?
?
Maturidi argues that acceptance of the first revelation necessitates acceptance of the final one. If you believe in the earlier scriptures, do you fully submit to the Quran as the continuation of that same line? Examine your heart: are there commands of the Quran that you resist or neglect, and what does that say about your acceptance of the entire line of revelation?
?
The Prophet's success was not possible without divine support, and the realization of that success is evidence. In your own life, have you ever experienced a situation where you needed Allah's support to succeed in conveying truth? How might your own reliance on divine support be a sign to you of the truth of this message?

Maturidi's Integration of Human Reality and Society
Maturidi emphasizes integration of human reality into theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi grounded his theology in human reality, psychology, and society, and see its application in key topics like prophethood and major sins.

Core Idea
Maturidi's theology is not abstract; it is built on human experience and society.

This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid shows us a side of Maturidi that is often overlooked: his deep engagement with the human condition. Rather than building theology in isolation, he rooted it in the realities of individual psychology and social life. As you read, notice how he connects these elements to specific theological issues, like prophethood and the status of a major sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms.
2

3
Connect to the examples
The passage says we can see the fruits in issues of prophethood and major sins. Think about how these topics require understanding human reality.

Source Text

ولدى الاطلاع على كتابي الماتريدي نراه عالما لم يجرد نفسه عن واقع الحياة، فإنه قد أعطى للعالم البشري دائما مكانة خاصة في جذور الفرد والمجتمع، كما أضاف إلى نظرته هذه الحالة النفسية للإنسان وواقع المجتمع. وقد نستطيع أن نرى ثمار هذه النظرة خاصة في مسائل النبوات ومسائل الكبيرة ومرتكبها بـ كتاب التوحيد.

And we can see the fruits of this view especially in the issues of prophecies (al-nubuwwāt) and the issues of major sin and its perpetrator (al-kabīra wa murtakibuhā) in Kitāb al-Tawḥīd.

It begins by stating that he was not a scholar who isolated himself from real life; rather, he gave constant attention to the human world, considering both individual and social dimensions. He also incorporated the psychological state of humans and the reality of society into his theological perspective. The passage then specifies where this practical orientation can be seen: in topics such as prophethood (al-nubuwwāt) and the issue of major sins and the status of the sinner (al-kabīra wa murtakibuhā) as discussed in Kitāb al-Tawḥīd. This shows that Maturidi's theology was not abstract but engaged with real human concerns.

Prophethood

The Necessity of a Prophetic Unifier from Human Conflict
Maturidi proves the necessity of a divinely appointed leader to unite conflicting human natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof for the necessity of a divinely appointed leader (prophet/imam) to unite conflicting human natures and prevent mutual destruction.

Core Idea
Human nature left unchecked leads to chaos; a wise Creator must send a guide.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument for prophethood based on observation and divine wisdom. He starts by noting that the world is built on opposing natures, especially humans, who have conflicting desires and passions. If left unguided, they would destroy each other, invalidating the wisdom of creation. This leads to the necessity of a unifying principle — a divinely appointed leader with clear proof. In this lesson, we will unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and logic come through.
Notice Maturidi's use of terms like طبائع (natures) and أهواء (inclinations).
2
Identify the problem of human conflict
Maturidi describes humans as having different natures, conflicting desires, and overpowering passions. He asks: what would happen if left to themselves? They would dispute over benefits and honor, leading to hatred, fighting, and mutual destruction.
This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the need for a unifier
Since such conflict would invalidate the wisdom of creation, Maturidi concludes there must be a principle (أصل) to unite them and restrain them. This principle is a divinely appointed leader.
The term أصل refers to the prophet or imam.
4
Recognize the role of divine wisdom
Maturidi argues that a wise Creator would not abandon humans in ignorance and overpowering inclinations without guidance. He must send someone who knows their conditions and can guide them.
This connects to God's attribute of wisdom (حكمة).
5
See the necessity of a proof
The appointed leader must come with a clear proof (دليل وبرهان) so people can recognize his specificity. This establishes the truthfulness of the prophet as the one to whom the world points as refuge.
This proof is often miracles or sound reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي هو مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق؛ وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير. فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو تعلق أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه. مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يُرد بتكوينهم سوى فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم وإذ ثبت ذا لا بد من أصل يؤلّف بينهم ويَكفّهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء فلزم طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. على أن الأحق في ذلك ـ إذ علم بحاجة كل ممن يُشاهد وضرورة كل من المعاين ـ أن لهم مديرًا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدَعُهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء - مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة ما به معاشهم وبقاؤهم دون أن يقيم لهم من يدلّهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له دليلاً وبرهانا يعلمون [به] خصوصه بالذي خصه من الإمامة لهم وأحوجهم إليه... فيما عليه أمرهم. فيكون في ذلك ما بينا: من صدق من ينتهي قوله إلى قول من دلّ عليه العالم بأمر العالم أنه هو الذي جعله المفزع لهم والمعتمد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the world in its origin was built on different natures and opposing aspects, especially that part which is intended by reason, which unites what should be united and separates what should be separated; it is what the philosophers call the 'small world' (microcosm). So it (i.e., mankind) has different inclinations and scattered natures, and overpowering desires embedded in them; if left to what they were created upon, they would dispute over the attraction of benefits and types of honor, nobility, kingship, and authority, leading to hatred then fighting, and in that is mutual destruction and corruption, which if the matter of the world's existence were attached to it, wisdom would be invalidated in its existence. Along with what mankind and all animals are made incapable of surviving except through nourishment and that by which their bodies are sustained for the terms appointed for them. So if He only intended their annihilation, the provision of what sustains them would not be conceivable. And since this is established, there must be a principle to unite them and restrain them from conflict and divergence which brings destruction and annihilation. So it is necessary to seek a principle that unites them to the extent possible within their capacity. Moreover, the most fitting in that—since the need of everyone seen and the necessity of every observed one is known—is that they have a Manager knowledgeable of their conditions and of what their survival depends on, and that He created them with needs; He would not abandon them in their ignorance and overpowering inclinations—along with their need to know what sustains their livelihood and survival—without appointing for them someone to guide them to that and make them know it. And it is necessary that He make for him a proof and evidence by which they know his specificity by that which He distinguished him with in terms of leadership (imāma) for them and made them in need of him... concerning their affairs. So in that which we have explained there is verification of the truthfulness of one whose words lead to the words of him whom the world points to, by the matter of the world, that he is the one whom He made the refuge and support. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the necessity of a prophet or imam based on wisdom. Maturidi first notes that the world is built on opposing natures, especially humans (the 'small world' or microcosm). Humans have conflicting desires and overpowering passions; left unchecked, they would fight and destroy themselves, invalidating the wisdom of creation. Additionally, humans and animals depend on sustenance; if God only intended annihilation, He wouldn't provide means of survival. Therefore, a unifying principle (aṣl) is needed to prevent chaos. The most fitting unifier is a wise manager (mudīr ʿālim) who knows their conditions. Since humans are ignorant and driven by desires, God must send someone with clear proof (dalīl wa burhān) to guide them. This establishes the truthfulness of the prophet as the one to whom the world points as refuge. Maturidi's argument moves from observational conflict to divine wisdom: a wise creator would not leave creation without guidance.

Key Terms
طبائع
أهواء
شهوات
أصل
إمامة
دليل وبرهان
صدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof for prophethood is merely political or sociological, not theological.
Correction: Maturidi grounds the argument in divine wisdom: a wise Creator would not leave humans to their own destructive natures without guidance. The need for a unifier is a rational theological necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if humans were left to their own natures without a unifier?
A) They would live in peace and harmony.
B) They would fight and destroy each other.
C) They would become enlightened philosophers.
D) They would invent advanced technology.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that a wise Creator must send a prophet with clear proof to guide humanity away from self-destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you felt your own desires pulling you into conflict with others? What step can you take today to submit a particular conflict to that divine guidance?
?
Do you live as though the Prophet (peace be upon him) is your only refuge and support in disagreements, or do you rely more on your own reasoning or societal norms? What would change in your relationships if you truly believed that his way is the sole path away from destruction?
?
Reflect on a time you ignored prophetic guidance because it seemed inconvenient. Maturidi says left to ourselves we are overcome by ignorance and desires. Can you pinpoint a specific desire that led you away from the Sunnah? Will you repent and recommit to following that guidance, no matter the cost?

Prophethood
