Necessity of a Divinely Appointed Imam for Guidance
Imam Maturidi argues for the necessity of a divinely appointed imam to guide people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that humanity requires a divinely appointed and divinely proven imam to guide them out of conflict and ignorance.

Core Idea
A divinely appointed imam with clear proof is necessary for human guidance and preservation.

Imam Maturidi presents a rational argument for the necessity of a divinely appointed leader. He begins by observing that humans naturally fall into conflict and self-destruction, making a unifying authority essential. However, not just any leader will do—the leader must be appointed by the all-knowing Creator and validated by a clear proof. This passage grounds the doctrine of imamate in both human need and divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentative style; Maturidi builds his case step by step.
2
Identify the problem: human conflict
The passage starts by stating that humans tend toward conflict and annihilation, making it impossible for them to sustain themselves without a unifying origin.
This is a premise derived from observing human nature.
3
Recognize the solution: a divine Director
Maturidi argues that the only sufficient origin is a wise Director (mudir) who knows human needs and preservation, i.e., Allah. This Director appoints a guide.
The word 'mudir' refers to God, not the imam.
4
Understand the proof for the imam
God gives the appointed guide a clear proof (dalil) and demonstration (burhan) so people recognize him as the true imam. The imam's truthfulness is established because his message aligns with God's decree.
The proof distinguishes the true imam from any pretender.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم وإذ ثبت ذا لا بد من أصل يؤلّف بد من أصل يؤلّف بينهم ويَكفّهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء / فلزم طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. على أن الأحق في ذلك ـ إذ علم بحاجة كل ممن يُشاهد وضرورة كل من المعاين ـ أن لهم مديرًا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدَعُهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء - مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة ما به معاشهم وبقاؤهم دون أن يقيم لهم من يدلّهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له دليلاً وبرهانا يعلمون [به] خصوصه بالذي خصه من الإمامة لهم وأحوجهم إليه... فيما عليه أمرهم". فيكون في ذلك ما بينا : من صدق من ينتهي قوله إلى قول من دلّ عليه العالم" بأمر العالم أنه هو الذي جعله المفزع لهم والمعتمد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Their annihilation did not permit the creation of that by which their preservation [could be achieved]. And when this is established, there must necessarily be an origin that reconciles them and restrains them from the conflict and divergence which lead to destruction and annihilation. Therefore, it became necessary to seek an origin that unites them upon [something] to the extent that their capacity can reach it. Moreover, the most appropriate in this matter — since [we] know the need of every observed [being] and the necessity of every perceived [being] — is that they have a Director (mudīr) who is knowledgeable of their states and of what their preservation depends on, and that He [i.e., God] created them with needs, and does not leave them with their ignorance and overwhelming desires — despite their need to know that by which their livelihood and preservation [are achieved] — without appointing for them someone to guide them to that and make them aware of it. And it is necessary that He make for him [i.e., the guide] a proof (dalīl) and a demonstration (burhān) by which they know his specificity by which he was distinguished from them in imamate (imāma) and made them in need of him... in what concerns their affair. So in that is what we have clarified: the truthfulness of the one whose statement ends with the statement of the one [i.e., the guide] indicated by the Knower of the world (al-‘ālim) regarding the affair of the world — that He [i.e., God] made him the refuge and the relied-upon for them. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a divinely appointed imam (leader) for human beings. The author begins by stating that humanity's tendency towards conflict and annihilation makes it impossible for them to sustain themselves without a unifying origin. This origin must be a wise Director (mudīr) who knows their conditions and what ensures their survival. God, having created them with needs and given them ignorance and desires, would not leave them without guidance. Therefore, He must appoint a guide and provide him with a clear proof (dalīl) and demonstration (burhān) so that people recognize his special status as imam. The conclusion is that the truthfulness of this guide is established because his message aligns with what the all-knowing God has ordained, making him the ultimate refuge for humanity.

Key Terms
مدير (mudīr)
إمامة (imāma)
دليل (dalīl)
برهان (burhān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any capable human leader can guide people
Correction: The passage insists the leader must be divinely appointed and proven by a clear proof from God.

Checkpoint

B) To guide people out of ignorance and conflict through a clear proof
C) To enforce religious rituals
D) To collect taxes

Key Takeaway
For Imam Maturidi, human nature's inclination to conflict and ignorance necessitates a divinely appointed imam whose authority is verified by a clear proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Imam Maturidi argues that a divinely appointed leader is necessary for preservation. In your own life, where are you trying to preserve yourself through your own plans rather than submitting to the guidance He has sent? What would it mean for you to make that guide your refuge starting today?

Necessity of Accepting Prophetic Reports and Definition of Mutawātir
Maturidi argues for accepting prophetic reports and defines mutawātir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason necessitates accepting prophetic reports and how the concept of mutawātir establishes certainty in transmission.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports are the most truthful because they come with miracles.

Imam Maturidi continues his discussion on knowledge by moving from general acceptance of reports to the specific case of prophetic reports. He argues that since reason compels us to accept reliable reports, we must accept the reports of prophets, which are accompanied by clear signs proving their truthfulness. He then introduces the definition of mutawātir, a report transmitted so widely that it cannot be false, even if individual transmitters are fallible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow from general report acceptance to prophetic reports.
2
Identify the key argument for accepting prophetic reports
Look for the phrase 'lā khabar aẓharu ṣidqan' and understand why Maturidi considers prophetic reports the most truthful.
He connects truthfulness to the presence of miracles (āyāt).
3
Understand the problem of transmission
Note that reports reach us through fallible narrators. Maturidi says we must examine such reports.
4
Grasp the definition of mutawātir
Find the description of mutawātir: a report that is never found to be a lie, and when it reaches that limit, its truth appears and the infallibility of its transmitters is established.
This is the solution to the problem of fallible narrators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ قد لزم قبول الأخبار بضرورة العقل لزِمَ قبول أخبار الرسل، إذ لا خبر أظهَرَ صدقًا من خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم، إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب مما بينا من المعارف التي يصير منكر ذلك متعنتا بضرورة العقل أوضح صدقا من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم. فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يُقضى عليه بالتعنّت والمكابرة. ثم الأخبار التي تنتهي إلينا من الرسل تنتهي على ألسن من يُحتمل منهم الغلط والكذب، إذ ليس معهم دليل الصدق ولا برهان العصمة، فحق مثله النظر فيه. فإن كان مثله مما لا يُوجد كَذِبًا قط فهو الخبر الذي من انتهى إليه مثله لزمه حق شهود القول تمن اتضح البرهان على عصمته، وذلك وصف خبر المتواتر: من أن كلاً منهم - وإن لم يقم دليل على عصمته - فإن الخبر منهم إذا بلغ ذلك الحد ظهر صدقه، وثبتت عصمة مثله عن
Then, since acceptance of reports is necessitated by the necessity of reason, acceptance of the reports of the messengers is necessitated. For there is no report more clearly truthful than their report, with what they have of signs (āyāt) clarifying their truthfulness. For there is no report that the heart trusts, among the cognitions we have mentioned, that the denier of which becomes obstinate by necessity of reason, that is more clearly truthful than the reports of the messengers, blessings of Allah be upon them. Whoever denies that is most deserving of judgement upon him of obstinacy and stubborn denial. Then the reports that reach us from the messengers reach us through tongues of those who may err or lie, for they have with them no proof of truthfulness nor evidence of infallibility. So the right thing is to examine such a report. If it is of the kind that is never found to be a lie at all, then it is the report that whoever encounters its like is obliged by the right of witnessing the statement, once the proof of his infallibility is clear. And that is the description of the concurrent report (al-khabar al-mutawātir): that each one of them — even if no proof of his infallibility is established — when the report reaches that limit, its truth appears and the infallibility of its like is established from...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous acceptance of reports to argue for the necessity of accepting prophetic reports. Since reason necessitates accepting reports in general, and the reports of prophets come with clear miracles (āyāt) that demonstrate their truthfulness, they are the most truthful reports. Denying them is the height of obstinacy. Then the author discusses the nature of prophetic reports as transmitted through fallible individuals. He introduces the concept of mutawātir: a report that is so widely transmitted that it could not possibly be a lie. This report, even though each transmitter individually is not infallible, collectively achieves certainty. The passage cuts off mid-explanation.

Key Terms
أخبار الرسل (akhbār al-rusul)
الآيات (al-āyāt)
الخبر المتواتر (al-khabar al-mutawātir)
العصمة (al-ʿiṣma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidi declares all transmitters of prophetic reports as infallible individually.
Correction: Maturidi says that the mutawātir report itself proves the infallibility of those who transmit it collectively, not individually. Each transmitter may be fallible, but the collective transmission ensures certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why are the reports of the prophets the most clearly truthful?
A) Because prophets are known to be truthful
B) Because they are accompanied by miracles (āyāt)
C) Because they are transmitted by infallible people
D) Because they are consensus of the community

Key Takeaway
Reason necessitates accepting prophetic reports because they are accompanied by miracles, and the mutawātir transmission ensures their truth despite fallible narrators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard many claims about Islam, some from scholars, some from the internet. Maturidi says that prophetic reports are the most truthful because of the miracles. But have you personally examined the evidence for the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)? Do you accept it based on hearsay or have you sought the signs (āyāt) that confirm his truthfulness? What would it take for you to be convinced beyond doubt?
?
Maturidi argues that reports from fallible narrators need scrutiny. In your own life, whose reports do you accept without question? Are there teachings from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that you've heard second-hand but never verified? This lesson calls you to examine the basis of your beliefs. Are you willing to trace back the transmission of what you believe to its source?
?
The concept of mutawātir gives certainty. But do you live with certainty about the core beliefs of your faith? Or are there doubts lurking because you haven't confirmed the tawātur of the reports they rely on? What step will you take today to strengthen your conviction?

Necessity of Accepting Prophetic Reports
Maturidi argues for the superior truthfulness of prophetic reports due to evidentiary signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers acceptance of prophetic reports an even stronger rational necessity than ordinary reports.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports are the most truthful because they are confirmed by miracles.

Maturidi builds on the idea that accepting ordinary reports is rationally necessary. He now argues that the reports of prophets carry an even greater claim on our acceptance because they are accompanied by clear signs (āyāt) that prove their truth. This passage shows why rejecting prophetic guidance is not just a doctrinal error but a form of irrational obstinacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical flow from the general necessity of reports to the specific case of prophetic reports.
The passage is a single sustained argument.
2
Identify the premise
Find where Maturidi restates the principle that acceptance of reports is necessitated by reason. This is the foundation for his argument.
3
Identify the conclusion
Determine what Maturidi concludes about prophetic reports. Look for the phrase 'acceptance of the reports of the messengers becomes incumbent.'
4
Understand the supporting reason
Maturidi gives a reason why prophetic reports are even more binding: they are accompanied by signs (āyāt) that clarify their truthfulness. Consider what these signs are (miracles).
No other reports have such clear evidence.
5
Reflect on the implication
The passage ends by stating that deniers are guilty of obstinacy (mukābara). Think about why rejecting such clear evidence is irrational.
This ties back to the necessity of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ قد لزم قبول الأخبار بضرورة العقل لزِمَ قبول أخبار الرسل، إذ لا خبر أظهَرَ صدقًا من خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم، إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب مما بينا من المعارف التي يصير منكر ذلك متعنتا بضرورة العقل أوضح صدقا من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم . فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يُقضى عليه بالتعنّت والمكابرة.
Then, since acceptance of reports is necessitated by necessity of reason (ḍarūrat al-`aql), acceptance of the reports of the messengers becomes incumbent, for there is no report more clearly truthful than their report, due to what they have of signs (al-āyāt) that clarify their truthfulness. Indeed, there is no report that the heart can be assured of, among the knowledges we have explained, which makes the denier of that obstinate by necessity of reason, that is more clearly truthful than the reports of the messengers, prayers of God be upon them. Whoever denies that is more deserving of being judged as obstinate and stubborn.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous argument. It states that if acceptance of reports in general is necessitated by reason (as shown earlier), then acceptance of the reports of the prophets is even more binding. The reason is that no report is 'more clearly truthful' than the prophets' report because their reports are accompanied by clear signs (āyāt) that demonstrate their truthfulness. These signs are miracles. The passage asserts that there is no report that the heart can find reassuring, among the types of knowledge that make a denier obviously obstinate, that is more clearly truthful than the prophets' reports. Therefore, anyone who denies prophetic reports deserves to be judged as obstinate and stubborn (mukābara). The argument is that prophetic reports meet the highest standard of truthfulness due to the accompanying miracles, so rejecting them is unreasonable.

Key Terms
أخبار الرسل (akhbār al-rusul)
آيات (āyāt)
ضرورة العقل (ḍarūrat al-`aql)
تعنت (ta`annut)
مكابرة (mukābara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that prophetic reports are accepted simply because they come from prophets, without any external evidence.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that prophetic reports are accepted because they are accompanied by clear signs (miracles) that prove their truthfulness, making them the most clearly truthful reports.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that prophetic reports are the most truthful?
A) Because prophets are infallible
B) Because they are supported by evidentiary miracles (āyāt)
C) Because they are numerous
D) Because they are transmitted by many

Key Takeaway
Prophetic reports are the most binding because they are accompanied by evidentiary signs that prove their truthfulness, making denial irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because it demanded too much of you? In what areas of your life are you at risk of being obstinate (mut‘annit) like those who reject prophetic reports despite clear signs?
?
The proof of prophethood lies in the signs (āyāt). How many of these signs have you personally witnessed or studied? If they are so clear, why does your heart sometimes waver? What would it take for you to be fully assured?
?
Maturidi says that denying prophetic reports is not a rational stance but stubbornness (mukābara). Think of a specific teaching of the Prophet ﷺ that you struggle to fully accept. Are you willing to admit that your resistance might be due to your own stubbornness rather than a lack of evidence? What will you do about it today?

Knowledge of the Maker from the Made
Maturidi argues for inferring the Maker from the made using analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses analogical reasoning to argue for the existence of a Maker of the world, even when direct sensory evidence is absent.

Core Idea
The made points to the Maker, just as writing points to a writer.

Maturidi responds to an opponent who claims we cannot know creation ex nihilo because it is not observable. He counters that many forms of knowledge are not based on sense perception, such as rational judgments of possibility and impossibility, and the atomic theory of disintegration. Then he argues that just as writing implies a writer, the composition and order of the world imply a Maker. This passage establishes that inference (istidlal) is a valid method for knowing Allah's existence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice Maturidi's argument structure: he first responds to the opponent's claim, then provides analogies.
2
Identify the key analogies
The passage uses the analogy of writing and writer, disintegration and disintegrator, etc. Note how these analogies are drawn from everyday experience.
3
Understand the role of inference
Maturidi argues that knowledge can come from inference beyond sense perception. Reflect on how rational judgments (possible/impossible) are non-sensory.
4
Apply to the world
The world is composed and disintegrated, so it requires a composer and disintegrator. Consider why the world's composition is more wondrous.
5
Connect to the lesson objective
See how this argument supports the Islamic theological position that Allah's existence is rationally knowable through His creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال: لا يُعلم صُنع شيء لا من شيء فهو المقدر بالموجود حساً. والمعارف نفسها خارجة عن الحس، وكذلك القول بيجوز ولا يجوز، وكذلك ما يدعى من التفرق لا الهلاك لا نعرفه بالحس، ثم قال به، فمثله ما نحن فيه مع ما كان فيه عقل وسمع وبصر وروح وغير ذلك مما لا يدري ممّ صُنع، فذلك يمنعه القول بما قال. وأيضاً إنه إذا لم يكن أحد يخبرنا عن قدمه أو كان أزليا من جوهر العالم فلا وجه للعلم به إلا بالاستدلال. ثم لا نعلم كتابة بلا كاتب، ولا تفرقا إلا بمفرق، وكذلك الاجتماع، وكذلك السكون والحركة، فيلزم في جملة العالم ذلك؛ إذ هو مؤلف مفرق، بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع.
And whoever says: one does not know the making of a thing from nothing [i.e., from non-being?] - that is measured by what exists sensibly. But the knowledges themselves are outside sense perception, and likewise the statement of 'possible' and 'not possible', and likewise what is claimed of disintegration without annihilation - we do not know it by sense, yet he affirms it. So similarly is what we are in with regard to what contains intellect, hearing, sight, spirit, and other things whose making is not known, so that prevents him from saying what he said. And also, if no one informs us about its pre-eternity, or if it is eternal from the substance of the world, then there is no way to know it except by inference. Then we do not know writing without a writer, nor disintegration without a disintegrator, and likewise composition, and likewise stillness and motion. So this is necessary for the totality of the world; for it is composed and disintegrated, rather the wonder in the composition of the world is greater.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage responds to an opponent who claims we cannot know the making of something from nothing (ex nihilo) because it is not observable. Maturidi counters that many knowledges are not based on sense perception, such as the rational judgments of possibility and impossibility, and the atomic theory of disintegration without annihilation. So the opponent already accepts non-sensory knowledge. Furthermore, the world contains intellect, hearing, sight, spirit, etc., whose origins are unknown, but that does not disprove a maker. Then he argues that we can infer the maker from the made: just as writing implies a writer, and disintegration implies a disintegrator, so the composition and motion of the world imply a Maker. The world's composition is even more wondrous, so its Maker is more evident.

Key Terms
صنع
حس
استدلال
كاتب
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument here is a proof for creation ex nihilo.
Correction: Actually, the argument is an inference from design to a designer, not a direct proof of creation from nothing.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to argue for a Maker of the world?
A) Writing requires a writer
B) Eating requires food
C) Color requires a colored object
D) Remaining requires a body

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses analogical reasoning from observable design (like writing) to conclude that the world must have a Maker, showing that inference is a valid path to knowledge of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate composition of your own body—your intellect, your senses, your spirit—do you truly see these as pointing to a Maker, or have you become numb to the wonder? If you are numb, what will you do today to reawaken that awareness?
?
Maturidi argues that we know writing requires a writer by necessary inference. Yet in your daily life, do you live as if the world has a Maker, or do you act as if it is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized this inference?
?
The opponent in the passage denies what he cannot sense. Are there areas of your faith where you demand sensory proof before submitting? What would it cost you to accept Allah's signs through inference alone?

The Absence of Evidence for Other Gods and the Proof from Miracles
Maturidi argues that no other god has claimed divinity or sent prophets with miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the absence of any rival god claiming divinity or preventing miracles proves the oneness of Allah.

Core Idea
No rival ever claimed divinity or stopped a prophet's miracle.

Maturidi builds a powerful historical and empirical case for tawhid. He observes that no other being has ever claimed to be God or sent prophets with miracles, and that the very success of the prophets—despite fierce opposition—proves that no rival god exists to obstruct them. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's argument
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two main claims: (1) no other god has claimed divinity or sent messengers, and (2) the miracles of the prophets would have been stopped by any rival.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the premises: no historical claim from another god, no messengers from another god, and the logical inference that if a rival existed, they would have prevented the prophets' miracles.
3
Analyze the proof from miracles
Focus on the logical structure: if there were another god, they would have stopped the prophets to protect their own divinity. Yet the prophets succeeded, so no other god exists.
This is a form of argument from non-opposition.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the success of the prophets—despite many enemies—is only possible because the true God is the One who subdues all. Consider how this strengthens tawhid.
The passage ends with praise: 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لم يذكر عن غير الإله الذي يعرفه أهل التوحيد دعوى الإلهية والإشارة إلى أثر فعل منه يدل على ربوبيته، ولا وجد في شيء معنى أمكن إخراجه عن جملته، ولا بعث رسلا بالآيات التي تقهر العقول وتبهر لها، فثبت أن القول بذلك خيال ووسواس. وأيضا مجيء الرسل بالآيات التي يضطر من شهدها أنها فعل من لو كان معه شريك ليمنعهم عن إظهارها، إذ بذلك إبطال ربوبيتهم وألوهيتهم. فإذ لم يوجد ولا منعوا عن ذلك، مع كثرة المكابرين لهم والمعاندين ممن لو أحبوا وجود الأنصار لهم في إظهار آياتهم لوجدوا، فثبت أن ذلك إنما سلم للرسل؛ لما لم يكن الإله الحق والخالق للخلق غير الواحد القهار الذي قهر كل متعنت مكابر عن التمويه فضلا عن التحقيق ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after that, nothing has been mentioned about any other than the God known to the people of tawhid claiming divinity or pointing to a trace of an act from Him indicating His lordship; nor was there found in anything a meaning that could be extracted from its totality; nor did He send messengers with signs that overwhelm minds and dazzle them. So it is established that the statement (of polytheism) is fantasy and whisper (of Satan). Also, the coming of messengers with signs that force the one who witnesses them to acknowledge that they are the act of One who, if He had a partner, would prevent them from manifesting them, since that would nullify their lordship and divinity. Since that (partner) does not exist and did not prevent them, despite the abundance of those who opposed them and were obstinate — people who, if they had desired supporters to help them in manifesting their signs, would have found them — so it is established that this (success of the messengers) was only secured for the messengers because the true God and Creator of creation is none other than the One, the Subduer, who subdued every stubborn opposer from deception, let alone truth. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage continues the proof of tawhid by empirical and historical reasoning. The author first notes that no one besides the true God has ever claimed divinity or shown a trace of such action. Furthermore, no other being has sent messengers with overwhelming miracles. Therefore, polytheism is mere fantasy. Then he points out that the messengers came with undeniable miracles that would have provoked any rival god to stop them, especially since many enemies would have welcomed such intervention. Since no such interference occurred, it proves that no partner exists. The messengers were only allowed to succeed because the real God is the One who subdues all.

Key Terms
أهل التوحيد
ربوبية
آيات
قهار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument relies on the absence of evidence alone (argument from silence).
Correction: Maturidi does not rely on a bare absence of evidence. He uses the positive evidence of prophets' miracles and the logical necessity that a rival would have opposed them, which did not happen.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the absence of any rival god preventing the prophets' miracles prove tawhid?
A) Because rivals would be too weak to interfere
B) Because the prophets' enemies would have used any rival's help if it existed
C) Because miracles are impossible if multiple gods exist
D) Because the prophets were not powerful enough to face rivals

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the absence of any rival god claiming divinity or preventing miracles proves that Allah alone is the true God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has your heart ever secretly entertained that other forces—people, money, or fate—might have real control over your life? How does this passage's argument that no other ever claimed divinity or acted divinely confront that secret thought?
?
When you see opposition to your faith—whether from your own desires or from others—do you truly believe that Allah alone subdues all opposition? If that conviction were deeper, what would change about how you face today's challenges?
?
If no other god ever sent prophets or miracles, what does that imply about the sincerity of your worship? Are you giving your whole heart to the One who alone has the right to it, or are you divided? What step will you take today to eliminate that division?

Second Proof: Moses' Request Shows Vision is Possible
Maturidi's second proof from Moses' request for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Moses' request for vision as evidence that seeing Allah is possible in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Moses' request for vision was not rebuked, proving vision is possible.

Maturidi now presents a second proof drawn directly from the Quranic story of Prophet Moses. When Moses asked to see Allah, Allah did not rebuke him or declare the request impossible. Instead, He gave a conditional response implying that vision is possible in principle. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why it is a powerful proof for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once alongside the translation. Notice the logical flow: a claim, a conditional, a contrast with other prophets, and a final Quranic quote.
The passage is dense but follows a clear argument.
2
Identify the core argument
Maturidi argues that if seeing Allah were impossible, Moses' request would be ignorance of his Lord. But a prophet cannot be ignorant of such a matter, so the request itself shows that vision is not impossible.
3
Consider the contrast with other prophets
Notice that Allah rebuked Noah and Adam for their mistakes, but He did not rebuke Moses for this request. This silence implies approval.
If the request were wrong, Allah would have forbidden it as He did with others.
4
Analyze Allah's conditional response
Allah said, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' This conditional statement implies that vision is not absolutely impossible—it is linked to the mountain's stability, which is a created thing.
If vision were impossible, Allah would have simply said 'it is impossible,' not given a condition.
5
Conclude the proof
Maturidi concludes that the lack of rebuke and the conditional response together prove that seeing Allah is rationally possible, even if it does not occur in this world.
This proof is used by many kalam scholars to defend the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني قول موسى عليه السلام: رب أرني أنظر إليك الآية؛ ولو كان لا تجوز الرؤية لكان ذلك السؤال منه جهلاً بربه، ومن يجهله لا يحتمل أن يكون موضعاً لرسالته، أميناً على وحيه. وبعد، فإن الله تعالى لم ينهه ولا أيأسه، وبدون ذلك نهى نوحاً وعاتب آدم، وغيرهما من الرسل. وذلك لو كان لا يجوز يبلغ الكفر. ثم قال: فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني.
And the second (proof) is the saying of Moses, peace be upon him: "My Lord, show me (that) I may look at You" (the verse); and if vision were not permissible, that question would be ignorance on his part of his Lord, and one who is ignorant of Him cannot be a place for His message, trustworthy over His revelation. Moreover, Allah, the Exalted, did not forbid him nor despair him, while He forbade Noah and rebuked Adam and other messengers without that. And that, if it were not permissible, would reach disbelief. Then He said: "But if it becomes stable in its place, then you will see Me" (Quran 7:143).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of seeing God, based on the story of Moses requesting to see Allah (Quran 7:143). Maturidi argues that if seeing God were absolutely impossible, then Moses' request would be ignorance of his Lord. But Moses was a prophet sent by God, so he could not have been ignorant of such a fundamental matter. Moreover, God did not forbid Moses or make him despair; rather, He gave a conditional response: 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' This conditional statement implies that seeing God is possible in principle, because God did not say 'It is impossible to see Me' but instead linked the possibility to the mountain's stability. In contrast, God directly forbade Noah and rebuked Adam when they made mistakes, showing that He would have done the same if Moses' request were wrong. Thus, the fact that God did not reprimand Moses indicates that the request was not inherently wrong, and that seeing God is possible.

Key Terms
موسى
الرؤية
الإدراك
النهي
اليأس
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Moses' request was a sin or that he was rebuked for it.
Correction: The passage shows that Allah did not rebuke Moses and instead gave a conditional answer, indicating the request was not sinful and that vision is possible.

Checkpoint
What does the lack of rebuke for Moses indicate about the possibility of seeing God?
A) That Moses was correct
B) That God does not rebuke prophets
C) That the request was not inherently wrong
D) That Moses was not serious

Key Takeaway
Moses' request for vision was not rebuked, which implies that seeing Allah is possible in principle, a key argument for the beatific vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a deep spiritual desire—like longing to see Allah or feel His closeness—do you allow yourself to ask Him freely, or do you hold back fearing it might be inappropriate? What would change if you adopted Moses' boldness in your dua?
?
Think of a time you assumed something was impossible because others said so, without checking if it was actually forbidden. How does Moses' example challenge you to re-evaluate those assumptions in your own life?
?
If you knew that asking Allah for something—even something immense—could never be a sin, what would you start asking for today? What is stopping you from asking right now?

Refutation of the Objection Regarding Moses' Request
Maturidi refutes the objection that Moses asked for a sign, not to see God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that Moses asked for a sign rather than to see God is invalid according to Maturidi's reasoning.

Core Idea
Moses' request was to see God, not a sign; signs were already given.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a potential objection to his earlier proofs about the impossibility of seeing God in this world. The objector suggests that Moses was only asking for a sign, not the vision of God. Maturidi provides multiple arguments to refute this, showing that the request was indeed for direct vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the objection and the four reasons given in response.
2
Understand the first two refutations
The first reason points to God's explicit statement 'You will not see Me', not 'You will not see My sign'. The second reason explains that requesting signs after sufficient signs is obstinacy, characteristic of disbelievers.
Moses is not a disbeliever, so this interpretation fails.
3
Grasp the third and fourth arguments
The third reason uses the mountain's stability to show the request was for seeing God, not a sign. The fourth reason draws on Abraham's argument with his people: he rejected a Lord who sets (impermanent), not a Lord who can be seen.
This shows that seeing God is not inherently problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لعله سأل آية يعلم بها ربه. قيل: لا يحتمل ذا لوجوه. أحدها أنه قال: لن تراني وقد أراه الآية. وأيضا إن طلب الآيات يخرج مخرج التعنت، وقد أراه الآيات. وذلك تعنت الكفرة إنهم لا يزالون يطلبون الآيات وإن كانت الكفاية قد ثبتت، فمثله ذلك. وأيضا إنه قال: فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني؛ والآية التي يستقر معها الجبل دون الآية التي لا يستقر معها؛ ثبت أنه لم يرد بذلك الآية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا محاجة إبراهيم قومه في النجوم، وما ذكر بالأفول والغيبة؛ ولم يحاجهم بأن لا يحب ربا يرى، ولكن حاجهم بأن لا يحب ربا يأفل، إذ هو دليل عدم الدوام.
For it is said: Perhaps he asked for a sign by which he might know his Lord. It is said: This is not possible for reasons. One of them is that He said: "You will not see Me" and He had shown him the sign. Also, requesting signs comes out as obstinacy, and He had shown him signs. And that is the obstinacy of the disbelievers: they do not cease requesting signs even though sufficiency has been established, so the like of that is similar. Also, He said: "But if it becomes stable in its place, then you will see Me"; and the sign with which the mountain becomes stable is different from the sign with which it does not become stable; it is established that He did not intend thereby the sign. And there is no power except by Allah. Also, the argument of Abraham with his people concerning the stars, and what he mentioned of setting and concealment; and he did not argue with them by (saying) "I do not love a Lord who is seen," but he argued with them by (saying) "I do not love a Lord who sets," for that is evidence of non-continuance.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer to the previously given proofs. The objector suggests that perhaps Moses was not asking to see God Himself, but rather asking for a sign (aya) by which he could know his Lord. The response gives multiple reasons why this interpretation is invalid. First, God explicitly said 'You will not see Me' (lan tarani), not 'You will not see My sign.' Second, requesting signs when sufficient signs have already been given is obstinacy ('anat), characteristic of disbelievers, and Moses was not obstinate. Third, God conditioned the vision on the stability of the mountain: 'If it becomes stable, then you will see Me.' The sign that stabilizes the mountain is different from the sign that destabilizes it; if Moses wanted a sign, the mountain's stability itself could be a sign, but God did not say that. So the intended object of seeing is God, not a sign. Fourth, the argument of Abraham against his people regarding the stars: Abraham did not argue that he does not love a Lord who can be seen; he argued that he does not love a Lord who sets (i.e., disappears). This shows that the issue of seeing God is not inherently problematic; rather, the problem for Abraham was impermanence (afool). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الآية
التعنت
إبراهيم
الأفول
الغيبة
الدوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses asked Allah for a sign, not to see Him.
Correction: The Qur'anic text and Maturidi's arguments show that Moses requested direct vision of Allah, not a sign.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is the objection that Moses asked for a sign invalid?
A) Because God said 'You will not see Me' not 'You will not see My sign'
B) Because Moses already saw signs
C) Because signs are not appropriate for prophets
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Moses asked to see Allah, not for a sign; the refutation demonstrates that the Qur'anic text and rational arguments exclude the sign interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever find yourself demanding a sign from Allah before you truly submit to His command? What would it take for you to trust His promise like Moses, without such demands?
?
Abraham argued about impermanence, not visibility. What does your heart secretly desire: a God you can see and control, or a God who is eternal and beyond your senses? How does this lesson reveal the object of your worship?
?
Have you ever acted with ta'annot—demanding more evidence when what Allah has given is already sufficient? Think of a specific area in your life where you have not accepted His signs. Will you repent and submit today?

Hadith of Seeing Allah Like the Moon (Argument 6)
Maturidi cites a hadith and an incident to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a hadith about seeing Allah like the moon and the Prophet's gentle response to support the doctrine of beatific vision.

Core Idea
The Prophet's gentle answer to the question about seeing Allah confirms that vision is not impossible.

In this argument, Maturidi draws on a well-known hadith about seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection like seeing the moon. Then he recounts an incident where the Prophet was asked directly if he had seen his Lord. Instead of rebuking the questioner, the Prophet gave a gentle reply, indicating that such a question is not forbidden and that the vision is plausible.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage once to get the narrative. Then read the translation to understand the story.
Notice that the hadith about seeing Allah like the moon is followed by a specific incident.
2
Identify the key textual evidence
Look for the hadith quote, the incident of the question, and the Quranic verse (Al-Ma'idah 101) that warns against asking about certain things.
The Quranic verse is used to contrast with the Prophet's gentle response.
3
Understand Maturidi's logical inference
Think about why Maturidi argues that the lack of rebuke implies permissibility and possibility. Consider what the Prophet could have done if the question were forbidden.
This inference is central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - وأيضا ما جاء عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في غير خبر، أنه قال: «سترون ربكم يوم القيامة كما ترون القمر لا تُضامون في رؤيته» . وسئل : هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال: «بقلبي» . قيل : فلم ينكر على السائل السؤال، وقد علم السائل أن رؤية القلب هي العلم وأنه قد علمه، وأنه لم يسأل عن ذلك. وقد حذر الله عز وجل المؤمنين عن السؤال عن أشياء قد كُفّوا عنها ، بقوله : يا أيها الذين آمنوا لا تسألوا عن أشياء ، فكيف يُحتمل أن يكون السؤال عن مثله لحي وذلك كفر في الحقيقة عند قوم . ثم لا ينهاهم عن ذلك، ولا يوبّخهم في ذلك، بل يلين القول في ذلك، ويُرِي أن ذلك ليس ببعيد . والله الموفق .
6 - Also what came from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in other than one report, that he said: 'You will see your Lord on the Day of Resurrection as you see the moon; you will not be harmed in seeing Him.' And he was asked: Did you see your Lord? He said: 'With my heart.' It was said: Then he did not rebuke the questioner for the question, and the questioner knew that the vision of the heart is knowledge and that he knew it, and that he had not asked about that. And Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, warned the believers against asking about things they were prohibited from, by His saying: 'O you who believe, do not ask about things' (Al-Ma'idah 101). So how could it be acceptable that the question about something like that be [considered] disbelief in reality according to some people? Then he did not forbid them from that, nor did he rebuke them for that, but he softened speech in that, and showed that it is not far off. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument 6) from prophetic hadith. Maturidi cites the famous hadith that believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection like seeing the moon, with no harm. Then he recounts the incident where the Prophet was asked if he had seen his Lord, and he replied 'With my heart'. Maturidi notes that the Prophet did not rebuke the questioner, even though the questioner knew that seeing with the heart means knowledge (ma'rifah), and that the question was about actual sight. Furthermore, Allah had warned against asking about certain things (Quran 5:101). If asking about seeing Allah were tantamount to disbelief, the Prophet would have rebuked him strongly. Instead, he answered gently and indicated it is not far-fetched. This demonstrates that the question is permissible and that the vision of Allah (in the afterlife) is a real possibility.

Key Terms
رؤية الله
روية القمر
بقلبي
لا تسألوا عن أشياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Asking about seeing Allah is forbidden and implies disbelief.
Correction: The Prophet did not rebuke the questioner, and his gentle response indicates the question is permissible and the vision is possible.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the Prophet's response to the question 'Did you see your Lord?' supports the doctrine of vision?
A) Because the Prophet confirmed he saw Him with his eyes.
B) Because the Prophet did not rebuke the questioner and gave a gentle reply.
C) Because the questioner was a disbeliever and was corrected.
D) Because the Prophet remained silent.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's lack of rebuke and gentle response to the question about seeing Allah demonstrates that the vision is not impossible and the question is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the promise of seeing Allah like the moon, does your heart yearn for that moment? Or are you indifferent? What does your level of desire say about your faith?
?
The Prophet answered gently even when asked about something that some consider disbelief. How do you respond when someone questions your beliefs — do you imitate his gentleness or do you become harsh?
?
If you truly believed you will see your Lord on the Day of Resurrection, how would that change the way you live today? What would you start doing or stop doing?

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Moses' Request
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's interpretation that Moses' request shows impossibility of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's alternative explanations for Moses' request to see God, refuting the claim that it proves vision is impossible.

Core Idea
Moses' request was about rank and teaching, not impossibility.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses a key theological controversy: the meaning of Moses' request to see God. Al-Ka'bi argued that the request itself shows that seeing God is impossible because Moses would not ask for the impossible. Maturidi refutes this by presenting alternative purposes for the request—either to elevate Moses' station or to teach that vision is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi refutes an opponent's view.
The Arabic is from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid.
2
Identify the refuted position
Al-Ka'bi claimed that Moses' request was based on knowledge of impossibility. Maturidi rejects this as corrupt.
The refutation is brief but decisive.
3
Discover alternative purposes
Maturidi offers two possibilities: elevation of rank or teaching permissibility. Consider how both negate the claim of impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر في أمر موسى عليه السلام أن ذلك [كان] على علم الإحاطة بالآيات. وقد بينا فساد ذلك. وما ذلك العلم بالذي يسأل؛ وهو رسول بعث إلى ما به نجاة الخلق؛ وذلك لا يكون بغير الممتحن؛ إذ هو تبليغ الرسالة والدعاء إلى العبادة، وهي محنة. بل سأل الرؤية ليجل به قدره وليعرف عظيم محله عند الله، أو أن يكون الله أمره به ليعلم الخلق جواز ذلك. وبالله التوفيق.
Then he mentioned concerning the matter of Moses, peace be upon him, that that [request] was upon knowledge encompassing the signs. And we have already shown the corruption of that. And what is that knowledge to the one who asks? He is a messenger sent to that by which the creation is saved; and that cannot be without the tester, for it is conveying the message and calling to worship, which is a trial. Rather, he asked for vision to elevate his rank and to know his great station with Allah, or that Allah commanded him to do so to teach the creation the permissibility of that. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage deals with al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the story of Moses (Quran 7:143). Al-Ka'bi claimed that Moses' request to see God was based on knowledge of the signs (i.e., he knew it was impossible) and that the verse proves impossibility. Maturidi refutes this, saying that even if Moses had such knowledge, it does not mean he would not ask. He points out that Moses was a messenger sent to save people through conveyance of the message, which necessarily involves trial. Therefore, his request for vision was either to elevate his own rank and demonstrate his high station before God, or it was commanded by God in order to teach the people that vision of God is permissible. Thus, the verse does not prove impossibility.

Key Terms
موسى
الرؤية
الآيات
المحن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' request to see God proves that seeing God is impossible.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the request serves other purposes, such as elevating his rank or teaching permissibility, so it does not prove impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one possible purpose of Moses asking to see God?
A) He was ignorant of God's invisibility.
B) To teach people that seeing God is permissible.
C) To show that he was superior to other prophets.
D) He was testing God's power.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request to see God does not prove impossibility; it was for elevating his rank or teaching permissibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever ask Allah for something that seems impossible to your mind? Moses' request was not based on despair but on hope for elevation. What does your own prayer life reveal about your trust in Allah's ability to grant beyond what seems possible?
?
The passage teaches that even a prophet's request had a deeper purpose: to elevate his rank. Are your requests to Allah focused on elevating your rank with Him, or are they primarily about worldly comfort? What change can you make today to align your prayers more with this prophetic model?

Refutation of the Mountain Argument
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's mountain argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that the mountain saw God by clarifying that the event was a sign of divine power, not a vision.

Core Idea
The mountain's crumbling was a sign, not a vision of God.

In this passage, Maturidi responds to an objection from al-Ka'bi, who argued that since the mountain 'saw' God (based on the Quranic verse), but mountains are not rational, it shows that only bodies can be seen. Maturidi counters by explaining that the mountain did not see; rather, it was a sign shown to Moses. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the objection from al-Ka'bi and Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the core distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between seeing God and seeing a sign. The mountain crumbled as a sign, not because it saw God.
The verse says 'manifested to the mountain' and it crumbled, but that does not imply the mountain saw.
3
Consider the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for the debate on ru'yat Allah (seeing God). It undermines al-Ka'bi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم استدل بأنه لم ير من يعقل، إنما أرى الجبل، والجبل لا يعقل ليعلمه وليراه. فيقال له: ولو كانت آية فالجبل لا يراها ولا يعقل. وإذا كان كذلك فالآية اذا صار اندكاك الجبل لا أن أراه الآية ليندك بها. وفي هذا أنه قد أرى موسى الآية و هو الجبل؛ والله تعالى يقول: لن تراني، وحَمَلَه على الآية، وقد رآها. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم سأل نفسه عن معنى توبته، ولا يسأل عنه، فزعم أنه لوجهين. أحدهما أنه علم بما أراه من الأدلة أن ذلك صغيرة [ف]تاب عنها. والثاني على العادة في الخلق من تجديدها عند الأهوال بلا حدوث ذنب. {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله :} ولو كان صغيرة لكان أولى من تركه إلى أن يعلم بالأدلة. وهي في الإعلام في غير حال الإغماء أحق منها في حال الإغماء. والثاني يصح ذلك عند معاينته الهول لا عند سكونه و إبداله بالأمن والإفاقة؛ وذلك وقت معاينته عصاه يهتز فولى مديرًا أحق. والله الموفق .
Then he argued that a rational being was not seen, but only the mountain was made to see, and the mountain does not understand to know it and see it. So it is said to him: Even if it were a sign, the mountain does not see it nor understand. And if so, then the sign is when the mountain crumbles, not that I show it the sign so that it crumbles because of it. And in this, he showed Moses the sign and it is the mountain; and Allah the Exalted says: 'You will not see Me,' and he interpreted it as the sign, and he had seen it. And there is no power except by Allah. Then he asked himself about the meaning of his [Moses'] repentance, though he is not to be questioned about it, and he claimed that it is for two reasons. One: that he knew from the evidences shown to him that that was a minor sin, so he repented from it. The second: based on the custom among people of renewing repentance at times of terror without occurrence of sin. {Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said:} Even if it were a minor sin, it would be more appropriate to leave it than to be taught by evidences. And it [the teaching] is more fitting when he is not in a state of unconsciousness. The second [reason] is valid at the time of witnessing terror, not at the time of its cessation and replacement with security and recovery; and that is at the time of his seeing his staff quivering, so he turned away fleeing, and that is more appropriate. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of al-Ka'bi. The objector argued that the mountain saw God (based on the verse 'when his Lord manifested to the mountain'), and since the mountain is not rational, it shows that only bodies can be seen. The author responds that the verse says the mountain was made into dust, not that it saw God. The manifestation was a sign, and the mountain's crumbling was the sign, not a vision. Then the author addresses al-Ka'bi's speculation about Moses' repentance. Al-Ka'bi claimed that Moses repented because he committed a minor sin by asking to see God, and that this proves it is impossible. The author counters by saying that Moses is not answerable for his action, but al-Ka'bi posited two reasons: either Moses knew from evidence that it was a minor sin, or it is the custom of people to renew repentance at times of awe without sin. Then Imam Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) comments that even if it were a minor sin, it would be better to leave it rather than learn its sinfulness through evidence, and the evidence is more appropriate in a state of consciousness. The second reason (renewal of repentance) is more fitting at the moment of terror, like when Moses saw his staff moving and fled. So al-Maturidi concludes that the repentance was due to awe, not sin.

Key Terms
اندكاك
آية
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mountain literally saw God.
Correction: The mountain's crumbling was a sign of God's power, not an act of seeing.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the mountain in response to al-Ka'bi's argument?
A) The mountain saw God.
B) The mountain crumbled as a sign.
C) The mountain is rational.
D) The mountain will see God in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The mountain's crumbling was a sign of divine power, not a vision of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted that a sign from Allah must fit your own expectations, like al-Ka'bi insisted the mountain must have seen God? What if the crumbling of something you cherished was actually a sign calling you back to Him—would you recognize it?
?
Moses was told 'You will not see Me,' yet he submitted. Is there an area of your life where you are demanding to 'see' before you believe, instead of trusting Allah's wisdom? Will you let go of that demand today?
?
When you face difficulty, do you see it as a meaningless catastrophe or as a possible sign from Allah? What would need to change in your heart for you to see the mountain's crumbling as a sign of His power rather than as proof of His absence?

The Meaning of Moses' Repentance
Maturidi discusses the meaning of Moses' repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's analysis of Moses' repentance and why it was not for a sin but due to awe.

Core Idea
Moses' repentance was from awe, not from sin.

After refuting the mountain argument, Maturidi addresses al-Ka'bi's speculation about Moses' repentance. Al-Ka'bi claimed Moses repented because he committed a minor sin by asking to see God. Maturidi counters with his own analysis, supported by Imam al-Maturidi's commentary, showing that repentance can occur without sin, especially in moments of overwhelming awe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on repentance
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Note the two reasons given by al-Ka'bi.
2
Understand al-Maturidi's critique
Al-Maturidi critiques the first reason and supports the second under specific conditions.
He argues that if it were a sin, it would be better to leave it than learn from evidence.
3
Reflect on the reason
Why does Maturidi conclude that repentance was due to awe and not sin? Consider the example of the staff.

D) He disobeyed God's command.

Key Takeaway
Moses' repentance was an expression of awe and humility before God's power, not a repentance from sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt overwhelmed by God's majesty—perhaps through a natural phenomenon, a life event, or a moment of clarity? Did you respond with repentance, even if you hadn't committed a conscious sin? If not, what held you back?
?
Are you quick to assume that repentance is only for sins, missing opportunities to humble yourself before Allah out of sheer awe? Identify one 'terror' or sign in your current life—something that reminds you of His power—and commit to turning to Him in repentance tomorrow, not because you sinned, but because He is worthy of that response.
?
Moses saw his staff turn into a snake and fled; that moment of terror drove him to repentance. What similar 'snakes'—unexpected events or realizations—are you avoiding or explaining away instead of letting them move you to awe-filled repentance?

The Renewal of Faith and Musa's Acceptance of 'Lan Tarani'
Maturidi's analogy of faith renewal for Musa's acceptance of 'lan tarani'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of renewing faith to explain Musa's acceptance of 'lan tarani'.

Core Idea
Musa's acceptance of 'lan tarani' is a model of faith renewal.

Maturidi presents a subtle interpretation of Musa's response to Allah's command 'You will not see Me.' Rather than indicating doubt or rejection, Musa's acceptance mirrors the way believers renew their faith with each new divine command. This passage clarifies how faith can be deepened without being abandoned.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage in Arabic once, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
The Arabic is one continuous sentence with a complex structure.
2
Identify Maturidi's analogy
Find the point where Maturidi compares Musa's action to believers renewing faith with each new verse or obligation. Understand the parallel.
3
Reflect on the implications for faith
Consider how this analogy shows that faith is not static but renewed with each divine command. Think about how this applies to your own faith journey.
This connects to the broader theme of tajdid al-iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنه يحتمل أن يكون إذ قال له لن تراني، وكان عنده جواز الرؤية في الشاهد واحتمال وسعه ذلك بما وعد الله له في الآخرة، رجع عما كان عنده وآمن بالذي قال: لن تراني، وإن كان في أصل إيمانه داخلاً، على نحو إحداث المؤمنين الإيمان بكل آية تنزل وبكل فريضة تتجدد، وإن كانوا في الجملة مؤمنين بالكل. والله الموفق.
But it is possible that when He said to him 'You will not see Me', and he held the permissibility of vision in the witnessed world and the possibility of its capacity due to what Allah promised him in the afterlife, he returned from what he held and believed in what He said: 'You will not see Me', even though he was included in the foundation of his faith, in the manner of the believers' renewal of faith with every verse that is revealed and every obligation that is renewed, even though they are in totality believers in all. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility that when Allah told Musa 'you will not see Me', Musa, who had previously considered the possibility of ru'ya in this world based on analogy with sensory sight and the promise of vision in the afterlife, returned from his previous position and believed in what Allah said: 'you will not see Me', even though he was already a believer in principle. This is similar to how believers renew their faith with every revealed verse and every new obligation, even though they are already believers in all of it. Maturidi ends with 'wa Allah al-muwaffiq'.

Key Terms
تجديد الإيمان (tajdid al-iman)
لن تراني (lan tarani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa doubted or rejected his faith after hearing 'lan tarani'.
Correction: Maturidi argues that Musa's acceptance of 'lan tarani' was not a rejection of faith but a renewal, similar to how believers affirm faith with each new divine command.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that faith is renewed with each divine command; Musa's acceptance of 'lan tarani' exemplifies this active renewal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are there any areas where you cling to an earlier opinion despite knowing that Allah has said otherwise? What will it take for you to make the same 'ruju' as Musa did when he heard 'lan tarani'?

Prophetic Proof Against Anthropomorphism
Maturidi argues that messengers' use of divine names proves they are not anthropomorphic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the prophets' mission and the verse 'Nothing is like Him' to prove that divine names do not imply anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Prophets could not have taught resemblance; they came to uphold tawhid.

Maturidi now provides a powerful proof from the very function of prophethood. If the divine names used by prophets implied any similarity to creation, the prophets themselves would be undermining the core message of tawhid. Since they all called to the oneness of Allah, the names must transcend creatureliness. This passage ties together revelation, rational consistency, and the pivotal verse 'Nothing is like Him.'

Guided Path
1
Understand the prophetic proof
Read the passage and note that Maturidi begins by stating that the coming of messengers with divine names is the proof. Consider why the prophets' authority is a strong foundation for this argument.
2
Examine the negative conditional
Focus on the conditional: if the names implied tashbih, the prophets would be undermining tawhid. But they all called to tawhid, so such a contradiction is impossible. This shows that the names must be free from likeness.
The logic is: if names imply tashbih, then prophets contradict their own message; but they don't, so names don't imply tashbih.
3
See how the Qur'anic verse resolves
Maturidi adds that the names are permissible because they allow Allah to transcend the ordinary meaning, and the verse 'Nothing is like Him' negates all thingness from Him. This completes the proof.
The verse is a decisive scriptural support for the rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على ما قلنا مجيئ الرسل والكتب السماوية بها، ولو كان في التسمية بما جاءت به الرسل تشبيه لكانوا سبب نقض التوحيد، وهم جميعا دعوا إلى عبادة الواحد وإلى معرفة وحدانية الباري؛ لم يجز أن يكون ذلك مما يحقق العدد ويثبت الموافقة للخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ولكن لما احتملت تلك الأسماء خروج المسمى بها على المعروفين من المسمين بها جاز مجيئهم بها مع قوله : ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى، ١١/٤٢] ، ليُنفى به شيئية الأشياء من الأركان البسيطة وهي الأعراض والصفات، والأعيان المركبة وهي الأجسام. وبالله المعونة.
Then the proof for what we have said is the coming of the messengers and the heavenly books with them [the names]. And if there were in the naming with what the messengers brought any likeness (tashbīh), they would be the cause of undermining tawhid, while they all called to the worship of the One and to knowledge of the oneness of the Creator; it would not be permissible that this should confirm multiplicity and establish similarity to creation. There is no power except with Allah. But because those names admit the possibility that the named [i.e., Allah] transcends the known beings thus named, it was permissible for them [the messengers] to come with them together with His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11), in order to negate by it the 'thingness' (shay'iyya) of things, namely the simple elements which are accidents and attributes, and the composite entities which are bodies. And with Allah is assistance.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides a proof from revelation that divine names do not imply anthropomorphism. Maturidi argues that if the names used by the prophets implied tashbīh (likeness), then the prophets would be undermining tawhid, whereas they all called to the worship of the One. Therefore, the names must not imply similarity. The reason the names are permissible is that they allow the named (Allah) to transcend the ordinary beings that bear those names. This is coupled with the verse 'Nothing is like Him' (42:11), which negates the 'thingness' (shay'iyya) of all created things—both simple entities (accidents and attributes) and composite entities (bodies)—from applying to Allah.

Key Terms
الرسل
التشبيه
التوحيد
ليس كمثله شيء
الشيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that using human-like names for God in revelation proves He resembles creatures.
Correction: Maturidi shows this would contradict the prophets' mission, as they all called to tawhid. The names are used with the understanding that Allah transcends the ordinary meaning, and the verse 'Nothing is like Him' negates any similarity.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use the verse 'There is nothing like Him' to negate regarding Allah?
A) His existence.
B) The 'thingness' of simple and composite entities from Him.
C) The names themselves.
D) The messengers' reliability.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the very mission of the prophets proves divine names are free from any resemblance to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the prophets could not have used any name that implies resemblance, then every time you recite the names of Allah, you are declaring His absolute uniqueness. Yet, do you ever catch your mind forming an image or likeness of Allah when you hear His names? What name provokes this in you, and what will you do today to replace that image with the truth that 'there is nothing like Him'?
?
Maturidi argues that the very mission of the prophets would be invalid if the names contained tashbih. How does this challenge the way you think about your own prayer or supplication? When you call upon Allah as 'All-Hearing' or 'All-Seeing', do you truly believe He hears and sees without any resemblance to human hearing and sight? If not, what mistaken belief must you repent from right now?
?
The verse 'There is nothing like Him' negates even the 'thingness' of accidents and bodies. Is there any attribute, habit, or expectation you've been attaching to Allah without realizing it—something you treat as a 'thing' He possesses? Write down one such assumption and replace it with a tanzih-based affirmation from this lesson.

The Purpose of Creation: The Tested Humans as the Goal
An argument that creation is purposeful, with humans as the intended goal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the world was created with purposes, and specifically the view that tested humans are the central goal of creation.

Core Idea
The world was created for the tested humans; all other things serve them.

This passage from Imam Maturidi presents a theological argument about the purpose of creation. It begins with the rational premise that a wise agent acts for a purpose, then introduces the view that the world was created for the tested humans—those who are accountable. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for understanding God's wisdom and the role of humanity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of purpose
Read the opening lines where the author states that creation must have purposes, otherwise the Creator would not be wise.
This is a rational argument used by the group.
2
Identify the tested humans
Note that the world is said to be created for the tested humans, in whom wisdom and folly appear.
3
Examine the purpose of other creatures
See that other creatures are created for the benefit of humans, for testing, and as signs.
4
Reflect on the purpose of humans themselves
Humans are created for worship and to strive for praise and blame.
5
Consider the transcendence of God
The author notes that God is above needing such purposes; humans are needy by nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و - وقال قوم: خلق العالم لعلل يكون منها وفيها وما بعدها. وذلك هو المعقول من الحكماء أنه لمقاصد تعقب الصنيع؛ وكذا كل فاعل لا يعلم عواقب فعله أنه لما ذا يفعله فهو غير حكيم. ثم اختلف في المعنى الذي له خلق فمنهم من يقول: خلق جل العالم للمُمْتَحَنين فيه؛ إذ ظهور الحكمة فيهم، وكذلك فيهم يظهر العلو والسلطان والجلال تظهر الحكمة والسفه فهم المقصودون من الخلق، وغيرهم من الخلائق خلقوا لهم لمنافع لهم، وللامتحان بها، وللدلالة، وسُخّروا لهم. والممتحنون خُلقوا للعبادة، أو لأنفسهم، ليسعوا لعواقب يُحمدون عليها ويذمون إليهم يقع ذلك. وضرورة جلّ خالقهم عن الوجهين؛ إذ هم الذين خلقوا محتاجين، رُكّب فيهم ما عرفوا به حوائجهم وما يقومون في قضائها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And a group said: He created the world for causes (ʿilal) that come from it, in it, and after it. And that is what is understood from the wise: that it is for purposes that follow the act; and likewise every agent who does not know the consequences of his act—what he does it for—is not wise. Then they differed concerning the meaning for which He created. Among them are those who say: He created—glorified be Him—the world for the tested ones (al-mumtaḥanīn) in it; for the manifestation of wisdom in them, and likewise in them appear highness, authority, majesty, wisdom, and folly. So they are the intended ones in creation, and other creatures were created for them: for their benefits, for testing through them, for indication, and they are subjected to them. And the tested ones were created for worship, or for themselves, to strive for outcomes for which they are praised or blamed, and that comes to them. And necessity exalts their Creator from both aspects; for they were created needy, and in them were compounded what they know of their needs and what they undertake in fulfilling them. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from a group that the world was created for purposes (ʿilal), defined as causes that come from, in, and after creation. This is the rational understanding from philosophers (ḥukamā'): every wise agent acts with a known purpose, otherwise he would not be wise. The group then disagrees on the specific purpose. One opinion states that God created the world primarily for the tested humans (al-mumtaḥanīn), i.e., humans who are tested through obligations. In them, wisdom, authority, majesty, and even foolishness appear, so they are the intended ones (al-maqṣūdūn). All other creatures (animals, heavens, etc.) are created for the sake of humans: for their benefits, as tests, as signs, and they are subjugated to humans. Humans themselves are created for worship (ʿibāda) or for their own sake, to strive for consequences of praise or blame. The author notes that the Creator is far above both of these aspects (i.e., being needy or needing worship in a way that benefits Him), since humans were created needy and given mechanisms to know their needs and fulfill them. The passage ends with the formula of reliance on God.

Key Terms
لعلل
مقاصد
ممتحنون
المقصودون من الخلق
العبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God created for His own benefit or out of need.
Correction: The passage clarifies that purposes are for creatures, not for God. God is above need, and humans are created needy.

Checkpoint
According to the group mentioned in the passage, what is the primary purpose of creation?
A) To demonstrate God's power.
B) For the tested humans, in whom wisdom and folly appear, and other creatures serve them.
C) For God's own pleasure.
D) For no reason at all.

Key Takeaway
The world was created for the tested humans, who are the intended ones, and all other creatures serve them, while humans themselves are created for worship and moral striving.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you are the intended goal of creation—not because you deserve it, but because Allah chose you for a test? How would your life change if you lived each day conscious that every creature and event exists for your test?
?
The passage says other creatures were created for your benefit and as signs. When was the last time you paused to reflect on a single created thing—like a tree or your own body—as a sign from Allah, not just a resource? What one change can you make today to see the world as a sign?
?
If your ultimate purpose is worship and striving for outcomes you will be held accountable for, what is one activity you engage in that contradicts this purpose? What could you replace it with starting tomorrow?
