The Rational Basis of Divine Commands
Maturidi explains the rational foundation for divine commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's commands and prohibitions are grounded in human rational nature and the intellectual obligation of gratitude.

Core Idea
Divine commands are rooted in human reason and the duty of gratitude.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi builds on al-Husayn's earlier points to explain why God issues commands and prohibitions. He argues that humans are created with the capacity to know benefit and harm, and to reason from the seen to the unseen. Therefore, it would be unjust for God to leave them without knowledge of guidance. Moreover, since God has bestowed countless blessings on humans, gratitude (shukr) is a rational obligation. This sets the stage for the role of promises and threats in encouraging obedience and deterring disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it starts with 'Then the statement concerning command and prohibition...' and connects to what al-Husayn said.
The passage ends abruptly due to OCR, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the key premises
The passage presents several premises: humans are created subjugated through discipline, they know benefit/harm, they reason from proof to absent. Identify each premise and see how it leads to the conclusion that knowledge must be present and gratitude is necessary.
3
Understand the logical necessity of gratitude
The passage argues that gratitude for blessings is rationally necessary (lazim fi al-'aql). Therefore God required it from humans. Consider why this makes commands and prohibitions not arbitrary but based on human nature.
This connects to the broader Islamic theological principle of the intrinsic goodness and badness of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب، مع ما تقدم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين: إن الله خلق خلقا مذللا بالتأديب، عارفا بالنفع والضر، مستدلا بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب، لم يجز أن لا يعرض المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم مع ما كان لمن خلقه نعم عليه في الخلقة، وشكر النعمة لازم في العقل، فاستأداه. ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب بتعظيمه، والترهيب عن
Then the statement concerning command and prohibition, and encouragement and deterrence, along with what has preceded, sufficient from that which al-Husayn mentioned: that God created a creation subjugated through discipline, knowing benefit and harm, deducing by what he witnessed of the proof from what is absent, it is not permissible that knowledge be absent [from him] nor that ignorance befall him, for then it would involve permitting lying and every blameworthy thing, along with what He created for him of blessings upon him in creation, and gratitude for blessing is necessary in the intellect, so He required it of him. Then [there is] promise and threat for encouragement through magnifying Him, and deterrence from...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of divine commands and prohibitions, building on al-Husayn's earlier points. It states that God created humans subjugated through discipline (ta'dib), capable of knowing benefit and harm, and able to reason from witnessed evidence (hujja) to the unseen. Consequently, it is not permissible that knowledge be absent from them or that ignorance befall them, as that would permit lying and all blameworthy deeds. Moreover, God has bestowed blessings upon them in their very creation, and gratitude (shukr) for these blessings is rationally incumbent (lazim fi al-'aql). Therefore, God required gratitude from them. Then the passage mentions promise and threat (wa'd wa wa'id) as a means of encouragement by magnifying God, and deterrence from (the text cuts off, likely from evil).

ترغيب وترهيب (encouragement and deterrence)
تأديب (discipline)
حجة (proof)
شكر النعمة (gratitude for blessing)
وعد ووعيد (promise and threat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's commands and prohibitions are arbitrary and have no rational basis.
Correction: The passage shows that commands and prohibitions are grounded in human rational nature (the ability to know benefit/harm and reason from proof) and the intellectual obligation of gratitude for blessings.

Checkpoint

A) Worship through ritual
B) Gratitude (shukr)
C) Constant prayer
D) Asceticism

Key Takeaway
God's commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary but are based on human rational nature and the necessary duty of gratitude for His blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says ignorance would permit lying and blameworthy things. Are there areas in your life where you deliberately avoid knowledge to justify wrongdoing? Will you seek the truth today, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
Since gratitude is necessary in the intellect, what does your lack of gratitude say about the state of your reason? How can you train your mind to see every blessing—even hardships—as a gift from God?

4 min
The Necessity of Command and Prohibition
Maturidi establishes the necessity of command and prohibition based on human nature and rational gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that divine command and prohibition are rationally necessary based on human nature and the obligation of gratitude.

Core Idea
Gratitude for blessings obligates obedience to divine commands.

Maturidi begins by referencing al-Husayn's statement about human creation: humans are disciplined, rational beings capable of discerning benefit and harm. Because they have been blessed, gratitude is rationally obligatory. Therefore, it is not fitting for God to leave them without guidance; hence command and prohibition are necessary. This passage sets the foundation for the need for divine law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's starting point.
2
Identify the premises
Notice how Maturidi describes human nature: submissive through discipline, knowing benefit and harm, inferring unseen from seen. These are the rational capacities.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, Maturidi concludes that it is impermissible for God to withhold knowledge or impose ignorance, as that would permit lying and blame. Instead, gratitude obligates obedience, so command and prohibition are demanded.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Think about why gratitude rationally leads to the need for commands. How does this argument relate to the broader Islamic theology of divine guidance?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب، مع ما تقدّم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين : إن الله خلق خلقاً مذللاً بالتأديب، عارفًا بالنفع والضر، مستدلاً بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب ، لم يجز أن لا يُعرض [عن] المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم مع ما كان لمن خلقه نعم عليه في الخلقة، وشكر النعمة لازم في العقل، فَاسْتَأداه . ثم [يكون] الوعد والوعيد للترغيب بتعظيمه، والترهيب عن الاستخفاف به . ثم إذ كرّمه بفنون كل الكرم فعلى ذلك ثوابه لا أمد له، وإذ كان الكفر غاية في العصيان فكذلك عقوبته. وأيضاً إن الإيمان تصديق بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاد ، والكفر تكذيب بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاد ، فعلى ذلك جزاؤهما . ولهذا يجوز العفو عما دون الكفر، لأنه ليس بجحد لما لا نهاية له . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the statement concerning command and prohibition, and encouragement and deterrence, along with the sufficient part of that which al-Husayn mentioned: God created a creation submissive through discipline, knowing benefit and harm, inferring by the evidence witnessed from the unseen, it is not permissible that they be turned away from knowledge nor that ignorance be brought upon them, for that would entail permitting lying and every blameworthy thing, along with what was for whom He created—a blessing upon him in creation, and gratitude for the blessing is obligatory in the intellect, so He demanded it from them. Then [there is] promise and threat for encouragement by magnifying Him, and deterrence from belittling Him. Then when He honored him with all kinds of honor, his reward is accordingly without end, and since disbelief is the ultimate in disobedience, so is its punishment. Also, faith is affirmation of what has no end and no limit, and disbelief is denial of what has no end and no limit, so accordingly is their recompense. For this reason, forgiveness is permissible for what is less than disbelief, because it is not denial of what has no end. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the necessity of command and prohibition, and the basis for reward and punishment. Maturidi builds on the earlier statement of al-Husayn: God created humans with a nature that is tamed through discipline, capable of knowing benefit and harm, and able to infer the unseen from visible evidence. Therefore, it is not appropriate for God to withhold knowledge from them or to bring ignorance upon them, as that would permit lying and all blameworthy acts. Instead, God demands gratitude for His blessings, which is rationally obligatory. This necessitates command and prohibition. Then Maturidi discusses reward and punishment: since God honored humans with all kinds of honor, their reward must be eternal; similarly, since disbelief is the ultimate disobedience, its punishment is eternal. The key reason: faith is affirmation of infinite truths, and disbelief is denial of infinite truths, so their recompense is infinite. Hence, forgiveness is possible for sins less than disbelief, because they do not involve denial of the infinite. The passage ends with the standard formula.

Eternal Reward and Punishment
Maturidi explains the purpose of promise and threat and the basis for eternal reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that reward is eternal because of God's honor, and punishment is eternal because disbelief is the ultimate disobedience.

Core Idea
God's honor demands eternal reward; ultimate disobedience demands eternal punishment.

In this passage, Maturidi explains the basis for eternal reward and punishment. He connects the promise and threat to the purpose of encouraging magnifying God and deterring from belittling Him. Then he argues that since God honored humans with all forms of honor, the reward must be without end; similarly, since disbelief is the ultimate rebellion, the punishment is likewise eternal. This establishes the proportionality of divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the two parallel statements.
2
Understand the purpose of promise and threat
Note that الوعد والوعيد are for encouragement (ترغيب) by magnifying God and deterrence (ترهيب) from belittling Him.
3
Analyze the reasoning for eternity
Observe the parallel: because God honored humans with all honor, reward has no end; because disbelief is the utmost disobedience, punishment has no end. This is a principle of proportional recompense.
4
Reflect on the justice aspect
Consider how this argument makes eternal punishment just, not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب، مع ما تقدّم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين : إن الله خلق خلقاً مذللاً بالتأديب، عارفًا بالنفع والضر، مستدلاً بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب ، لم يجز أن لا يُعرض [عن] المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم مع ما كان لمن خلقه نعم عليه في الخلقة، وشكر النعمة لازم في العقل، فَاسْتَأداه . ثم [يكون] الوعد والوعيد للترغيب بتعظيمه، والترهيب عن الاستخفاف به . ثم إذ كرّمه بفنون كل الكرم فعلى ذلك ثوابه لا أمد له، وإذ كان الكفر غاية في العصيان فكذلك عقوبته. وأيضاً إن الإيمان تصديق بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاد ، والكفر تكذيب بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاد ، فعلى ذلك جزاؤهما . ولهذا يجوز العفو عما دون الكفر، لأنه ليس بجحد لما لا نهاية له . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the statement concerning command and prohibition, and encouragement and deterrence, along with the sufficient part of that which al-Husayn mentioned: God created a creation submissive through discipline, knowing benefit and harm, inferring by the evidence witnessed from the unseen, it is not permissible that they be turned away from knowledge nor that ignorance be brought upon them, for that would entail permitting lying and every blameworthy thing, along with what was for whom He created—a blessing upon him in creation, and gratitude for the blessing is obligatory in the intellect, so He demanded it from them. Then [there is] promise and threat for encouragement by magnifying Him, and deterrence from belittling Him. Then when He honored him with all kinds of honor, his reward is accordingly without end, and since disbelief is the ultimate in disobedience, so is its punishment. Also, faith is affirmation of what has no end and no limit, and disbelief is denial of what has no end and no limit, so accordingly is their recompense. For this reason, forgiveness is permissible for what is less than disbelief, because it is not denial of what has no end. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the necessity of command and prohibition, and the basis for reward and punishment. Maturidi builds on the earlier statement of al-Husayn: God created humans with a nature that is tamed through discipline, capable of knowing benefit and harm, and able to infer the unseen from visible evidence. Therefore, it is not appropriate for God to withhold knowledge from them or to bring ignorance upon them, as that would permit lying and all blameworthy acts. Instead, God demands gratitude for His blessings, which is rationally obligatory. This necessitates command and prohibition. Then Maturidi discusses reward and punishment: since God honored humans with all kinds of honor, their reward must be eternal; similarly, since disbelief is the ultimate disobedience, its punishment is eternal. The key reason: faith is affirmation of infinite truths, and disbelief is denial of infinite truths, so their recompense is infinite. Hence, forgiveness is possible for sins less than disbelief, because they do not involve denial of the infinite. The passage ends with the standard formula.

Correction: Maturidi argues that disbelief is 'غاية في العصيان' (the utmost in disobedience), which justifies eternal punishment, not the number of sins.

Infinite Nature of Faith and Disbelief
Maturidi explains the infinite nature of faith and disbelief and the permissibility of forgiving lesser sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why faith and disbelief result in eternal recompense due to their infinite objects, and why forgiveness is permissible for lesser sins.

Core Idea
Faith affirms the infinite; disbelief denies the infinite; their recompense is accordingly infinite.

Maturidi provides a deeper rationale for eternal reward and punishment: faith is the affirmation (تصديق) of what has no end, and disbelief is the denial (تكذيب) of what has no end. Therefore, their recompense must also have no end. He then uses this to explain why forgiveness is permissible for sins less than disbelief, as they do not constitute denial of the infinite. This argument connects the nature of faith and disbelief directly to the eternity of their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Pay attention to the key words: تصديق, تكذيب, لا نهاية له.
2
Analyze the argument for eternity
Faith affirms what is infinite, belief in God's attributes, etc. Disbelief denies that. Since the object is infinite, the recompense is infinite.
3
Understand the exception for lesser sins
Forgiveness is permissible for sins other than disbelief because they are not denial of the infinite. This distinguishes major and minor sins from kufr.
4
Reflect on divine mercy and justice
How does this explain why God may forgive lesser sins but not disbelief without repentance?

Messengers and the Active Intellect in Divine Commands
Maturidi provides three justifications for the wisdom of commandments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that both the messengers and the active use of intellect confirm the wisdom behind divine commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
Messengers provide proof even when intellect falls short; intellect must be actively used.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents three cumulative arguments for why divine commands and prohibitions are necessarily wise. Even if our minds cannot fully grasp the wisdom, the messengers bring sufficient evidence. Moreover, the intellect itself is not meant to be idle; like our limbs, it must be engaged. This lesson unpacks these interconnected points.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the passage lists three supporting points introduced with 'مع' (along with).
Pay attention to the phrase 'فمثله العقل' which is challenging; it likely means 'and such is the intellect'.
2
Identify the Three Arguments
Find the three arguments Maturidi makes: (1) Messengers bring sufficient proof even if intellect falls short. (2) Intellect abhors being unused, like limbs. (3) The same principle applies to action and its guidance.
Each argument builds on the previous one.
3
Reflect on Revelation and Reason
Consider how Maturidi balances revelation and reason: messengers provide proof, but intellect must also be active. This refutes the idea that revelation replaces reason.
The closing phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a standard expression of reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع ما كان فيما جاء بهما الرسل عن دليل كاف يُلزم القول بعظم الحكمة فيهما لو قصرت عقولنا عن الوقوف على ذلك. مع ما في العقل إباء ترك استعماله كسائر الجوارح التي لم يُحتمل تعطيلها عن المنافع التي هي فمثله العقل . مع ما كان الذي ذكرت في سائر الجوارح هو حق الفعل أيضا وإشارته. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And besides, there is in what the messengers brought a sufficient proof that obligates affirming the greatness of wisdom in them (command and prohibition) even if our intellects fall short of apprehending that. Along with the fact that the intellect abhors abandoning its use, like the other limbs that are not allowed to be left idle from their benefits, and such is the intellect. Along with the fact that what I mentioned regarding the other limbs is also the truth of action and its indication. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage provides additional justifications for the necessity of commands and prohibitions. First, even if our intellects cannot fully grasp the wisdom behind them, the messengers bring sufficient evidence that obligates us to affirm that great wisdom exists. Second, the intellect itself abhors being left unused, just as we do not allow our bodily limbs to remain idle from their beneficial functions; similarly, the intellect must be used to understand divine commands. Third, the same principle that applies to the use of limbs applies to action and its guidance—the intellect must be engaged in moral and religious obligations. The passage ends with the standard phrase, 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms

عقل
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi rejects the role of intellect in understanding divine commands.
Correction: Maturidi argues that messengers provide proof even if intellect falls short, and that intellect must be actively used, so both are complementary.

Key Takeaway
Messengers and intellect together affirm the wisdom of divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Maturidi says the intellect abhors being unused. What area of your faith have you neglected to ponder? How does that neglect affect your obedience? Write down one question about that area and seek an answer from the Qur’an or Sunnah before the end of today.

Classification of Deniers of Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates five types of deniers of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of people who deny prophethood as identified by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Denying prophethood stems from ignorance of God, His commands, or overconfidence in reason.

Imam Maturidi begins by acknowledging that people have spoken about prophethood. While the righteous affirmed it, various groups denied it. In this passage, he classifies these deniers into five distinct categories, ranging from atheists to those who dismiss miracles as mere sorcery. Understanding these categories helps us address objections to prophethood systematically.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the order of categories (a) through (e).
The passage uses letters to mark each category.
2
Identify each category
Find each category and note what the denier is missing: Creator, command, reason, or proper understanding of miracles.
Category (e) is subtle: it claims the prophet's contemporaries were simply unable to match the signs.
3
Reflect on the progression
Consider how the categories move from complete denial of God to more refined objections. This sets up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } تكلم الناس في الرسالة ؛ فأثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر . وأنكرها : (أ) من جهل صانعه بـ) ومن أقر [به] ممن جهل أمره ونهيه، جـ) ومن أقر بذلك ممن زعم أن في العقل الغنى عن الرسالة ؛ (د) مع ما أمكن مقابلة آيات من ادعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة هـ) وبعد، فإنه يحتمل ظهور عجز من حضرهم بما لم يكن [هم] في ذلك النوع تكلف واجتهاد، ولم يكونوا امتحنوا قُوى الجميع . { قال الشيخ : } (أ) فنناظر من أنكر الصانع في إثباته، إذ التنازع في إرساله لا يتمكن إلا بعد لزوم القول بهستيته وثباته . مع ما أمكن الأمران جميعا بآيات الرسل؛ إذ هم قوم نشأوا بين قوم عرفوا أحوالهم، وقد كانوا أدركوا منتهى وسعهم . فلما جاءوا بالآيات التي قهرت عقولهم - مع علمهم بأن وسعهم لا يحتمل إنشاء مثلها - لزمهم العلم بصدقه فيما أخبر من مرسله، وأن تلك الآيات مما أنشأها من أرسله لتكون رسالته من عليم حكيم قادر على إنشاء الأدلة على إثباته، ليعلموه بها وإن / لم يشهدوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist—may Allah have mercy on him—said: People spoke concerning prophethood (al-risala). The imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity affirmed it. And it was denied: (a) by one who is ignorant of his Maker; (b) and by one who acknowledged [Him] but was ignorant of His command and prohibition; (c) and by one who acknowledged that but claimed that the intellect is self-sufficient from prophethood; (d) along with the possibility of opposing the signs of one who claimed prophethood with the work of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers; (e) and furthermore, it is possible that the incapacity of those who were present with him became apparent because they had no effort and striving in that type, and they had not tested the powers of everyone. The Shaykh said: (a) We shall debate with the one who denies the Maker about establishing Him, for dispute about His sending cannot be accomplished except after affirming His existence and stability. Along with the possibility of both matters by the signs of the messengers; for they are a people who grew up among a people who knew their states, and they had comprehended the utmost of their capacity. So when they brought signs that overpowered their intellects—while knowing that their capacity could not produce the like of them—they were compelled to know the truthfulness of the messenger in what he informed concerning his sender, and that those signs were produced by the one who sent him, so that His sending be from an All-Knowing, Wise, Capable One who can produce evidences for His establishment, that they might know Him through them even if they do not see Him. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a classification of those who deny prophethood and a refutation of their positions. The author (the faqih, i.e., Imam Maturidi) lists five categories of deniers: (a) atheists who deny the Creator; (b) those who acknowledge God but reject His commands; (c) those who claim reason suffices without revelation; (d) those who dismiss prophetic miracles as comparable to sorcery; and (e) those who argue that the prophet's miracles might be matched by others if they had tried. Then the Shaykh (presumably the same author) responds, focusing first on refuting atheists by establishing God's existence, since the dispute about His sending requires first affirming His being. He then gives a general proof for prophethood: miracles (ayat) are produced by prophets among their own people, who knew them intimately and knew the limits of human capacity. When the prophets brought signs that overwhelmed their intellects, and the people knew that they themselves could not produce such things, they were compelled to accept the prophet's truthfulness and that the miracles came from God. This establishes that prophethood is from an All-Knowing, Wise, Capable God who provides evidences for His own existence, so that people may know Him through these signs.

Key Terms
الرسالة (prophethood)
الصانع (Creator)
العقل (reason)
الكهنة (soothsayers)
السحرة (sorcerers)
المشعبذة (jugglers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All deniers of prophethood are atheists.
Correction: Maturidi lists five categories, including those who acknowledge God but reject His commands, those who rely solely on reason, and those who dismiss miracles as trickery.

Checkpoint
Which category of deniers does the passage identify as those who claim reason is sufficient without prophethood?
A) Those ignorant of the Maker
B) Those who acknowledge God but ignore His commands
C) Those who claim intellect is self-sufficient from prophethood
D) Those who compare miracles to sorcery

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's classification shows that denial of prophethood arises from various forms of ignorance: of God, His commands, the limits of reason, or the nature of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which of the five categories would you find yourself if you were honest about your reactions to a prophetic command that contradicts your personal desires? What does that reveal about the state of your iman, and what will you do today to align your will with revelation?
?
When you see a miracle attributed to a prophet in the Quran, do your thoughts ever wander to explanations like 'maybe it was just a trick'? How does Maturidi's classification challenge you to purify your heart from subtle forms of denial, and what concrete step will you take to strengthen your conviction?
?
If someone told you that reason alone can guide you to all truth, would you feel a sense of pride or caution? How does this lesson expose the arrogance of relying solely on your intellect, and what will you sacrifice this week to submit your mind to the words of the Prophet?

Proof of Prophethood Through Miracles
Maturidi refutes atheists and presents the miracle-based proof for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses miracles to prove the truthfulness of prophets and refute the deniers.

Core Idea
Miracles performed by prophets among their own people compel rational acceptance of their truthfulness.

After classifying the deniers, Imam Maturidi presents a robust proof for prophethood. He first addresses the atheist by focusing on establishing the Creator, then provides a general argument: prophets were known people who performed signs beyond human capacity, proving their connection to God. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال الشيخ' to the end. Then read the translation.
The argument has two parts: refuting the atheist and the miracle proof.
2
Identify the two parts
First, the debate with the denier of the Maker. Second, the proof using the signs of the messengers.
The second part is the core argument.
3
Understand the key elements
Notice that the prophets grew up among their people, who knew their limitations. When they brought signs that overpowered intellects, the people knew they could not produce the like, so they concluded the signs were from God.
4
Reflect on the refutation
This argument refutes category (d) and (e) from the classification: miracles are not sorcery because the prophet's background precludes it, and the contemporaries indeed were unable to match them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } تكلم الناس في الرسالة ؛ فأثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر . وأنكرها : (أ) من جهل صانعه بـ) ومن أقر [به] ممن جهل أمره ونهيه، جـ) ومن أقر بذلك ممن زعم أن في العقل الغنى عن الرسالة ؛ (د) مع ما أمكن مقابلة آيات من ادعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة هـ) وبعد، فإنه يحتمل ظهور عجز من حضرهم بما لم يكن [هم] في ذلك النوع تكلف واجتهاد، ولم يكونوا امتحنوا قُوى الجميع . { قال الشيخ : } (أ) فنناظر من أنكر الصانع في إثباته، إذ التنازع في إرساله لا يتمكن إلا بعد لزوم القول بهستيته وثباته . مع ما أمكن الأمران جميعا بآيات الرسل؛ إذ هم قوم نشأوا بين قوم عرفوا أحوالهم، وقد كانوا أدركوا منتهى وسعهم . فلما جاءوا بالآيات التي قهرت عقولهم - مع علمهم بأن وسعهم لا يحتمل إنشاء مثلها - لزمهم العلم بصدقه فيما أخبر من مرسله، وأن تلك الآيات مما أنشأها من أرسله لتكون رسالته من عليم حكيم قادر على إنشاء الأدلة على إثباته، ليعلموه بها وإن / لم يشهدوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist—may Allah have mercy on him—said: People spoke concerning prophethood (al-risala). The imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity affirmed it. And it was denied: (a) by one who is ignorant of his Maker; (b) and by one who acknowledged [Him] but was ignorant of His command and prohibition; (c) and by one who acknowledged that but claimed that the intellect is self-sufficient from prophethood; (d) along with the possibility of opposing the signs of one who claimed prophethood with the work of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers; (e) and furthermore, it is possible that the incapacity of those who were present with him became apparent because they had no effort and striving in that type, and they had not tested the powers of everyone. The Shaykh said: (a) We shall debate with the one who denies the Maker about establishing Him, for dispute about His sending cannot be accomplished except after affirming His existence and stability. Along with the possibility of both matters by the signs of the messengers; for they are a people who grew up among a people who knew their states, and they had comprehended the utmost of their capacity. So when they brought signs that overpowered their intellects—while knowing that their capacity could not produce the like of them—they were compelled to know the truthfulness of the messenger in what he informed concerning his sender, and that those signs were produced by the one who sent him, so that His sending be from an All-Knowing, Wise, Capable One who can produce evidences for His establishment, that they might know Him through them even if they do not see Him. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a classification of those who deny prophethood and a refutation of their positions. The author (the faqih, i.e., Imam Maturidi) lists five categories of deniers: (a) atheists who deny the Creator; (b) those who acknowledge God but reject His commands; (c) those who claim reason suffices without revelation; (d) those who dismiss prophetic miracles as comparable to sorcery; and (e) those who argue that the prophet's miracles might be matched by others if they had tried. Then the Shaykh (presumably the same author) responds, focusing first on refuting atheists by establishing God's existence, since the dispute about His sending requires first affirming His being. He then gives a general proof for prophethood: miracles (ayat) are produced by prophets among their own people, who knew them intimately and knew the limits of human capacity. When the prophets brought signs that overwhelmed their intellects, and the people knew that they themselves could not produce such things, they were compelled to accept the prophet's truthfulness and that the miracles came from God. This establishes that prophethood is from an All-Knowing, Wise, Capable God who provides evidences for His own existence, so that people may know Him through these signs.

Key Terms
الآيات (signs/miracles)
الرسل (messengers)
الصانع (Creator)
العقل (intellect)
الصدق (truthfulness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles alone are insufficient proof for prophethood.
Correction: Maturidi argues that miracles are decisive when performed in a context where the people knew the prophet's background and limitations.

Checkpoint
What compelled the people to accept the truthfulness of the prophet according to Maturidi?
A) The prophet's moral character
B) The signs that overwhelmed their intellects and were beyond their capacity
C) The prophet's lineage
D) The prophet's knowledge of the unseen

Key Takeaway
Miracles serve as conclusive proof for prophethood when considered in the context of the prophet's known background and human limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly examined the miracles of the Quran and let them compel your heart, or do you find yourself making excuses because accepting them would demand a change in your life? What is holding you back from that rational conclusion?
?
If a miracle were performed in front of you today, would you submit to its message, or would you rationalize it away? What does your answer reveal about your trust in your own capacity versus recognizing the power of God?

Five Groups Who Deny Prophethood
Imam Maturidi enumerates five groups of deniers of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of people who reject prophethood as outlined by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Prophethood is denied in five distinct ways—each rooted in a different error.

Imam Maturidi begins his discussion on prophethood by mapping the landscape of denial. He lists five groups who reject the institution of prophethood, ranging from outright atheism to nuanced rationalism. Understanding this classification helps us see the targeted nature of the proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the five groups
Find each of the five categories (a) through (e) in the text. Note how each group denies prophethood for a distinct reason.
Group (a) is atheists, (b) theists who reject divine law, (c) rationalists, (d) those who claim miracles can be counterfeited, (e) skeptics about universal human capacity.
3
Understand the progression
Notice the progression from denial of God to denial of the need for revelation. The first group rejects the Creator entirely, while later groups accept God but reject aspects of prophethood.
4
See why the author addresses atheists first
Reflect on why the author says 'We will argue with the one who denies the Maker' before addressing the others. This shows the foundational nature of belief in God.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكلم الناس في الرسالة؛ فأثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر. وأنكرها: (أ) من جهل صانعه، (ب) ومن أقر به ممن جهل أمره ونهيه، (ج) ومن أقر بذلك ممن زعم أن في العقل الغنى عن الرسالة، (د) مع ما أمكن مقابلة آيات من ادعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة، (هـ) وبعد، فإنه يحتمل ظهور عجز من حضرهم بما لم يكن لهم في ذلك النوع تكلف واجتهاد، ولم يكونوا امتحنوا قوى الجميع. قال الشيخ: (أ) فنناظر من أنكر الصانع في إثباته، إذ التنازع في إرساله لا يتمكن إلا بعد لزوم القول بهستيته وثباته. مع ما أمكن الأمران جميعا بآيات الرسل؛ إذ هم قوم نشأوا بين قوم عرفوا أحوالهم، وقد كانوا أدركوا منتهى وسعهم. فلما جاءوا بالآيات التي قهرت عقولهم - مع علمهم بأن وسعهم لا يحتمل إنشاء مثلها - لزمهم العلم بصدقه فيما أخبر من مرسله، وأن تلك الآيات مما أنشأها من أرسله لتكون رسالته من عليم حكيم قادر على إنشاء الأدلة على إثباته، ليعلموه بها وإن لم يشهدوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
People spoke about the message (risalah); the imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men affirmed it. And it was denied by: (a) one who was ignorant of his Maker, (b) one who acknowledged Him but was ignorant of His command and prohibition, (c) one who acknowledged that but claimed that the intellect is sufficient apart from the message, (d) with the possibility of countering the signs of one who claimed the message with the craft of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers, (e) and moreover, it is possible that the incapacity of those whom he attended appeared because they had no effort and exertion in that type [of thing], and they had not tested the powers of all. Al-Shaykh said: (a) We will argue with the one who denies the Maker regarding His affirmation, for dispute about His sending cannot occur except after the necessity of affirming His existence and permanence. Moreover, both [denial and affirmation] are possible with the signs of the messengers, for they are a people who grew up among people who knew their conditions, and they had attained the utmost of their capacity. So when they brought signs that overwhelmed their intellects—while knowing that their capacity could not produce the like—knowledge of his truthfulness in what he reported from his Sender became necessary for them, and that those signs were created by the One who sent him so that his message would be from the All-Knowing, the Wise, the Powerful, capable of creating proofs for His affirmation, that they might know Him by them even if they do not see Him. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a proof. The author first enumerates five groups of people who deny prophethood: (a) atheists, (b) theists who reject divine law, (c) rationalists who think intellect suffices, (d) skeptics who claim miracles can be counterfeited, (e) skeptics who argue that the prophet's contemporaries may have been incapable of replicating the miracle but that does not prove inability of all. Then the author addresses the first group (atheists) directly. He argues that before discussing prophethood, one must first establish the existence of the Creator. The proof for prophethood from miracles is presented: prophets are known to their people, who know their capacities. When the prophet brings a miracle that exceeds the capacity of his people, they must accept that it comes from God, because they know that the prophet himself could not produce it. Therefore, the miracle proves both the existence of God and the truthfulness of the prophet. The passage ends with a standard formula of reliance on God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the classification is merely a historical list, but it systematically categorizes all forms of denial of prophethood.
Correction: The classification is exhaustive in covering the logical possibilities: rejection of God, rejection of revelation, sufficiency of reason, skepticism about miracles, and skepticism about human incapacity.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which group denies prophethood because they think the intellect is sufficient?
A) One who is ignorant of his Maker
B) One who acknowledges Him but is ignorant of His command and prohibition
C) One who claims that the intellect is sufficient apart from the message
D) One who says miracles can be countered by sorcery

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is denied in five ways, each rooted in a different error: ignoring the Maker, ignoring His law, over-reliance on reason, skepticism about miracles, or skepticism about human limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you truly live as though God's commands and prohibitions are real and binding, or do you, like group (b), acknowledge God but ignore His law in your daily choices? Name one command you have been neglecting and commit to act on it today.
?
When you hear about the Prophet's miracles, does any trace of doubt creep in, perhaps thinking they could be exaggerated or matched by others? How does that doubt affect your trust in his prophethood, and what will you do to strengthen your certainty?

The Miracle Argument for Prophethood and God's Existence
Imam Maturidi provides a rational proof for God's existence and prophethood via miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles of the prophets prove both the existence of God and the truthfulness of the messenger.

Core Idea
A miracle, witnessed by a people who know the prophet's limits, forces rational acknowledgment of God.

Imam Maturidi now addresses the first group of deniers—the atheists. He argues that before discussing prophethood, one must first establish the existence of the Creator. Then he presents the classic argument from miracles: prophets come among people who know them, and when they bring signs that exceed human capacity, the people must accept that the signs come from God, thus confirming both God's existence and the prophet's truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'قال الشيخ' onward
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: first establish God, then prophethood through miracles.
2
Understand the logical order
The author says dispute about sending cannot occur until God's existence is established. This highlights the priority of theology over prophethood in debate.
3
Follow the miracle reasoning
Prophets grow up among people who know their capacities. When they bring signs that overwhelm the intellect, people realize they could not produce them, so the signs must be from God.
The key is the known incapacity of the prophet and his people.
4
Conclude the argument
The miracle proves the truthfulness of the prophet's message and that it comes from an All-Knowing, Wise, Powerful Being who creates evidence for Himself.
The passage ends with a formula of reliance on Allah.

Source Text

This passage is a classification and a proof. Necessity of Divine Commands and Prohibitions
Proves the necessity of law from divine wisdom and human nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine commands and prohibitions are necessary according to Imam al-Maturidi, based on divine wisdom and human nature.

Core Idea
Divine law is not arbitrary but necessary to prevent societal collapse.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses those who accept God but reject His commands. He argues that such rejection implies a lack of wisdom in creation, and more practically, without law humans would destroy each other in the struggle for survival. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the two distinct parts: a theoretical argument and a practical one.
2
Understand the first argument: denying commands implies denying wisdom
The author says that one who denies command and prohibition and promise and threat does not ascribe wisdom to creation. This leads to a view that creation is purposeless, which is not the act of a wise being. The wisdom of God in creation shows He is Wise.
This argument refutes the idea that God's actions are arbitrary.
3
Understand the second argument: law is needed to prevent conflict and annihilation
The author describes human nature: love of life, desire for pleasure, and need for sustenance. Without law, everyone would selfishly pursue these, leading to conflict, mutual repulsion, and eventual annihilation. So God established lawful/unlawful and commands/prohibitions to ensure social harmony and individual survival.
This is a practical necessity rooted in human nature.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some think God's commands are arbitrary tests. The lesson shows they are necessary for order and survival, arising from divine wisdom and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ب) ثم من أنكر الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد لم يحصل للإنشاء حكمة، وإنما حصل منه على الإنشاء ثم الإفناء. ثم معلوم أن كل من ذلك عاقبة فعله ليس بحكيم. فدلت حكمة صانع العالم - بما جعل فيه من الأدلة على وحدانيته وعظيم سلطانه - على أنه حكيم. والله الموفق. مع ما كان الله سبحانه - إذ هو غني بذاته حكيم في فعله - خلق الخلق للبقاء إلى نهاية قدرة جعلها لهم؛ ثم لم يجعل البقاء إلا بالأغذية، وقد حبب إليهم البقاء ودوام الحياة. فلو لم يجعل عليهم الأمر والنهي لبادر كل إلى ما يطمع فيه البقاء ودوام الحياة، مع ما له من اللذة والشهوة؛ ثم يفعل أقرانه بذلك الشيء نحو فعله، فيحدث بينهم التنازع والتجاذب، ويحملهم ذلك على التدافع؛ وفي ذلك خوف الفناء بما به جعل البقاء. فلزم جعل الحرمات والحل والأمر والنهي بما فيه من الوعد والوعيد؛ ليعلم كل ما له مما ليس له؛ فيسلم من كل عداوة وتبقى له روحه.
(b) Then one who denies the command and prohibition and promise and threat does not ascribe wisdom to the creation; rather, he obtains from it creation and then annihilation. Then it is known that anyone whose action has such an outcome is not wise. So the wisdom of the Maker of the world—by what He placed in it of proofs for His oneness and great authority—indicated that He is Wise. And Allah is the Giver of success. Moreover, Allah—since He is Self-sufficient by His essence, Wise in His action—created creation for permanence until the end of the capacity that He assigned to them; then He did not make permanence except through nourishment, and He made them love permanence and continuation of life. So had He not imposed upon them command and prohibition, everyone would hasten to what they desire for permanence and continuation of life, along with what they have of pleasure and desire; then their peers would do the same thing towards that thing, resulting in conflict and contention among them, and that leads to mutual repulsion; and in that is fear of annihilation by that through which permanence is made. So it was necessary to establish inviolabilities and lawful and unlawful, and command and prohibition including promise and threat, so that each may know what is his and what is not his; thus he is safe from all enmity and his soul remains.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine law (shar'). The author addresses the second group who acknowledge God but reject commands and prohibitions. He argues that one who denies that creation has a wise purpose reduces it to mere creation followed by annihilation—that is not the act of a wise being. The wisdom of God, evident in the order of the universe, shows He is Wise. Then the author presents a practical argument: God, being self-sufficient and wise, created humans for a limited period of survival, sustaining them through food and making them love life. If there were no law, each person would selfishly pursue survival and pleasure, leading to conflict, strife, and ultimately destruction. Hence, God necessarily established lawful and unlawful, commands and prohibitions with promises and threats, so that each person knows his rights and duties, thus ensuring social harmony and individual survival. The argument is based on the observed human nature and the need for order, deriving it from divine wisdom.

Key Terms
أمر ونهي
حكمة
بقاء
تدافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's commands are arbitrary tests with no deeper reason.
Correction: This lesson shows that commands and prohibitions arise from divine wisdom and are necessary to prevent societal collapse and ensure human survival.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say divine commands and prohibitions are necessary?
A) Because God needs to test humans.
B) Because without them, humans would destroy each other in the struggle for survival.
C) Because God likes to give orders.
D) Because humans are naturally obedient.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary but rooted in God's wisdom and necessary to prevent human self-destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You love life and desire to continue. How would your pursuit of these desires destroy your relationships if there were no divine limits? Where have you already seen the seeds of that destruction in your own heart?
?
The passage says denying commands and prohibitions implies creation is purposeless. Do you ever live as if your actions have no eternal consequences? What would it mean for you today to act as if every choice is seen by the Wise Creator?
?
The argument shows that law is necessary to prevent mutual annihilation. When have you resented God's commands as restrictive? How does this lesson change your view of His laws as a gift of preservation?

Why Divine Commands Are Not Pointless
Refutes the objection that commands are unnecessary because God knows all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's commands and prohibitions are not pointless despite His self-sufficiency and omniscience.

Core Idea
God's commands are not for His benefit but for His wisdom.

A common objection to divine commands is that they would be pointless if God gains nothing and already knows everything. This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents this objection and offers a profound reply rooted in God's transcendence and wisdom. Understanding this reply clarifies the nature of divine legislation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines (up to 'disappears') to understand the objector's logic based on human analogies.
Notice how the objector uses the witnessed realm (shahid) as a basis.
2
Read the refutation: creation analogy
Read the response: just as Allah created the world not for benefit or harm, so too His commands are not for such purposes.
This is the key analogy that dissolves the objection.
3
Notice the distinction between needy and Wise
The author distinguishes between the needy (who act for benefit) and God (who is Wise and Self-sufficient).
This shows that using human analogies for God is invalid.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with the statement that the Wise does not commit evil. Consider how this connects to the nature of divine commands.
Though truncated, the point is that God's wisdom ensures commands are good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أنكر الأمر والنهي والمحنة ذهب إلى معنى المحنة في الشاهد؛ إنما هو لظهور ما خفى وتجلي ما استتر، والأمر والنهي لمنفعة ينالها الآمر والناهي أو مكروه يدفعه، فإذ كان الله غنيا بذاته عليما بالسرائر والخفيات ذهب معنى المحنة والأمر والنهي. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: إن أمره ونهيه ومحنته على ما يذكر، فإن كان فعله لذلك لكان لمكروه يدفع أو محبوب يجلب أو عيب عنه يتخلى. والله سبحانه أنشأ العالم لا للذي يذكر، فمثله الأمر والنهي والمحنة. مع ما كان ذلك التقدير إنما هو فعل المحتاجين مما تعلو درجاتهم وتجل أقدارهم، ولو فعلوا غير ذلك كان عليهم في فعل ذلك ضرر عاجل وشر آجل. فأما من هو حكيم بذاته، غني، فهو لا يفعل لنفع ولا لدفع، فمثله الأمر والنهي. مع ما بينا من اختلاف الممتحنين في الغنى والحكمة لم يجز تقدير أحدهما بالآخر. ولا يحتمل الحكيم فعل الشر الذي يخالف حكمة الربوبية.
And one who denies the command, prohibition, and trial went to the meaning of trial in the witnessed realm: it is only for the manifestation of what is hidden and the disclosure of what is concealed, and command and prohibition are for a benefit that the commander and prohibitor gain or a harm they repel. So since Allah is Self-sufficient by His essence, Knowing of secrets and hidden things, the meaning of trial, command, and prohibition disappears. Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say—and with Allah is success—: Indeed His command, prohibition, and trial are as mentioned. If His action were for that, it would be for a harm to repel or a beloved to attract or a defect to disengage from. And Allah created the world not for what is mentioned, so likewise are the command, prohibition, and trial. Moreover, that estimation is only the action of the needy, by which their degrees rise and their statuses become exalted; and if they did otherwise, they would incur immediate harm and future evil in doing that. As for one who is Wise by His essence, Self-sufficient, He does not act for benefit nor for repulsion, so likewise are the command and prohibition. Moreover, since we have shown the difference between the tested and the tester in wealth and wisdom, it is not permissible to analogize one with the other. And the Wise does not commit evil that contradicts the wisdom of lordship.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that in the observable world, trials and commands are meant to reveal hidden matters or to benefit the commander or protect him from harm. Since God is self-sufficient and knows all hidden things, such commands and trials are pointless. The author (Al-Faqih) replies by drawing an analogy with creation: God created the world not for any benefit or harm to Himself, but as an act of wisdom. Similarly, commands and prohibitions are not for God's benefit but are consistent with His wisdom. The author further distinguishes between needy beings, who act for self-benefit, and God, who is self-sufficient and wise—He does not act for such purposes. Therefore, commands and prohibitions are not analogous to human commands; they are part of the divine wisdom. The passage ends with the assertion that God does not do evil that contradicts His wisdom. (Note: the passage is truncated; the full argument continues on the next page.)

Key Terms
محنة
غني
حكيم
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God gains nothing from commands, they are arbitrary or pointless.
Correction: The passage shows that God's commands are not for His benefit but for His wisdom, just as creation is not for His benefit. They are expressions of divine wisdom, not arbitrariness.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that God's commands are pointless because He gains nothing?
A) He says God does gain something: human worship.
B) He says commands are for human benefit, not God's.
C) He says God's actions are not for benefit or harm, just like creation.
D) He says the objection is invalid because God is all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God's commands are not for His own benefit but are rooted in His wisdom, just as His creation is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God’s commands are for His wisdom and not for His benefit, would you still obey when you see no personal benefit in it? What does your obedience say about your understanding of God’s wisdom?
?
When you find a command difficult, do you secretly think it is pointless? This passage says God’s commands are not pointless—what does that mean for how you approach your struggles in obedience?
?
The passage shows that God is not like us—He commands not for any need. How does this affect your view of His commands? Are you obeying out of love for His wisdom or out of habit?

Prophethood

Rational Goodness Necessitates Commands and Prophethood
Maturidi argues that reason's moral judgments necessitate divine commands and messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reason's inherent approval of truth and justice and disapproval of falsehood and injustice leads to the rational necessity of divine commands, reward and punishment, and the sending of prophets.

Core Idea
Reason's moral intuition demands divine guidance.

Maturidi begins by asserting that intellects naturally recognize truth and justice as good, and falsehood and injustice as evil. This intrinsic moral sense, he argues, compels reason to command good and forbid evil, making divine commands rationally necessary. Moreover, this reasoning leads to the necessity of prophets to clarify what is ambiguous, ensuring that humanity can be held accountable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational foundation
Read the passage carefully. Maturidi starts by stating that intellects inherently deem truth and justice good, and falsehood and injustice bad. Recognize this as the starting point of his argument.
Look for the words 'حسن' (good) and 'قبح' (ugly) in the Arabic.
2
Trace the consequences
Notice how Maturidi moves from this moral judgment to intellects commanding and prohibiting actions, and then to the necessity of command, prohibition, reward, and punishment. Understand the logical flow.
The phrase 'فيجب الأمر والنهي بضرورة العقل' is key.
3
Connect to prophethood
Finally, see why prophets become necessary: to guide people to the clear signs of justice and truth and to explain ambiguous matters. This completes the argument.
The term 'الرسل' (messengers) appears in the final part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه قد حسن في العقول الصدق والعدل وقبّح فيها الجور والكذب، فجعل الفريق الأول عظيما في القلوب كريما، والثاني حقيراً مهينا، فتصير العقول آمرة بكسب ما يُعلى شرف من رزق منها، وناهية عما فيه هَوانُ صاحبها؛ فيجب الأمر والنهي بضرورة العقل ثم الثواب، لتتم الكرامة لمن اختار سبلها والقيام بوفائها، والعقاب لمن آثر هواه على إشارة العقل. وفيما ذكرنا لزوم القول بالرسل ليدلوهم على معالم العدل والصدق ومنار ضدهما، على الإشارة إلى كل شيء أشكلت مائيته؛ ليكون أمر الأحوال للحمد موافقا. والله الموفق.
And moreover, truth and justice have been deemed good in intellects, and injustice and falsehood deemed ugly, so He made the first group great in hearts, noble, and the second group contemptible, humiliated. Thus intellects become commanding to acquire what elevates the honor of one who is endowed with it, and prohibiting what involves the disgrace of its possessor. So command and prohibition are necessary by necessity of intellect, then reward, so that nobility may be perfected for whoever chooses its paths and fulfills its obligations, and punishment for whoever prefers his desire over the indication of intellect. And in what we have mentioned is the necessity of affirming messengers to guide them to the landmarks of justice and truth and the beacon of their opposites, and to indicate everything whose nature is ambiguous, so that the state of affairs may be in harmony with praise. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an argument that reason itself necessitates divine commands, prohibitions, and prophethood. Maturidi states that intellects inherently recognize truth (ṣidq) and justice (ʿadl) as good, and falsehood and injustice as ugly. Consequently, these qualities are esteemed or despised in hearts. Reason then commands the pursuit of what elevates honor and forbids what brings disgrace. Therefore, command and prohibition are rationally necessary, and reward and punishment follow to complete the honor for those who obey and to punish those who follow desire over reason. Moreover, this leads to the necessity of prophets (rusul) who guide people to the clear signs of justice and truth and clarify ambiguous matters, so that all affairs may be in harmony with praise of God.

Key Terms
الصدق
العدل
الجور
الكذب
الرسل
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone is sufficient without revelation
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason leads to the need for prophets to clarify what is ambiguous.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do intellects naturally command?
A) Pursuit of pleasure
B) Acquisition of what elevates honor
C) Rejection of all authority
D) Following desire

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational argument shows that divine commands and prophethood are not arbitrary but are necessitated by the very structure of reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson states that intellect commands actions that elevate honor and prohibits actions that bring disgrace. Look at your own life: are you more driven by reason's command to pursue truth and justice, or by your desires? What specific decision today will you make to follow the intellect's guidance over your whims?
?
The passage concludes that messengers are necessary to clarify ambiguous matters. Where in your understanding of faith or morality do you feel uncertainty that requires prophetic guidance? Why do you hesitate to seek it?
?
Maturidi says that reward is needed to perfect nobility for those who choose the path of reason. If you truly believed that every choice to follow truth and justice leads to eternal nobility, would you change how you act in a seemingly small moral dilemma this week? What is holding you back?

The Rational Necessity of Prophets
Maturidi concludes the necessity of prophets to guide to justice and truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that reason itself requires sending prophets to clarify justice, truth, and the essence of things.

Core Idea
Reason points beyond itself: prophets are needed to complete moral guidance.

Maturidi has established that humans can know some good and evil through reason, but they need additional guidance to fully discern the landmarks of justice and truth. In this concluding passage, he asserts that the very logic of moral reasoning necessitates the sending of messengers. He also highlights that prophets clarify the essential nature of things that reason alone finds problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is a conclusion drawn from previous arguments.
The passage is concise, so pay attention to every term.
2
Identify the key claims
Find the necessity of messengers, their role in guiding to justice and truth, and the indication of everything whose quiddity is problematic.
The term 'ma'iyya' (quiddity) is central.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider the final phrase: 'so that the state of affairs may be in accordance with praise.' What does this imply about divine guidance?
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Relate this passage to the overall Maturidi argument that prophethood is a rational necessity, complementing reason.
This reinforces the harmony between reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيما ذكرنا لزوم القول بالرسل ليدلوهم على معالم العدل والصدق ومنار ضدهما، على الإشارة إلى كل شيء أشكلت مائيته؛ ليكون أمر الأحوال للحمد موافقا. والله الموفق.
And in what we have mentioned, there is the necessity of affirming the messengers, to guide them to the landmarks of justice and truth and the beacon of their opposites, so as to indicate everything whose quiddity is problematic, so that the state of affairs may be in accordance with praise. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This short passage draws a conclusion from the previous arguments. Maturidi says that based on what has been mentioned (the need for commands, prohibitions, and rational discernment of good and evil), there is a necessity (luzum) of affirming the messengers (al-rusul). Their role is to guide people to the landmarks (ma'alim) of justice and truth and to the beacon (manar) of their opposites. They also indicate everything whose quiddity (ma'iyya) is problematic—that is, things that reason alone might not fully grasp. The purpose is that the state of affairs may be in accordance with praise (fi an yakunu amr al-ahwal li-l-hamd muwafiqan), meaning that the world runs in a praiseworthy manner under divine guidance. Thus, prophethood is a logical extension of the rational need for moral guidance.

Key Terms

عدل
صدق
مائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are only needed to deliver laws and commands.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophets also guide to the essential nature of things (ma'iyya) and to the landmarks of justice and truth, which reason alone may not fully grasp.

Checkpoint

B) To guide people to the landmarks of justice and truth and clarify what is problematic
C) To establish political authority
D) To punish disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is rationally necessary to guide humans to the full landmarks of justice, truth, and the essential nature of things.

?
Maturidi says prophets indicate 'everything whose quiddity is problematic.' What is one deep question about life or morality that you have not resolved through reason alone? Are you willing to submit to prophetic teaching on that matter today?

Human Need for Guidance Towards Outcomes
Maturidi argues humans need a guide due to ignorance about consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument for why humans need prophetic guidance due to ignorance about consequences.

Core Idea
Humans cannot see consequences; they need a guide who can.

Maturidi begins by observing that no rational person would neglect themselves and fall into desires. Instead, they strive to moderate desires for good outcomes, but they suffer from ignorance that leads them astray. This establishes the need for someone who knows the outcomes of affairs—a prophet—to discipline the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Maturidi's argument.
Notice the key terms: عاقل, شهوات, عواقب الأمور.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the passage: premise (no rational person neglects themselves), problem (ignorance about consequences leads to harm), solution (need for a guide who knows outcomes).
This is a rational argument for prophethood.
3
Reflect on the need for guidance
Ask yourself: Why can't humans rely on their own reason alone? How does Maturidi prove the necessity of a prophet?
The answer lies in ignorance being a cause of self-destruction.
4
Connect to Aqidah
Understand that this argument is a foundation for belief in prophethood from a rational perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل في الشاهد يرضى إهمال نفسه عن التعاهد أن تنهمك في الشهوات، بل كل يجتهد على تسويتها على ما لا يضرها، وعلى ما يُحمد عواقبه. على ما فيها من الجهل الذي يُعطبه بما به يرجو نجاته ويضره فيما به يطمع نفعه. فذلك يُحوجه إلى من يعلم عواقب الأمور، حتى يروض نفسه على إشارته، دون أن يهملها لشهواتها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after, there is no rational person in the witnessed world who would be pleased with neglecting himself from taking care so that he becomes engrossed in desires. Rather, everyone strives to make them [desires] moderate in a way that does not harm him and in a way whose consequences are praiseworthy — despite the ignorance in him that destroys him through that by which he hopes for deliverance and harms him through that by which he seeks benefit. So that necessitates for him someone who knows the outcomes of affairs, so that he may discipline his soul according to his guidance, without neglecting it to its desires. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
Maturidi presents a psychological argument for the need for prophets. He states that no rational person (la 'aqil) in this world (al-shahid) would be content to neglect himself and become engrossed in desires. Instead, everyone strives to moderate their desires so that they are not harmful and their consequences are praiseworthy. However, humans suffer from ignorance (jahl) that can destroy them even through what they hope will save them. Therefore, they need someone who knows the outcomes of affairs (man ya'lamu 'awaqib al-umur) to guide them, so that they can discipline their souls (yarawwida nafsahu) according to his guidance, rather than abandoning them to their desires. This need for a knower of consequences is a rational basis for prophethood.

شهوات
عواقب الأمور
رياضة النفس

Misconception: Some think humans can manage their lives through reason alone without divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human ignorance about consequences makes them prone to error, so they need a guide who knows outcomes.

C) Wealth

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that human ignorance about consequences necessitates a divinely guided teacher (prophet) to discipline the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Prophethood as a Rational Necessity
Maturidi asserts that the need for prophets is necessary by reason for both religion and world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that affirming prophethood is obligatory by rational necessity, covering both religious and worldly needs.

Core Idea
Reason itself demands prophets for both religion and worldly life.

Maturidi builds his argument for prophethood on rational grounds. He has already discussed the need for divine commands and prohibitions; now he ties that need directly to the institution of prophethood. This passage asserts that reason, by its own necessity, requires messengers to fulfill religious and worldly purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the immediate connection to prior arguments about command and prohibition.
2
Identify the key claim
Locate the phrase 'by the necessity of reason' (bi-ḍarūrat al-ʿaql) and the dual need for religion and world (dīnan wa dunyā).
This is the core of Maturidi's rational argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about what it means for prophethood to be rationally necessary even in worldly affairs. How does this strengthen the case for prophecy?
The misconception warns against limiting the need to religion only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نرجع إلى مناظرة من أنكر الرسالة للوجوه التي ذكرت، بعد إقراره بالتوحيد وإيمانه بالأمر والنهي، مع ما فيما ذُكرت من أدلة الأمر والنهي مقرونة بالحاجة إلى الرسالة كفاية لمن تصح نفسه. ثم نقول: يجب القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل في إيجاب الحاجة إليها دينا ودنيا، ثم في إثبات الإفضال من الله إن كان في العقل منه غنى.
Then we return to debating one who denied the message (risala) for the reasons mentioned, after his affirmation of tawhid and his belief in command and prohibition, along with what was mentioned of evidences for command and prohibition connected to the need for the message — sufficient for one whose soul is sound. Then we say: Affirmation of the message is obligatory by the necessity of reason in establishing the need for it in religion and world, and then in establishing the bestowal of favor from God, if reason has self-sufficiency in that.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
Maturidi now returns to a dialectical context: debating someone who denies prophethood (al-risala) but already affirms tawhid and believes in divine commands and prohibitions. The previous arguments for commands and prohibitions, linked to the need for messengers, are sufficient for anyone with a sound soul. Then he makes a strong claim: it is obligatory to affirm the message by the necessity of reason (bi-darurat al-'aql) in establishing the need for it both in religion (dinan) and in the world (dunya). Furthermore, reason itself might deem prophethood as a divine favor (ifdal) even if reason were self-sufficient otherwise. The point is that rational necessity extends not only to commands but to the institution of prophethood that conveys and interprets those commands.

Key Terms
رسالة
ضرورة العقل
إيمان
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophethood is only needed for religion, but Maturidi says it is needed for worldly affairs as well.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states the need is 'dīnan wa dunyā' (in religion and world), so prophethood is essential for both spheres.

Checkpoint

A) Because of tradition
B) By the necessity of reason in establishing need in religion and world
C) Because of consensus
D) Because of miracles

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that the need for prophets is not just religious but also worldly, and it is a rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specifically would change if you truly submitted to that rational necessity? Commit to one action this week that demonstrates this trust.
?
Maturidi says the need for prophets is 'by necessity of reason'. When did you last allow your reason to lead you to submission rather than to pride? Reflect on a situation where your intellect resisted revelation - what was the root of that resistance? Resolve to approach that area with humility.
?
The passage notes that even if reason were self-sufficient, prophethood is still a divine favor. Do you treat the gift of prophethood as a favor or as an inconvenience? What would it mean for your daily life to receive the Prophet's message as a pure favor from God? Name one grace you have ignored.

Practical Need for Prophets in Agriculture, Medicine, and Domestication
Maturidi demonstrates rational need for prophets through practical arts and sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi holds that reason alone is insufficient for practical sciences like agriculture, medicine, and animal domestication, and why prophets are rationally necessary.

Core Idea
Prophets teach not only religion but also the practical arts essential for human survival.

Maturidi presents a unique argument for prophethood grounded in everyday human needs. He points out that while reason can recognize general benefits and harms, it cannot determine the specific details of agriculture, medicine, or the domestication of wild animals. This lesson explores how these practical necessities logically require divinely guided teachers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic slowly, noting the repeated phrase 'ليس في العقول سبيل' (there is no way in minds). This is the core of the argument.
The passage is dense; try to grasp the overall flow before diving into details.
2
Identify the practical domains listed
Make a mental list: agriculture, medicine (including poisons), crafts (clothing/shelter), domestication of beasts. Maturidi uses each to show reason's limitation.
3
Understand the argument pattern
For each domain, notice that Maturidi first states that reason cannot attain necessary knowledge, then concludes that a divinely informed teacher is required.
The pattern is: 'no way in reason' → 'therefore necessity of one whom God informs.'
4
Connect to the broader theme
Ask yourself: How does this argument show that prophethood is rationally necessary? Maturidi is grounding theology in everyday human experience.
This lesson is part of the larger Maturidi proof for prophethood.
