⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأمر الدنيا [جار] فيما به أيضا قوام الدين نحو أن خلق البشر وجعلهم أهل المحنة، وأنبت لهم من الأرض بما أنزل من ماء السماء أغذية لهم وأدوية، ثم أنبت منها الأدواء والسموم القاتلة، وقبح في عقولهم الامتحان بأنفسهم ليعرفوا المؤذى من المغذى ، لما لعل [فيه] عطب الممتحن، وليس في العقول سبيل تعرف ذلك. لزم القول بمن يُطْلِعه الله على كل جوهر منها لتحيي بما يأكلون أبدانهم ويقيموا به دينهم. ثم في الابتداء ليس في العقول [سبيل] تعرف الوجوه التي تنبت من الزراعة وما فيها من التدبير . ثم بعد التمام والعلم بجوهر [0] لا بد ممن يعلم كيف يستعمله حتى يصلح للاغتذاء، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك. ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يُدفَع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حدّه، لأنه [إنها يتعلم] ممن يعلم حد ذلك ثم دواءه إن ضره بالقدر الذي به يدفع ضرره، ثم علوم الطب، مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن. ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنّهم ، والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد. ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأمل فيها أنها لأي منفعة خلقت، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا، ولا كيف يروّضها؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما هي له، حتى تنقاد.
The matter of the world — by which also the establishment of religion — is such that He created humans and made them people of trial, and grew for them from the earth with what He sent down from the sky water, nourishments and medicines, then grew from it ailments and lethal poisons, and it was deemed ugly in their minds to test themselves to know the harmful from the nutritious, because perhaps the tester would perish, and there is no way in minds to know that. So it becomes necessary to affirm one whom God informs of every substance of them, so that they may revive their bodies by what they eat and establish their religion. Then in the beginning, there is no way in minds to know the aspects that grow from agriculture and the management in it. Then after completion and knowledge of the substance, there must be one who knows how to use it until it becomes suitable for nourishment, according to the differences in what was made for its rectification. Then He placed in food types of harm, to which the beneficiary succumbs if he does not preserve its limit, because he learns from one who knows that limit, then its remedy if it harms him with that measure by which he repels its harm. Then the medical sciences, with the variation of temperaments and the use of lethal poisons, so that they may know the extent of benefit which the body endures. Then in the types of crafts by which their covering and shelter are established, and protection for them from heat and cold. Then in what He created for them of difficult beasts, which the observer does not know for what benefit they were created, nor whether they were created for his benefit or not, nor how to tame them, for each of them is innately averse to that which is for it, until they become submissive.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage provides a practical, almost empirical, argument for the need for prophets. Maturidi argues that worldly matters (amr al-dunya) are intertwined with religion (qiwam al-din). God created humans for trial, grew plants from earth and water as food and medicine, but also grew diseases and lethal poisons. Reason finds it ugly (qubih) to test oneself to distinguish harmful from nutritious because one might die. Reason has no way (laysa fi al-'uqul sabil) to know that. Thus, it is necessary to affirm someone whom God informs (yutli'uhu Allah) about every substance, so that humans may nourish their bodies and establish their religion. Similarly, reason cannot initially know the ways of agriculture. After produce is known, one needs a teacher to know how to use it properly. Food also contains harmful aspects that require knowing their limits and antidotes. The medical sciences, with varying temperaments and lethal poisons, require guidance to know beneficial doses. Crafts for clothing and shelter, and domestication of wild animals, also require knowledge that reason alone cannot provide. All these point to the need for divinely guided teachers—prophets—to teach humans these essential arts and sciences.

Key Terms
زراعة
طب
دواب
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets only teach religious laws and rituals, not practical worldly knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophets are necessary to teach practical arts like agriculture, medicine, and domestication, which reason alone cannot perfect.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why cannot humans simply learn about harmful substances through experimentation?
A) Because experimentation is forbidden by religion
B) Because the tester might die, and reason deems this ugly
C) Because all substances are safe
D) Because experiments require expensive equipment

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that prophets are rationally necessary because human reason cannot independently master the practical arts essential for life and religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you take for granted the knowledge of what is harmful to your body? If you truly believed that without prophetic guidance you might not even know which foods are safe, would you be more desperate for God's messengers? What step can you take this week to learn from the Prophet ﷺ about your daily habits?
?
You trust doctors, farmers, and engineers, but do you trust the prophets as much? If reason alone cannot figure out agriculture and medicine, what makes you think you can figure out the purpose of your own life without revelation? Where are you relying on your own judgment instead of seeking divine guidance?
?
The passage says that even wild animals are made submissive for you only through knowledge from God. Are there areas in your life—like your desires, your work, your relationships—that you are still trying to 'tame' without God's guidance? What would change if you truly acted on the belief that you need prophetic guidance for every aspect of your life?

Prophethood
intermediate
8 min
Why Reason Demands Prophethood
Maturidi states the rational necessity of prophethood after accepting tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reason itself necessitates the need for prophethood in religious and worldly affairs.

Core Idea
Reason recognizes its own limits and demands prophethood for complete guidance.

After establishing tawhid and the reality of divine commands, Maturidi turns to the question of prophethood. He argues that reason alone, when properly understood, points to the need for prophets. This passage presents the core rational argument for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the Argument
Recognize that Maturidi is addressing someone who already accepts tawhid and divine command/prohibition. The debate about prophethood comes after these foundations are laid.
This shows the systematic order of Maturidi's theology: tawhid first, then prophethood.
2
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note the key phrases: 'يجب القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل' (it is necessary to affirm prophethood by the necessity of reason).
The passage is dense; take your time.
3
Identify the Core Claim
Find the main claim: reason itself necessitates prophethood. Maturidi says 'بضرورة العقل' (by the necessity of reason) – meaning reason demands it, not just revelation.
4
Analyze the Dual Need
Notice that reason requires prophethood for both religious ('دينا') and worldly ('دنيا') affairs. This shows prophethood is not only for spiritual matters but also for social and ethical guidance.
This counters the idea that reason is self-sufficient for worldly matters.
5
Consider the Grace Aspect
Maturidi adds that even if reason were sufficient, prophethood would still be an additional grace (إفضال) from God. This emphasizes that prophethood is both necessary and a gift.
This nuance balances necessity with divine generosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ) ثم نرجع إلى مناظرة من أنكر الرسالة للوجوه التي ذكرت، بعد إقراره بالتوحيد وإيمانه بالأمر والنهي، مع ما فيما ذُكرت من أدلة الأمر والنهي مقرونة بالحاجة إلى الرسالة كفاية لمن تصح نفسه. ثم نقول: يجب القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل في إيجاب الحاجة إليها دينا ودنيا، ثم في إثبات الإفضال من الله إن كان في العقل منه غنى.
We return to the debate with one who denies prophethood for the reasons mentioned, after his affirmation of tawhid and his faith in command and prohibition. With what was mentioned of the proofs of command and prohibition coupled with the need for prophethood, it suffices for one who corrects his soul. Then we say: It is necessary to affirm prophethood by the necessity of reason in affirming the need for it in religion and world, and then in establishing God's grace if reason is sufficient from it.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage begins by situating the current argument within the broader debate: after the opponent has accepted tawhid and the existence of divine command and prohibition, we return to debate the denier of prophethood. The previous proofs have already established the need for prophethood. Then Maturidi states the rational necessity of affirming prophethood: reason itself necessitates the need for prophethood in both religious and worldly affairs. Additionally, if reason were sufficient, the argument would then go on to establish God's grace, but the focus here is on the necessity. The phrase 'in establishing God's grace' suggests that even if reason could know some things, the sending of prophets is an additional grace from God. However, the main point is that reason itself requires prophethood.

Key Terms
رسالة
ضرورة العقل
إيجاب الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason alone is fully sufficient for all guidance, both religious and worldly, making prophethood unnecessary.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason itself recognizes its own limits and requires prophethood. Reason necessitates the need for prophets, and even if it were sufficient, prophethood would still be a grace from God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the basis for affirming prophethood after establishing tawhid?
A) Revelation alone
B) The necessity of reason
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Miracles of prophets

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is not merely a revealed truth; reason itself demonstrates its necessity for complete human guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you relied solely on your own reasoning to navigate a difficult situation, ignoring or neglecting prophetic guidance. What was the outcome? This passage shows that reason itself demands prophethood. In your daily life, what concrete steps can you take to prioritize the prophetic guidance found in the Quran and Sunnah over your own judgments?
?
The passage states that prophethood is needed for both religious and worldly affairs. Identify one specific area of your worldly life—such as your career, finances, or family relationships—where you are most tempted to rely solely on your own intellect. How can you actively seek and apply prophetic guidance in that area starting today?
?
Maturidi says that even if reason were sufficient, prophethood would still be a divine grace. Do you truly feel the sending of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) as an unearned gift, or do you sometimes take it for granted? How does your answer affect your gratitude and your commitment to following his sunnah rigorously?

Human Need for Guidance in Sustenance and Agriculture
Maturidi argues from food and medicine to the need for prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even basic activities like obtaining food and medicine require knowledge beyond unaided reason, pointing to the necessity of a divinely taught guide.

Core Idea
We cannot instinctively know what nourishes or harms us, so someone must teach us.

Maturidi begins his proof by focusing on the most fundamental human needs: food and medicine. He argues that humans cannot inherently distinguish between beneficial and harmful substances, nor can they figure out agriculture or food preparation by reason alone. This establishes the need for a source of knowledge beyond the mind, which is divine teaching through a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sustenance and medicine
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi lists three sequential challenges: identifying harmful vs beneficial substances, the processes of agriculture, and proper preparation of food.
The key phrase is 'ليس في العقول سبيل' (there is no way in minds) repeated for emphasis.
2
Identify the logical flow
The argument moves from the need to know the essence of things, to the inability to manage agriculture, to the necessity of knowing how to prepare food. See how each step points to the same conclusion: we need someone informed by Allah.
3
Reflect on the medical example
The final part about medicine highlights that even with knowledge of foods, we need to know the remedy for harms, which also requires teaching. This reinforces the point that all practical sciences are learned, not invented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأمر الدنيا [جار] فيما به أيضا قوام الدين نحو أن خلق البشر وجعلهم أهل المحنة، وأنبت لهم من الأرض بما أنزل من ماء السماء أغذية لهم وأدوية، ثم أنبت منها الأدواء والسموم القاتلة، وقبح في عقولهم الامتحان بأنفسهم ليعرفوا المؤذى من المغذى، لما لعل [فيه] عطب الممتحن، وليس في العقول سبيل تعرف ذلك. لزم القول بمن يُطْلِعه الله على كل جوهر منها لتحيي بما يأكلون أبدانهم ويقيموا به دينهم. ثم في الابتداء ليس في العقول [سبيل] تعرف الوجوه التي تنبت من الزراعة وما فيها من التدبير. ثم بعد التمام والعلم بجوهر [0] لا بد ممن يعلم كيف يستعمله حتى يصلح للاغتذاء، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك. ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يُدفَع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حدّه، لأنه [إنها يتعلم] ممن يعلم حد ذلك ثم دواءه إن ضره بالقدر الذي به يدفع ضرره، ثم علوم الطب، مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن. ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنّهم، والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد. ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأمل فيها أنها لأي منفعة خلقت، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا، ولا كيف يروّضها؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما هي له، حتى تنقاد وتخضع. ثم في أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم [في] ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب؛ ثم في معرفة طرقها، إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها؛ ثم في تعرّف الألسن التي بها قامت النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد. ثم [في] تعرف الأسماء التي لولا هي ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفي معرفة تربية الصغار؛ ثم في العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور تعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكيفية استعمالها؛ وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين [على] أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول. والله الموفق.
The matter of the world [is related] to that by which also the foundation of religion stands: that He created humans and made them people of trial, and grew for them from the earth by what He sent down from the sky water, nourishments and medicines, then grew from it ailments and lethal poisons, and made ugly in their minds the testing of themselves to know the harmful from the beneficial, because perhaps therein is the destruction of the tester, and in minds there is no way to know that. So it is necessary to affirm one whom God informs about every essence of them so that they may live by what they eat for their bodies and establish their religion. Then in the beginning, in minds there is no way to know the aspects that grow from agriculture and what is in it of management. Then after completion and knowledge of the essence, there must be someone who knows how to use it until it becomes suitable for nourishment, according to the difference of what was made for its improvement. Then He placed in food types of harm that are repelled from the beneficiary if he does not preserve its limit, because he learns from one who knows the limit of that and then its cure if it harms him to the extent that he repels its harm, then the sciences of medicine, with the variation of natural dispositions and the use of lethal poisons, so that they may know the extent of the beneficial that sustains the body. Then in the types of crafts by which the foundation of their clothing and shelter stands, and protection from heat and cold. Then in what was created for them of wild animals that the deliberator does not know for which benefit they were created, nor whether they were created for his benefit or not, nor how to tame them, since the nature of each is aversion to what is for it, until they become submissive and yield. Then in the types of trades by which their religion and world cannot stand except through them. Then in what distributes their needs across the lands, which is not in their nature nor in their minds what guides them or shows them for every need where it is sought; then in knowing its routes, since in reason there is no indication of its location nor of its routes; then in learning the languages by which the soul stands for livelihood and understanding of the Hereafter. Then in learning the names without which no need would be understood nor could anyone know its location. Then in the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and in knowing the upbringing of children; then in the knowledge of managing nourishments that are not attained, then in the manifestation of people learning from each other all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, routes of lands, taming of animals, and how to use them; and all that I mentioned is the clear proof that their foundations are teaching and indication, not extraction by intellects. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof by enumeration of various domains where human reason alone is insufficient and guidance from a divinely informed teacher is necessary. The argument proceeds in stages: First, in the realm of food and medicine, humans cannot know by themselves what is harmful and what is beneficial; they need someone who knows the essences of things. Second, agriculture requires knowledge of the processes that reason cannot attain. Third, even after obtaining food, one must know how to prepare it correctly. Fourth, medicine involves understanding the balance of natures and the use of poisons for cure. Fifth, crafts such as clothing and shelter require learned skills. Sixth, taming wild animals is not instinctive. Seventh, trade and commerce require knowledge of needs and routes. Eighth, languages and names are conventional, not derived from reason. Ninth, reproduction and child-rearing require taught knowledge. Tenth, all these sciences and arts are learned from others, not invented by reason. The conclusion is that the foundations of all these are teaching and divine indication, not rational extraction. Thus, the need for a prophet is proven.

Key Terms
تعليم
إشارة
استخراج العقول
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can humans not identify harmful substances by themselves?
A) They lack intelligence
B) The test might destroy them
C) They are too lazy
D) The poisons are invisible

Key Takeaway
Human reason cannot independently distinguish beneficial from harmful substances; this points to the need for prophetic teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many times today have you eaten or taken medicine without once stopping to consider that you could not have known its benefit without generations of trial—and ultimately without divine guidance? Will you begin your next meal with that awareness and gratitude to the One who informed humanity?
?
You trust a doctor's prescription without hesitation. Why then do you resist trusting the prescriptions of the Prophets, when even your body's health depends on revealed knowledge? What is the disease in your heart that makes you accept worldly experts but question divine guidance?
?
Maturidi says testing oneself with poison leads to destruction. In your spiritual life, have you been testing harmful beliefs or actions—thinking you can handle them—when the Prophet already warned you? Will you repent and return to the One who knows every essence?

Human Dependence in Crafts and Taming Animals
Maturidi argues from crafts and animal taming to the need for prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that crafts for clothing and shelter, as well as the domestication of wild animals, rely on transmitted knowledge beyond unaided reason.

Core Idea
We are taught crafts and animal taming; we do not invent them ourselves.

Moving from basic sustenance, Maturidi now considers other necessities: clothing, shelter, and the use of animals. Humans cannot simply reason out how to weave cloth, build houses, or tame wild beasts. These skills are learned from others, indicating a chain of teaching that ultimately goes back to divine instruction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on crafts and animals
Focus on the Arabic text that lists 'أنواع الحرف' (types of crafts) and 'الدواب الصعبة' (wild animals). Notice the repeated theme of not knowing by reason.
Key phrase: 'ليس يعلم المتأمل فيها' (the deliberator does not know).
2
Analyze the argument for crafts
Crafts are essential for clothing and shelter. Maturidi states they are foundational ('قوام') but reason alone cannot derive them. This implies they must have been taught.
3
Examine the example of wild animals
Wild animals are created with a nature of aversion. Humans cannot figure out their benefit, whether they are useful, or how to tame them without being taught. Taming requires knowledge of their nature and methods.

فأمر الدنيا [جار] فيما به أيضا قوام الدين نحو أن خلق البشر وجعلهم أهل المحنة، وأنبت لهم من الأرض بما أنزل من ماء السماء أغذية لهم وأدوية، ثم أنبت منها الأدواء والسموم القاتلة، وقبح في عقولهم الامتحان بأنفسهم ليعرفوا المؤذى من المغذى، لما لعل [فيه] عطب الممتحن، وليس في العقول سبيل تعرف ذلك. لزم القول بمن يُطْلِعه الله على كل جوهر منها لتحيي بما يأكلون أبدانهم ويقيموا به دينهم. ثم في الابتداء ليس في العقول [سبيل] تعرف الوجوه التي تنبت من الزراعة وما فيها من التدبير. ثم بعد التمام والعلم بجوهر [0] لا بد ممن يعلم كيف يستعمله حتى يصلح للاغتذاء، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك. ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يُدفَع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حدّه، لأنه [إنها يتعلم] ممن يعلم حد ذلك ثم دواءه إن ضره بالقدر الذي به يدفع ضرره، ثم علوم الطب، مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن. ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنّهم، والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد. ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأمل فيها أنها لأي منفعة خلقت، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا، ولا كيف يروّضها؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما هي له، حتى تنقاد وتخضع. ثم في أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم [في] ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب؛ ثم في معرفة طرقها، إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها؛ ثم في تعرّف الألسن التي بها قامت النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد. ثم [في] تعرف الأسماء التي لولا هي ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفي معرفة تربية الصغار؛ ثم في العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور تعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكيفية استعمالها؛ وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين [على] أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول. والله الموفق.
The matter of the world [is related] to that by which also the foundation of religion stands: that He created humans and made them people of trial, and grew for them from the earth by what He sent down from the sky water, nourishments and medicines, then grew from it ailments and lethal poisons, and made ugly in their minds the testing of themselves to know the harmful from the beneficial, because perhaps therein is the destruction of the tester, and in minds there is no way to know that. So it is necessary to affirm one whom God informs about every essence of them so that they may live by what they eat for their bodies and establish their religion. Then in the beginning, in minds there is no way to know the aspects that grow from agriculture and what is in it of management. Then after completion and knowledge of the essence, there must be someone who knows how to use it until it becomes suitable for nourishment, according to the difference of what was made for its improvement. Then He placed in food types of harm that are repelled from the beneficiary if he does not preserve its limit, because he learns from one who knows the limit of that and then its cure if it harms him to the extent that he repels its harm, then the sciences of medicine, with the variation of natural dispositions and the use of lethal poisons, so that they may know the extent of the beneficial that sustains the body. Then in the types of crafts by which the foundation of their clothing and shelter stands, and protection from heat and cold. Then in what was created for them of wild animals that the deliberator does not know for which benefit they were created, nor whether they were created for his benefit or not, nor how to tame them, since the nature of each is aversion to what is for it, until they become submissive and yield. Then in the types of trades by which their religion and world cannot stand except through them. Then in what distributes their needs across the lands, which is not in their nature nor in their minds what guides them or shows them for every need where it is sought; then in knowing its routes, since in reason there is no indication of its location nor of its routes; then in learning the languages by which the soul stands for livelihood and understanding of the Hereafter. Then in learning the names without which no need would be understood nor could anyone know its location. Then in the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and in knowing the upbringing of children; then in the knowledge of managing nourishments that are not attained, then in the manifestation of people learning from each other all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, routes of lands, taming of animals, and how to use them; and all that I mentioned is the clear proof that their foundations are teaching and indication, not extraction by intellects. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof by enumeration of various domains where human reason alone is insufficient and guidance from a divinely informed teacher is necessary. The argument proceeds in stages: First, in the realm of food and medicine, humans cannot know by themselves what is harmful and what is beneficial; they need someone who knows the essences of things. Second, agriculture requires knowledge of the processes that reason cannot attain. Third, even after obtaining food, one must know how to prepare it correctly. Fourth, medicine involves understanding the balance of natures and the use of poisons for cure. Fifth, crafts such as clothing and shelter require learned skills. Sixth, taming wild animals is not instinctive. Seventh, trade and commerce require knowledge of needs and routes. Eighth, languages and names are conventional, not derived from reason. Ninth, reproduction and child-rearing require taught knowledge. Tenth, all these sciences and arts are learned from others, not invented by reason. The conclusion is that the foundations of all these are teaching and divine indication, not rational extraction. Thus, the need for a prophet is proven.

Key Terms
تعليم
إشارة
حرفة
دابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the natural disposition of wild animals according to Maturidi?
A) Friendliness
B) Aversion to what they are for
C) Obedience
D) Indifference

Key Takeaway
Crafts and animal domestication are not derived from reason but are taught, supporting the need for prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own skills—sewing, cooking, using technology—do you acknowledge that you learned them from others, or do you secretly take credit as if you invented them? How does this lesson challenge your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Think of a wild animal you've seen. Without any prior teaching, could you know its purpose or how to tame it? If not, then how can you claim that reason alone suffices for life's deepest questions? What does that reveal about your need for revelation?
?
Imagine a craft you depend on daily—like weaving or building. If you had to figure it out from scratch with no teacher, how long would it take? Now, does your gratitude extend to the One who provided the means of learning through prophets?

The Universality of Teaching: Trade, Language, and Conclusion
Maturidi concludes his proof by listing trade, language, names, child-rearing, and declaring that all knowledge is taught.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that trade, languages, names, and child-rearing all rely on learning from others, culminating in the proof that all human knowledge comes from divine teaching.

Core Idea
All skills and sciences are transmitted; no one invents them from scratch.

Maturidi now expands his proof to encompass trade, the distribution of needs across lands, languages, names, reproduction, and child-rearing. He concludes that every one of these domains is learned from others, not extracted by reason. This comprehensive argument establishes that the ultimate source of all beneficial knowledge is divine teaching through a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the latter part of the passage
From 'أنواع التجارات' to the end. Notice the wide range: trade, locating needs, languages, names, reproduction, and upbringing. The list is meant to be exhaustive.
Key phrase: 'أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول' – their foundations are teaching and indication, not extraction by intellects.
2
Trace the logical conclusion
After listing many examples, Maturidi states that all people learn from each other. Then he delivers the clear proof: the origins are teaching and divine indication.
3
Reflect on the role of the prophet
If all these necessary skills are taught, then the ultimate teacher must be someone whom Allah informs – a prophet. This completes the proof from human dependence.

?

The Universal Practice of Seeking Advice: A Proof for Prophethood
Maturidi argues from the universal practice of seeking advice to the rational necessity of a truthful prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the universal human practice of seeking advice to demonstrate the rational necessity of a truthful prophet.

Core Idea
People's reliance on advisors proves the need for a divine guide.

Maturidi presents an argument grounded in everyday human behavior: when facing important matters, people instinctively seek counsel from those with greater knowledge. This universal practice reveals a fundamental truth about human intellect—it is not self-sufficient. In this lesson, we will examine how Maturidi builds from this observation to conclude that a truthful prophetic advisor is a rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the cascading structure: practice → inference → conclusion → application.
2
Identify the key components
The passage has four main parts: the description of the practice, the inference that intellects are insufficient, the rational conclusion to turn to a truthful advisor, and the application to various fields of knowledge. Find each part in the text.
The word 'فدل' marks the inference, and 'لزم' marks the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi extends the argument to include not only religion but also worldly knowledge and even sorcery. How does this strengthen his case?
Think about the universal scope of the need for transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مع الأمر المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يجربهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم لهم فضل في العلم ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء. فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وأداء حاجاتهم جميعا. [ف] لزم في العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق، وذلك ظن الخلق بأولئك أنه وصل إليهم العلوم على ألسن هؤلاء. فعلى ذلك أمر الدين والدنيا، وعلى ذلك علم السحر. وتعتبر جواهر الأشياء بأنواع المعالجات، وعلوم محاربة أعداء الدين والأموال، كلها مستفادة في الأمر الظاهر من الألسن، وما عنها يوجد. فأول ذلك تعليم يكون من العليم الحكيم.
This, along with the known and existing practice of some turning to others at calamities and what they experience of important matters to seek their opinion and act upon their counsel, because they have superiority in knowledge, then teaching of the arts of etiquette, then each one benefiting from the types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. So that indicates that they did not see in their intellects sufficiency from seeking help and fulfilling all their needs. Therefore, it is rationally necessary to turn to a truthful advisor, and that is the assumption of people about those that knowledge reached them on the tongues of these. So upon that is the matter of religion and the world, and upon that is the knowledge of sorcery. And the essences of things are considered by types of treatments, and the sciences of fighting enemies of religion and wealth, all are acquired in the apparent matter from tongues, and from them they come. So the beginning of that is teaching from the All-Knowing, All-Wise.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi presents another argument: the universal practice of people turning to others for help in important matters, such as calamities, shows that they recognize their own intellectual insufficiency. They seek the counsel of those with superior knowledge, and they learn arts and sciences from books and wise people. This demonstrates that they do not consider their intellects sufficient. Therefore, reason itself necessitates turning to a truthful advisor (ناصح صدوق), i.e., a prophet. People assume that knowledge comes to them through the tongues of these wise individuals. This applies to both religious and worldly matters, as well as to knowledge like sorcery and medicine. All these are acquired through language and transmission. The ultimate source of this teaching is God, the All-Knowing and All-Wise.

Key Terms

ناصح صدوق
تعليم
استفادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that seeking advice is merely a conventional social habit, not something that indicates a rational need for divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this universal practice reveals a genuine recognition of intellectual limitation, making it a rational necessity, not just a convention.

Checkpoint

B) That intellects are insufficient and need a truthful advisor
C) That prophets are unnecessary
D) That books alone are sufficient

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of seeking advice reveals human intellectual insufficiency and rationally necessitates turning to a truthful prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a major life decision, do you genuinely seek guidance from the Quran and Sunnah as your truthful advisor, or do you rely more on your own reasoning and popular opinion? What does your typical decision-making process reveal about your actual belief in the need for prophethood?
?
The passage argues that all knowledge comes through transmission. Are you actively connecting yourself to the ‘tongues’ of the Prophet (ﷺ) by studying authentic sources, or are you content with what you think you already know? If you were to be honest, how much of your religious understanding is from direct learning versus assumption?
?
Maturidi says people turn to advisors because they see their own intellects are insufficient. Do you truly acknowledge your own intellectual insufficiency in matters of faith, or do you subtly assume you can figure out religion on your own? What would change in your life if you fully embraced your need for a divine teacher?

Prophethood

From Limb Flexibility to the Necessity of a Prophet
Maturidi argues from human physical structure to the necessity of a prophet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the unique flexibility of human limbs to argue that humans are created for worship, which logically requires a prophet to explain how each limb is used in worship.

Core Idea
Human limb flexibility reveals a purpose beyond survival: worship, requiring divine guidance.

Maturidi presents a subtle argument from design. He observes that humans can move their limbs in countless ways—grasping, spreading, turning—far beyond what is needed for mere survival. This excess capacity points to a higher purpose: worship. And since worship requires knowledge, there must be a teacher sent by God – a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Maturidi's flow of reasoning.
Pay attention to the contrast between humans and animals.
2
Identify the key observation
Notice that Maturidi highlights the flexibility of joints enabling many actions (grasping, spreading, giving, taking, etc.). Ask why this is significant.
The flexibility allows a wide range of movements beyond basic needs.
3
Understand the inference to worship
Realize that because humans can do more than necessary for utility, they must be created for a higher purpose—worship.
Worship uses all limbs, so a guide is needed.
4
Connect to the necessity of a prophet
Maturidi concludes that since worship requires knowing how each limb is used, an explainer (mubayyin) is necessary—that is a prophet.
This is the core of the argument for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا، إن الله إذ خلق البشر خلقا أمكنه استعمال كل جارحة منه بما جعل من اللين بالمفاصل؛ يقبض بها ويبسط، ويعطى ويأخذ، ويتقلب على مختلف الأحوال، وينتشر في مفترق الأفعال مما لو لم يكن خلقه لاستعمال جميع ذلك في العبادة لجعل فيه وسع العمل والنفع خاصة كالدواب والطيور، فثبت أنه خلق للعبادة، فلا بد من مبين مائيتها في كل جارحة.
And also, God when He created humans as a creation enabling him to use every limb from him by what He made of flexibility in the joints; he grasps with them and spreads, gives and takes, turns over in various states, and spreads out in diverse actions—such that if He had not created him for the use of all that in worship, He would have made in him capacity for work and benefit only, like beasts and birds. So it is established that he was created for worship; therefore there must be an explainer of the manner of that in every limb.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a teleological argument for prophethood based on human physical design. Maturidi observes that humans have flexible joints enabling a wide range of actions—grasping, spreading, giving, taking, changing positions—far beyond what is needed for mere survival or utility. If humans were created only for worldly benefit, like animals, they would have limited capacities. But since they have this extensive flexibility, it proves they are created for worship. Worship is a purposeful activity that requires knowledge of how to perform it with each limb, and since that knowledge is not self-evident, an explainer (مبين) is necessary—namely, a prophet.

Key Terms
خلق
استعمال الجارحة
اللين بالمفاصل
العبادة
مبين

Prophethood

The Need for an Arbitrator in Human Conflict
Maturidi proves prophethood from the prevalence of conflict and lack of an impartial judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof for the necessity of prophets based on the prevalence of human conflict and the absence of an impartial arbiter.

Core Idea
Conflict reveals humanity's need for a divine messenger.

Maturidi presents a compelling argument drawn from everyday human experience: people constantly disagree, each claiming to be right, yet no one among them is accepted as a neutral judge. This impasse leads to corruption and destruction, which reason recognizes as evil. The only solution is to turn to the Creator, who knows what is best for His creation, and who sends messengers to guide them. This passage lays out that logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument from a common observation.
The passage is concise; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the key premise
Find where Maturidi states that conflict is the root of all corruption. Why is that important for his argument?
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the reasoning from conflict → no arbiter among humans → need for divine guidance → necessity of a messenger. See how each step is linked.
Maturidi uses the phrase 'the outcome of intellects has ended at the One who aids them' to show that reason itself points to God.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common mistake that humans can solve their conflicts through reason alone. Why does Maturidi's argument show this is insufficient?
He emphasizes that no human is accepted as a neutral judge, so an external divine source is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك مما توجب ضرورة العقل الحاجة إلى الرسل وجوه. أحدها وجود التنازع الظاهر بين الخلق على ادعاء كل منهم أنه أحق بالحق وأولى بالإصابة، واتفاق أن ليس فيهم من يفزع إليه ليحكم بينهم، ويريهم بما به تتألف قلوبهم وتجتمع كلمتهم. ومعلوم أن التنازع هو أصل كل فساد ومقدمة كل فناء، وذلك كله قبيح في العقول. فقد انتهت عاقبة العقول إلى من يعينها ويردها إلى ما جعلت هي له من الصلاح والمعرفة. ومعلوم أن لا أحد أعلم بذلك ممن خلقها وأنشأها. وفي ذلك لزوم القول برسول يعلم أنه من عنده جاء. وبالله التوفيق.
Then the fundamental principle in that—of what rational necessity imposes the need for messengers—are aspects. One of them is the existence of apparent conflict among creatures, each claiming that he is more entitled to the truth and more likely to be correct, and the agreement that there is none among them to whom one may resort to judge between them, and to show them that by which their hearts are reconciled and their word is united. And it is known that conflict is the root of all corruption and the prelude to all destruction, and all that is ugly in the intellects. So the outcome of intellects has ended at the One who aids them and returns them to what they were created for of rectitude and knowledge. And it is known that no one is more knowledgeable of that than the One who created them and originated them. And in that is the necessity of asserting a messenger who is known that he came from Him. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a social proof for prophethood based on the problem of conflict. Maturidi identifies the fundamental principle that necessitates prophets: the existence of widespread disputes among people, each claiming truth, with no one among them who can be accepted as an impartial judge to reconcile hearts and unify speech. Since conflict is the source of all corruption and destruction, and reason deems it ugly, the intellects must turn to the One who created them and knows best what brings them rectitude. Therefore, a messenger from God is necessary to provide that divine judgment and guidance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can resolve their conflicts through reason alone without divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since no human is accepted as an impartial judge, and conflict leads to corruption which reason deems ugly, reason itself points beyond humanity to the Creator who sends messengers.

?

Scholarly Disparity as a Proof for Prophethood
Maturidi begins an argument from differing scholarly ability (fragment).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi begins to argue that the variation in scholars' insights points to the necessity of prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Scholars differ; a perfect guide is needed.

This lesson examines the opening of a proof from Imam Maturidi for the necessity of prophethood. He observes that scholars vary in their ability to discern what benefits them in religious and worldly affairs. This disparity, he suggests, indicates the need for a divinely guided messenger. However, the argument is cut short—what follows would complete the proof.

Guided Path

The passage is incomplete due to a page break.

3

Reflect on how this fragment fits into broader proofs of prophethood.

Rational Necessity of Gratitude and Prophethood
Maturidi argues from rational obligation of gratitude to necessity of a messenger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the intellect requires a messenger to know how to fully thank the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Gratitude to God cannot be fully known by reason alone; a messenger is needed.

This lesson presents Maturidi's foundational argument for prophethood. He starts from a universal rational principle: the intellect recognizes the goodness of thanking the Benefactor. Then he shows that the fullness of blessings and the proper gratitude for each limb surpasses reason, thus necessitating a divine informer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
2
Identify the rational premise
Notice Maturidi's opening: it is rationally established that knowing and thanking the Benefactor is good, and denial is evil. This is the foundation.
This premise is taken as self-evident in kalam.
3
Examine the two expressions (ibaratayn)
The passage presents two distinct arguments showing the insufficiency of reason. Find them and see how each leads to the need for a messenger.
The first concerns the scale of blessings, the second concerns the specific gratitude of each limb.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both expressions converge: the intellect cannot fully know the gratitude due, so it necessitates an informer (mukhbi) from God.
This is a rational proof for prophethood, not a traditional one.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument shows that prophethood is not arbitrary but rationally required for fulfilling the duty of gratitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يُلزم القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل هو أنه قد ثبت حسن معرفة المنعم والشكر له في العقل، وقبح الجحود له والكفران بنعمته. ثم ما من شيء تقع عليه حاسة من حواسه إلا والله عليه في سلامة حاسته وما أدرك نِعَمَ يعجز عن الإحاطة بها. ثم بعد هذا له عبارتان؛ إحداهما تفاوت استحقاق المنعمين الشكر، وتفاضل أقدار النعم مما لا يبلغ علم أحد نهايتها إلا علم من أنشأها. فعلى هذا لا يبلغ عقل بما به تمام شكرها إلا هو، فيلزم العقل من يخبر عمن منه تلك النعم. والأخرى أن تلك النعم إذ هي تفرقت على الحواس وأصابت كل جارحة منها، فلزم استعمال كل جارحة في شكر ما الله عليها من النعم. مع ما إذا أردت أن تعرف قدرها اعتبر بالمبتلى بالآفة بها؛ لعله يخف عليه بذل الدنيا. ثم كان كل ما بكل جارحة تؤدى من الشكر لا يُعرف بالعقل، فيلزم القول بمخبر يخبر عن الله.
Then from what necessitates the assertion of prophethood by rational necessity is that it has been established in the intellect the goodness of knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him, and the evil of denying Him and ingratitude for His blessing. Then, there is nothing that any of his senses perceives except that the blessing of God upon him is in the soundness of his sense and what he perceives of blessings that he is incapable of encompassing. Then after this, He has two expressions: one is the differentiation of the deserving of the benefactors of thanks, and the disparity of the measures of blessings, the end of which no one's knowledge reaches except the knowledge of the One who originated them. Thus, the intellect cannot attain the full gratitude due to them except through Him, so the intellect necessitates the one who informs about whom those blessings come from. The other is that those blessings, when they are distributed over the senses and affect each limb, then it is necessary to use each limb in gratitude for what God has bestowed upon it of blessings. And when you want to know their measure, consider the one afflicted with a defect in it; perhaps it becomes easy for him to give up the world. Then, all that is performed by each limb of gratitude cannot be known by the intellect, so the assertion of an informer who informs about God is necessitated.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood. It begins by establishing a rational premise: the intellect recognizes the goodness of knowing and thanking the Benefactor (al-mun'im) and the evil of denying Him. Then it observes that every sensory perception involves a divine blessing (ni'mah) that the perceiver cannot fully comprehend. From this, two expressions (ʿibāratān) are derived: (1) The degrees of gratitude owed to different benefactors vary, and the full extent of blessings cannot be known except by their Originator; hence the intellect requires someone to inform it about God's blessings. (2) Blessings affect each limb individually, requiring each limb to express gratitude in a manner not known by intellect alone, thus necessitating a messenger (mukhbi) to inform about God.

Key Terms
المنعم
الشكر
العقل
الجوارح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the intellect alone can fully discharge gratitude without divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the intellect recognizes the obligation but cannot specify the full extent or manner of gratitude, thus requiring a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the intellect necessarily require a messenger?
A) Because gratitude to the Benefactor cannot be fully known or performed by intellect alone.
B) Because prophethood is an arbitrary divine command.
C) Because the intellect is inherently evil and needs correction.
D) Because all religious obligations are purely traditional.

Key Takeaway
The intellect's recognition of gratitude to the Benefactor rationally necessitates a messenger to inform of the full measure and manner of thanks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Pick one limb you used today. What specific gratitude does that limb demand from you that you have not given? Will you commit to a concrete act of gratitude with that limb this week?
?
The passage says the intellect cannot fully know how to thank. Can you recall a time you assumed your gratitude was complete but later realized it fell short? How does that expose your need for divine guidance?
?
You have blessings whose full extent only the Originator knows. Is your current level of gratitude truly satisfying, or do you feel a deficit? What one step will you take to seek the messenger’s guidance on proper thankfulness?

The Proof from Human Design for Worship and Prophethood
Maturidi argues from human anatomy that worship is the purpose of creation, requiring prophetic guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues from the design of the human body that humans are created for worship, and why this establishes the necessity of a prophetic messenger.

Core Idea
The human body's extra capabilities prove we are made for worship, not just survival.

Maturidi presents a teleological argument grounded in human anatomy. He observes that our limbs are far more versatile than what is needed for mere worldly benefit — unlike animals. This extra capacity indicates a higher purpose: worship. But since reason alone cannot specify how each limb should worship, a messenger from God is required to explain it. In this passage, we see the logical link between creation and prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the description of human capabilities: contracting, extending, giving, taking, etc. Then read the translation.
The Arabic uses 'تقبض بها ويبسط' to show range.
2
Identify the key observation
Maturidi points out that if humans were only for worldly work and benefit, they would be like animals. What is the extra capacity he highlights?
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion has two parts: first, that humans are created for worship; second, that an explainer (mubayyin) is needed for the modality of worship. Why is the explainer necessary?
4
Reflect on the proof
This is an argument from design: the specific design of the human body points to a specific purpose (worship). Can you think of other examples of design pointing to purpose?
Optional reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً، إن الله إذ خلق البشر خلقا أمكنه استعمال كل جارحة منه بما جعل من اللين بالمفاصل؛ يقبض بها ويبسط، ويعطى ويأخذ، ويتقلب على مختلف الأحوال، وينتشر في مفترق الأفعال مما لو لم يكن خلقه لاستعمال جميع ذلك في العبادة لجعل فيه وسع العمل والنفع خاصة كالدواب والطيور، فثبت أنه خُلق للعبادة، فلا بد من مبين مائيتها في كل جارحة.
And also, when God created humans as a creation that enabled them to use every limb by what He placed of suppleness in the joints: they contract and extend, give and take, turn in various states, and spread in diverse actions. If He had not created them for the use of all that in worship, He would have made in them merely capacity for work and benefit, like beasts and birds. So it is established that they were created for worship, so an explainer is necessary to specify its mode in every limb.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a teleological proof for prophethood. Maturidi observes that the human body is uniquely designed with flexible joints and diverse capabilities (grasping, giving, turning) far beyond what is needed for mere survival. If the purpose were only worldly work and benefit, humans would have been created like animals. The extra capacity indicates that humans are created for worship (ʿibādah). Since the specific mode of worship for each limb cannot be known by reason alone, a messenger (mubayyin) is necessary to explain it.

الجوارح
البيان
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the human body's capabilities are solely for worldly benefit and no further purpose is needed.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the extra capability beyond survival indicates a purpose of worship, which requires divine guidance.

Checkpoint

A) Humans are more intelligent than animals.
B) Humans are created for worship and need a messenger.
C) Humans should use their limbs for unlimited pleasure.
D) The body is irrelevant to religion.

Key Takeaway
The design of the human body with its flexible and diverse capabilities proves that humans are created for worship, and this necessitates a prophetic messenger to explain the specific modes of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof argues that if you were only for this world, you would be like an animal. Yet, when you live only for worldly gain, are you not reducing yourself to that level? What would it take for you to worship Allah with every limb today, starting with the next action of your hands?

The Social Argument for Prophethood
Maturidi argues that pervasive human conflict necessitates a prophetic arbiter from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the universal reality of human conflict to prove the necessity of prophets as divine arbiters.

Core Idea
Human conflict proves we need a judge from God.

Maturidi's argument from social conflict is a powerful demonstration of prophethood's rational necessity. He observes that human beings inevitably disagree, and no one among them can serve as an impartial judge. This leads to corruption, which the intellect itself recognizes as evil. Yet the intellect, by its own logic, points to the need for a messenger from the Creator, who alone knows the remedy for conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument sink in.
Notice how Maturidi builds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the core premise
Maturidi states that conflict (tanāzuʿ) is apparent among all people, each claiming superiority. No one can arbitrate. This conflict is the root of all corruption.
This is a factual observation of human nature.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
The intellect recognizes that conflict leads to evil. But intellect alone cannot resolve it. So intellect turns to the Creator, who knows the purpose of creation. Therefore, a messenger from God is necessary.
This shows the limitation of unaided reason.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this argument says about the role of prophethood in society. Does it make prophethood a rational necessity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك ـ مما توجب ضرورة العقل الحاجة إلى الرسل ـ وجوه. أحدها وجود التنازع الظاهر بين الخلق على ادعاء كل منهم أنه أحق بالحق وأولى بالإصابة، واتفاق أن ليس فيهم من يُفزَع إليه ليحكم بينهم، ويُريَهم بما به تتألف قلوبهم وتجتمع كلمتهم. ومعلوم أن التنازع هو أصل كل فساد ومقدمة كل فناء، وذلك كله قبيح في العقول. فقد انتهت عاقبة العقول إلى من يعينها ويردها إلى ما جعلت هي له من الصلاح والمعرفة. ومعلوم أن لا أحد أعلم بذلك ممن خلقها وأنشأها. وفي ذلك لزوم القول برسول يُعلم أنه من عنده جاء. وبالله التوفيق.
Then the foundation in this — from what rational necessity makes the need for messengers obligatory — are aspects. One of them is the existence of apparent conflict among creation, each claiming that he is more entitled to truth and more deserving of correctness, and the agreement that there is none among them to whom one may resort to judge between them and show them that by which their hearts become united and their word becomes collected. And it is known that conflict is the root of all corruption and the precursor of every destruction, and all that is evil in intellects. So the final outcome of intellects reaches the One who aids them and returns them to that for which they were created of goodness and knowledge. And it is known that no one is more knowledgeable of that than the One who created and originated them. In that is the necessity of asserting a messenger about whom it is known that he has come from Him. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a social argument for prophethood. Maturidi observes that conflict (tanāzuʿ) is prevalent among humans, with each claiming to be correct. There is no impartial human arbiter to resolve these disputes and unify hearts. Conflict is intrinsically evil and leads to corruption and destruction. The intellect, recognizing its own limits, must turn to the Creator, who alone knows the purpose of creation and the means of goodness. Therefore, a messenger from God is necessary to provide definitive guidance and arbitration.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human intellect alone can resolve all social conflicts without divine guidance.

Key Takeaway
Human conflict and the intellect's inability to resolve it rationally necessitate a messenger from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you still rely on your own reasoning? What step can you take today to submit to divine arbitration in that matter?

?
Maturidi argues that intellect reaches its limit and needs aid. In which area of your life have you reached the end of your own reasoning? Have you truly turned to the messenger's guidance there, or are you still trying to figure it out alone? What would it look like to let God's revelation be the final arbiter in that decision?

Prophethood

The First Proof: Scholars Differ, God Knows More
Maturidi's first proof: gradation of knowledge implies God's exclusive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that gradation of knowledge among scholars points to God's exclusive knowledge, which He conveys through messengers.

Core Idea
Human knowledge is limited; God's knowledge is complete and conveyed through messengers.

Maturidi begins with a simple observation: scholars vary in their knowledge. If humans lack some beneficial knowledge, it is reasonable that God possesses it and sends messengers to share it. This lesson unpacks that first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: observation, consequence, conclusion.
The proof is based on common experience.
2
Identify the premise
Maturidi states that scholars differ in perceiving what benefits them. This is the starting point.
3
Understand the inference
If scholars differ, it is possible that God has something beneficial that no human knows. Therefore, He would send messengers to convey it.
This is a possibility argument, not a necessity yet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و دلیل آخر، إنه معلوم أن العلماء يتفاضلون في إدراك ما به مصالحهم في أمر الدين والدنيا، يكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره. وإذا ثبت ذلك فلا ندفع أن يكون عند الله ما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه، فيوصله إليهم برسله. والله المعين. ودليل آخر، إنه لا يخلو الأمر من أن يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه عقله، أو يلزم على بعض الصدور عما أراه غيره ممن هو أرجح منه عقلاً. فإن كان الحق هو الأول ليجب الجمع بين العقول والقولُ لكل بالإصابة إذا قال بما أراه عقله، وفي ذلك شهادة بإصابة كل ذي دين اعتمد على عقله، وذلك محال لتناقض الآراء والأقوال. وإن كان الوجه الثاني فيصير عقله كرسول يأتيهم من عند الله، فيحتاج ذلك إلى دليل يعلمنا شخصه. ثم لا فصل بين دليل يقوم بصدقه فيما يخبر عن الله أو بإصابة الحق في كل ما ينطق به عن عقله. والله الموفق. فهذا، مع ما يُعلم أن الأشغال وازدحامها على العقول تلبسها، وكذلك الهموم وأنواع ما جبل عليه البشر، وكذلك أنواع الألم وأسباب لا تحصى مما يشغل العقول ويمنعها عن الإحاطة بالحق في كل لطيف وجليل، وكذلك غلبة الشهوات وكثرة الأماني واللذات. فلذلك لا بد من رسول الله ليبينهم ويدلهم عند الاشتباه على الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد بينا بحمد الله حاجة العقول للرسل والقول بهم، وعجز العقول عن الإحاطة بالكل. والأصل في ذلك وجهان؛ أحدهما أن الله تعالى جعل لكل مدرك آلة بها يدرك، ثم كل من هؤلاء يحيط لذاته دون أسباب تتصل به ثم مع ذلك تعترضه آفات يلزمه إنفادها بالمواد له من الأعوان والحفظ له من الأضداد التي هي من الأضداد التي هي أعداء تمنع، ليشهد بذلك بحق الإدراك؛ على العلم بمنع البعد ذلك واللطافة عن العمل حق العمل.
And another proof: It is known that scholars differ in their perception of what is beneficial for them in religious and worldly affairs; one may have something that another does not. If that is established, we do not deny that God may have something for the well-being of His servants that His creation does not have, and He conveys it to them through His messengers. And God is the Helper. Another proof: The matter cannot be other than either one returns to what his intellect calls him to, or he must follow what another, who is more sound in intellect, shows him. If the truth is the first, then it would be necessary to combine all intellects and declare everyone correct when he says what his intellect shows him, and that would be a testimony to the correctness of every adherent of a religion who relies on his intellect, but that is impossible due to the contradiction of opinions and statements. If the second way, then his intellect becomes like a messenger coming to them from God, and that requires a proof to inform us of his person. Then there is no distinction between a proof that establishes his truthfulness in what he reports about God or that he is correct in everything he speaks by his intellect. And God is the Giver of success. This, along with what is known: that preoccupations and their crowding upon intellects confuse them, likewise worries and the types of human nature, likewise types of pain and countless causes that occupy intellects and prevent them from encompassing truth in every subtle and great matter, likewise the dominance of desires and abundance of wishes and pleasures. Therefore, a messenger from God is necessary to make them clear and guide them to truth when in doubt. And there is no power except with God. We have explained, praise be to God, the need of intellects for messengers and the assertion of them, and the inability of intellects to encompass everything. The foundation in that is two aspects: One is that God Almighty has made for every perceiver an instrument by which it perceives, then each of these encompasses by itself without causes connected to it, yet still afflictions confront it that necessitate its depletion by the materials of helpers and preservation from the opposites—which are from the opposites that are enemies that prevent—so that it witnesses by that the right of perception; based on the knowledge that distance, smallness, and transparency prevent the action of true perception.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage contains two short proofs and a concluding section summarizing obstacles. The first proof: Scholars differ in knowledge; since they have partial knowledge, God may have exclusive knowledge of what is beneficial for humanity, which He conveys through messengers. The second proof: People either follow their own intellect or that of a superior person. If everyone followed their own intellect, contradictions would arise, making it impossible for all to be correct. Therefore, one must follow a superior intellect, which then functions like a messenger, requiring a proof to identify him. The conclusion lists obstacles that hinder the intellect: worldly preoccupations, worries, pains, desires, and pleasures. Hence, a messenger is necessary. Finally, two principles are mentioned: (1) Each sense has its own instrument, but even then, it can be hindered by afflictions and enemies; (2) Distance, smallness, and transparency prevent perfect perception, analogously indicating human limitation.

Key Terms
العلماء
التفاضل
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human intellect alone can discover all religious truths.
Correction: Maturidi argues that human knowledge is partial, so God may have truths beyond human reach, necessitating revelation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use as the starting point for this proof?
A) The existence of prophets
B) The difference in knowledge among scholars
C) The human need for religion
D) The perfection of intellect

Key Takeaway
Human knowledge is graded and incomplete; God's knowledge is complete and He may share it through messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision about your religion or worldly life, do you honestly seek guidance from God's revelation, or do you trust your own limited reasoning first? What would it take for you to truly submit to the possibility that God knows what you do not?
?
Think of one area of your life where you are most confident in your own knowledge—maybe a career choice, a relationship, or a belief. Are you genuinely open to the possibility that God has sent guidance through His messengers that contradicts your opinion? How would you respond if it did?
?
The proof begins with scholars differing in knowledge. Consider your own teachers or scholars you follow. Do you assume that one of them has complete knowledge? How does this lesson challenge you to remain humble and aware that only God possesses full understanding of what truly benefits you?

The Second Proof: Contradiction of Intellects Requires a Messenger
Maturidi's second proof: contradiction of intellects leads to need for a messenger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the diversity and contradiction of human intellects necessitate a divinely appointed guide.

Core Idea
If everyone follows their own intellect, contradictions prove not all are right; a superior intellect serves as a messenger.

Maturidi presents a dilemma: either people rely solely on their own intellect, leading to contradictions, or they follow a superior intellect. If the latter, that intellect functions like a messenger and must be identified by a proof. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two options presented.
The passage contains a conditional structure.
2
Analyze the dilemma
First option: everyone follows their own intellect → contradictions → impossible for all to be correct. Second option: follow a superior intellect → that intellect becomes like a messenger.
3
Connect to prophethood
Maturidi concludes that a divinely sent messenger is needed, and his truthfulness must be proven.
This proof establishes necessity, not just possibility.

Obstacles to Intellect and the Two Principles of Perception
Maturidi's summary of intellectual obstacles and the two principles of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various obstacles that prevent human intellect from grasping truth and the two foundational principles that explain these limitations.

Core Idea
Human intellect is hindered by many obstacles; messengers remove these doubts.

Maturidi concludes by listing the internal and external factors that confuse the intellect: preoccupations, desires, pains, etc. He then presents two principles about perception: each sense has its own instrument, yet afflictions and physical factors (distance, smallness) still impede perfect perception. This underscores human limitation and the need for divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the list of obstacles.
The list is cumulative.
2
Understand why obstacles necessitate messengers
Because intellect is so often confused, a clear divine message is needed to clarify truth in times of doubt.
This is the practical conclusion.
3
Study the two principles
The first principle: God gave each sense an instrument. The second: even with instruments, afflictions and physical factors prevent perfect perception.
Analogous to intellectual obstacles.

?

?

?

Prophethood
beginner

Third Proof: Obstacles to Intellect Necessitate a Messenger
Maturidi lists four impediments to intellect and concludes a messenger is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human intellect faces multiple obstacles—occupations, anxieties, pains, and desires—which make a divinely sent messenger necessary for guidance.

Core Idea
Intellect alone cannot overcome its own hindrances; revelation is needed.

Maturidi presents a practical proof for prophethood rooted in human experience. He observes that our minds are constantly occupied, troubled, distracted, and pulled by desires. These internal and external factors prevent us from reaching certainty in subtle and great matters. Therefore, a messenger from Allah is required to clarify the truth when it becomes ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully along with the translation to grasp Maturidi's list of obstacles and his conclusion.
Notice the repetition of 'وَكَذَلِكَ' (and likewise) as he introduces each category.
2
Identify the obstacles
Maturidi lists four categories of obstacles: occupations crowding the mind, anxieties and innate dispositions, pains and countless causes, and the predominance of desires and wishes. Write them down.
These are empirical observations, not theoretical.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that because of these obstacles, there is no alternative to a messenger from Allah who will clarify and guide people when truth is ambiguous.
The messenger compensates for the intellect's limitations.
4
Reflect on the proof
Consider why this empirical argument is strong: it is based on common human experience and shows the practical insufficiency of unaided reason.
This proof complements other rational proofs for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا، مع ما يُعلم أن الأشغال وازدحامها على العقول تلبسها، وكذلك الهموم وأنواع ما جبل عليه البشر ، وكذلك أنواع الألم وأسباب لا تحصى مما يشغل العقول ويمنعها عن الإحاطة بالحق في كل لطيف وجليل، وكذلك غلبة الشهوات وكثرة الأماني واللذات. فلذلك لا بد من رسول الله ليبينهم ويدلهم عند الاشتباه على الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
This, along with what is known that occupations and their crowding upon intellects obscure them, and likewise anxieties and the types [of things] with which humans are disposed, and likewise types of pain and innumerable causes that occupy intellects and prevent them from encompassing the truth in every subtle and great [matter], and likewise the predominance of desires and abundance of wishes and pleasures. Therefore, there is no alternative to a messenger from Allah to clarify for them and guide them to the truth when it is ambiguous. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a third proof based on empirical observation of human limitations. Maturidi catalogs several impediments that prevent the intellect from achieving certainty: worldly occupations and their crowding into the mind, anxieties and innate human dispositions, physical pain and countless other distractions, and the overpowering nature of desires, hopes, and pleasures. These factors collectively prevent the intellect from encompassing the truth in all matters, both subtle and grand. Because of these internal and external obstacles, it becomes necessary for Allah to send messengers who can clearly explain and guide people when truth becomes ambiguous. The proof is inductive, showing the practical insufficiency of unaided reason. The phrase 'There is no power except through Allah' acknowledges divine assistance.

Key Terms
عقل
رسول الله
شهوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi claims the intellect is evil or inherently incapable.
Correction: He does not say intellect is evil; he says it is encumbered by various obstacles, so revelation complements it.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as an obstacle to the intellect?
A) Occupations and their crowding.
B) Anxieties and innate dispositions.
C) The need for physical sleep.
D) Desires and pleasures.

Key Takeaway
The human intellect, despite its capacity, is hindered by occupations, anxieties, pains, and desires, making divine guidance through prophets indispensable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific situation where your desires or worries clouded your judgment about a truth you knew. How did that experience reveal the need for guidance beyond your own intellect? What will you do differently next time?
?
Among the four impediments listed—occupations, anxieties, pains, desires—which one most often prevents you from seeing the truth clearly? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to seek divine guidance in that area instead of relying solely on your reasoning.
?
If you truly believe that your intellect is insufficient without revelation, how does that change the way you approach learning your faith? Name one area of your life where you have been trusting your own opinion over revealed instructions, and commit to one specific act of submission today.
