The Self-Defeating Dilemma of Denying Prophethood
Maturidi refutes an objection by showing a self-defeating dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that denying prophetic reports leads to a self-defeating dilemma.

Core Idea
Absolute rejection of testimony is self-defeating.

Maturidi presents a powerful logical refutation against those who reject the possibility of prophetic reports altogether. He shows that such a stance inevitably traps the objector in a contradiction, either by forcing him to accept some reports (which he cannot consistently do) or by invalidating his own claim. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that it is incomplete. Understand the context: Maturidi is responding to an objection about the signs of prophethood.
The text is cut off at the end, but the logical argument is still clear.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two possibilities Maturidi presents: either the objector accepts one truthful report (which would contradict his denial) or he rejects all reports (which invalidates his own statement).
This is a classic self-refutation argument.
3
Reflect on the force of the argument
Consider why this is a strong refutation. The objector cannot consistently deny all reports because his own claim is itself a report.
The argument does not require accepting all reports, only that wholesale rejection is logically untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر في الآيات ، فإن لكل من ذلك علامةً تُعلم، وآية تُظهر [ماهيته] . مع ما كانت المعارضة فاسدة، لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون يصدق أحدا في الخبر، فيكون ذلك السؤال عليه ، أو لا يقر بشيء ألبتة، وفي ذلك سقوط خبره هذا عن نفسه. مع ما كانت
And what was mentioned in the verses, for each of that has a sign by which it is known, and a verse that manifests its [essence]. Along with that, the objection is invalid, because it is free from either affirming someone's truthfulness in the report, and that would be a question against him, or not acknowledging anything at all, and in that is the invalidation of his own report about himself. Along with what was...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This fragment appears to be a refutation of an opponent who denies the need for messengers or denies the validity of prophetic signs. Maturidi argues that such denial leads to a dilemma: either the opponent accepts at least one truthful report (which would then be used against him, as he must explain why he accepts that report but not others), or he rejects all reports entirely—in which case his own statement (the report) about his position is also invalidated. The argument is a classic self-refutation. The phrase 'along with what was' suggests the argument continues but is cut off. Despite its incompleteness, the logical thrust is clear: absolute rejection of testimony is self-defeating.

Key Terms
آيات
خبر
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi's argument forces one to accept all reports without discrimination.
Correction: The argument only shows that wholesale rejection of testimony is self-defeating; it does not require accepting every report, but rather acknowledges that some reports must be reliable for discourse to be meaningful.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Maturidi present to the objector who denies prophetic reports?
A) Either accept all reports or accept none, but both are impossible.
B) Either accept one report (contradicting oneself) or reject all reports (making his own claim invalid).
C) Either believe in God or believe in nothing.
D) Either follow reason or follow revelation.

Key Takeaway
Denying all prophetic reports is self-refuting because it undermines the very basis of testimony, including the objector's own claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a claim, do you ever stop to consider whether your own position might be self-refuting? Think of a belief you hold that, if applied consistently, would undermine itself. What would it cost you to bring logical consistency to your beliefs?
?
The objection to prophethood fails because it relies on testimony while denying it. Where in your daily life do you rely on testimony (e.g., news, advice) yet dismiss other testimonies without reason? How can you be more consistent in your trust?
?
This argument shows that absolute denial leads to logical collapse. Is there an area in your spiritual life where you have adopted a posture of complete rejection (even unconsciously)? What would it mean to open yourself to evidence, even if it challenges your comfort?

The Self-Refuting Denial: Maturidi's Refutation of a Prophethood Denier
Maturidi refutes a denier's objection about reports and miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Maturidi uses to refute a denier of prophethood and how accepting some reports undermines wholesale rejection of prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Denying all reports is self-refuting, and accepting some reports undermines denial of prophecy.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses an unnamed opponent who denied prophethood by questioning the reliability of reports and miracles. The Imam presents a devastating logical dilemma: the denier either accepts some reports (which then turns against him) or rejects all reports (which refutes his own statement). Additionally, he argues that prophetic miracles are more evident through direct observation, and that reports from non-prophetic eras actually support the necessity of accepting reports, thus further entrenching the case for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: the denier either accepts some reports or rejects all. Both options lead to contradiction. Identify this dilemma in the text.
Look for the phrase 'he either accepts someone's report... or he does not affirm anything at all'.
2
Role of Observational Knowledge
Maturidi compares prophetic miracles to other observed phenomena, arguing that miracles are more evident. Notice how he uses the denier's acceptance of observational knowledge against him.
The text says: 'the comparisons... by way of direct observation are more evident than what he said'.
3
Implication of Non-Prophetic Reports
The denier's mention of reports from outside the prophetic era is turned against him: accepting those reports commits him to accepting reports in general, which includes prophetic reports.
Read the final sentence: 'what he mentioned from other than the era of the messengers... is speech about accepting reports...'.
4
Read and Reflect
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Then read the commentary to see how these points unfold logically.
Notice how Maturidi builds his case step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د) وما ذكر في الآيات، فإن لكل من ذلك علامة تُعلم، وآية تُظهر [ماهيته]. مع ما كانت المعارضة فاسدة، لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون يصدق أحدا في الخبر، فيكون ذلك السؤال عليه، أو لا يقر بشيء ألبتة، وفي ذلك سقوط خبره هذا عن نفسه. مع ما كانت المقابلات بالخروج على غير الحقائق من طريق العيان أظهر مما قال، ثم لم يجب به نفي علم العيان، [ف]كيف وجب ما ذكر؟ وما ذكر من غير عصر الرسل فذلك كلام في قبول الأخبار يلزمه من وجه يُضطر إليه، فيُبطل سعيه.
D) And what was mentioned in the verses—for each of that there is a sign by which it is known, and a miracle that manifests its essence. Moreover, the opposition is invalid, because he either accepts someone's report, and then that [acceptance] becomes an argument against him, or he does not affirm anything at all, and in that case his own report is self-refuting. Moreover, the comparisons that deviate from truth by way of direct observation are more evident than what he said; then if he does not accept the negation of observational knowledge, how can what he mentioned be necessitated? And what he mentioned from other than the era of the messengers—that is speech about accepting reports that necessarily entails from a direction he is compelled to, so his effort is nullified.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's refutation of an unnamed denier of prophethood. The denier had apparently objected based on inconsistencies in reports or miracles. Maturidi argues that the denier's opposition is invalid (fāsida) because he faces a dilemma: either he accepts some reports (akbbār) from others, and then that very acceptance can be turned against him (since he accepts reports but denies prophetic reports), or he rejects all reports entirely, in which case his own statement (being a report) is self-refuting. Furthermore, when comparing prophetic miracles with other phenomena, the miracles are more evident through direct observation than the denier's claims. If he cannot deny observational knowledge, then his objection fails. Finally, the denier's mention of reports from non-prophetic eras—this actually proves the necessity of accepting reports, which supports prophethood. Thus his entire argument collapses.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-muʿāraḍa)
الخبر (al-khabar)
العيان (al-ʿiyān)
الرسل (al-rusul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting all reports is a consistent and reasonable position for a denier of prophethood.
Correction: Maturidi shows that rejecting all reports is self-refuting because the denier's own statement is a report, and accepting any report (even his own) opens the door to accepting prophetic reports.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma Maturidi presents to the denier of prophethood?
A) He must either believe in all prophets or none
B) He either accepts some reports (which undermines his rejection) or rejects all reports (which self-refutes)
C) He must choose between reason and revelation
D) He must admit that miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation shows that any denial of prophethood based on reports is logically incoherent because it either accepts reports (undermining the denial) or rejects all reports (self-refuting).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have dismissed a religious claim in the past, did you apply the same burden of proof to claims you accept about everyday matters? How does Maturidi's dilemma expose a double standard in your reasoning?
?
If you were to reject all testimony—including that of historians, scientists, and eyewitnesses—would your own worldview collapse? Are you willing to examine where you selectively accept reports?
?
Maturidi says the opponent's effort is nullified. Is there an area of your life where you are fighting against clear evidence because it is uncomfortable? What would it cost you to submit to the truth?

Prophethood as Divine Favor: Beyond Reason's Sufficiency
Maturidi argues that sending messengers is a divine favor, even if intellect alone suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Maturidi, sending messengers is an act of divine grace (ifḍāl) even if human reason could suffice, and that denying this favor reveals ignorance.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a gratuitous favor that eases the path, not a rational necessity.

Maturidi completes his discussion on prophethood by addressing a key objection: if reason is sufficient for knowing God, why send messengers? He argues that even if reason could suffice, sending messengers is still permissible and even a great favor from Allah. This lesson explores his reasoning, showing how divine benevolence goes beyond mere sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp Maturidi's overall argument before analyzing details.
Notice the conditional opening: 'ثم لو كان في العقل الغنى عنه الجاز...'
2
Identify the conditional structure
Maturidi starts with a hypothetical: even if intellect were self-sufficient, sending messengers is permissible (jā'iz) as a favor. This sets up his core point.
He uses 'الإفضال' (ifḍāl) to emphasize gratuitous favor.
3
List the examples of gratuitous favors
The passage enumerates multiple favors from Allah that go beyond strict necessity: ears, eyes, paired limbs, abundant evidences for tawḥīd, and fruits.
Each is introduced as something given in abundance, even if a little would suffice.
4
Reflect on the conclusion: ingratitude shows foolishness
Maturidi concludes that counting the sending of messengers as a burden rather than a favor indicates foolishness (ḥumq) and ignorance (jahl). Consider why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لو كان في العقل الغنى عنه الجاز إرسال الرسل من طريق الإفضال، إذ الله موصوف معروف بالاحسان، فيه تتقلب عباده، وما من نعمة الله تعالى إلا والله تعالى على عباده فضل زينهم وجمالهم نحو الأذنين والعينين، وكل ذي عدد في الجسد، ثم في كثرة النعم، ثم في كثرة ما أنشأ من دلائل التوحيد والرسالة، وإن كان بدون ذلك كفاية، ثم بكثرة الفواكه والملاذ، وإن كان القليل من ذلك كافيا. وبعد، لو كان بالعقل كفاية فهو يسده الحد في ذلك؛ والتعاون بأنواع استشارة أهل النظر فيما خص الله لهم وأزاح عنهم الإشكال، ثم الاجتهاد الوافر له يبذل فيه كل مجهود. فكان في إرسال الرسل تيسير عليهم وتخفيف، وذلك من عظيم المنن، فكفران مثله يدل على حمق الرجل وجهله بالمنن حتى عدها بلاء مع ما للعقول أشغال وللنفوس أهواء تستر.
Then even if the intellect were self-sufficient for it, it would be permissible to send messengers by way of favor (ifḍāl), for Allah is described and known by beneficence (iḥsān); His servants turn about in it. And there is no blessing from Allah except that Allah upon His servants is a favor—their adornment and beauty such as the ears and eyes, and every paired thing in the body, then in the abundance of blessings, then in the abundance of what He has created of evidences of tawḥīd and prophethood, even if without that there would be sufficiency; then in the abundance of fruits and pleasures, even if a little of that would suffice. Moreover, if the intellect were sufficient, it would reach its limit in that. And cooperation through various types of consultation of the people of insight (ahl al-naẓar) regarding what Allah has specifically granted them and removed of difficulty, then abundant effort (ijtihād) in which every exertion is expended. Thus, in sending messengers there is facilitation and alleviation for them, and that is among the great favors. So ingratitude for this indicates the foolishness of the man and his ignorance of favors, even counting them as affliction, despite the preoccupations of minds and the concealment of souls' whims.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by demonstrating that prophethood is a divine favor (ifḍāl, iḥsān) even if reason could theoretically suffice without it. Maturidi argues that it is permissible (jāʾiz) for Allah to send messengers as an act of grace. He describes Allah as characterized by beneficence, and all blessings—from the creation of ears and eyes to abundant fruits—are favors beyond what is strictly necessary. Similarly, the evidences for tawḥīd and prophethood are abundant. Even if intellect could suffice, it reaches its limit (ḥadd), and cooperation and consultation among scholars (ahl al-naẓar) and extensive ijtihād are still needed. Messengers bring ease (taysīr) and relief (takhfīf), which are great favors. Denying this favor and counting it as a burden reveals the foolishness (ḥumq) and ignorance (jahl) of the denier, especially given that minds are often preoccupied and souls conceal their whims.

Key Terms
الإفضال (al-ifḍāl)
الاحسان (al-iḥsān)
التيسير (al-taysīr)
المنن (al-minan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reason is enough, prophets are unnecessary and sending them is pointless.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophethood is a gratuitous favor that eases the path, not a necessity; it is permissible and beneficial even if reason could suffice.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does ingratitude for the sending of messengers indicate foolishness?
A) Because messengers bring new laws not known to reason
B) Because it is a denial of divine favors that ease understanding
C) Because reason alone cannot know God
D) Because all blessings are necessary, not gratuitous

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that prophethood is a divine favor that goes beyond what reason requires, and ingratitude for this favor indicates ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat the guidance of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as a burden rather than a divine favor that eases your path? What would change if you truly saw it as a gift?
?
Maturidi says that ingratitude for the favor of prophethood indicates foolishness and ignorance. In what subtle ways might you be ungrateful for the ease that revelation brings, preferring your own intellect or whims over prophetic guidance?
?
Given that Allah grants abundant blessings beyond necessity, including your own reasoning ability and the prophets, what specific favor are you neglecting to thank Him for today, and what concrete step will you take to show gratitude?

Prophethood

Messengers as Divine Favor and Aid to Intellect
Maturidi argues for messengers as divine favor and aid against desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends the sending of messengers as an additional divine favor and necessary aid against the forces of desire, even assuming intellect is sufficient.

Core Idea
Even a sufficient intellect needs messengers—against the overwhelming desires that screen it.

Maturidi here takes a striking approach: he does not argue that intellect is insufficient. Instead, he argues that even if intellect were enough, God's abundant favor and the reality of human desire make messengers an essential aid. This passage reframes prophethood as both a luxury of divine generosity and a necessity for human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythmic argument come through.
Notice the repeated structure 'then... then...' (ثم).
2
Identify the key premise
Maturidi starts with 'even if intellect were independent'—he is not arguing against intellect but adding to it. Find where he says messengers are permissible by way of favor (الإفضال).
3
Trace the two lines of argument
First, the abundance of God's blessings beyond sufficiency shows His favor. Second, desire has helpers, so intellect needs helpers too. Messengers are those helpers.
Look for the phrase 'فكيف ينكر جعل أعوان للعقول؟'.
4
Understand the practical role of messengers
Messengers make the unseen (reward, punishment) like direct observation, easing the difficult command to abandon lusts. This shows why they are needed despite intellect.
The conclusion: 'ليصير ذلك بحق العيان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لو كان في العقل الغنى عنه الجاز إرسال الرسل من طريق الإفضال، إذ الله موصوف معروف بالإحسان، فيه تتقلب عباده، وما من نعمة الله تعالى إلا والله تعالى على عباده فضل زينهم وجمالهم نحو الأذنين والعينين، وكل ذي عدد في الجسد، ثم في كثرة النعم، ثم في كثرة ما أنشأ من دلائل التوحيد والرسالة، وإن كان بدون ذلك كفاية، ثم بكثرة الفواكه والملاذ، وإن كان القليل من ذلك كافيا. وبعد، لو كان بالعقل كفاية فهو يسده الحد في ذلك؛ والتعاون بأنواع استشارة أهل النظر فيما خص الله لهم وأزاح عنهم الإشكال، ثم الاجتهاد الوافر له يبذل فيه كل مجهود. فكان في إرسال الرسل تيسير عليهم وتخفيف، وذلك من عظيم المنن، فكفران مثله يدل على حمق الرجل وجهله بالمنن حتى عدها بلاء مع ما للعقول أشغال وللأنفس أهواء تستر العقول، فإرسال الرسل معونة لهم وإرشاد، وذلك هو الذي جبلت العقول على حبّه. مع ما فيه تذكير وتنبيه وتحذير لوجه التقصير، فيكون ذلك مما يحث على النظر ويدعو إلى الفكر واستعمال العقول، وذلك معروف في جميع أمور الدنيا وسياسات الملك. مع ما جعل الهوى منازعا له، وقد جعل للهوى أعوان من الأماني والشهوات وشياطين مزينة لها، فكيف ينكر جعل أعوان للعقول؟ أحقهم بذلك الرسل. وبعد، فإن جميع نوازع الهوى شاهدة حسية، وجميع أسباب عمل الحق غائبة؛ إذ فعل المذكر هو ذكر الثواب والعقاب والأمر بترك الشهوات والملاذ. وذلك أمر عسير على الطبع والهوى، فيحتاج في ذلك إلى الاستعانة برؤية من تُذكر رؤيتهم المعاد، ويخبرون عن المنقلب بما فيه من اليسر والعسر، ليصير ذلك بحق العيان فيسهل على الطبع سهولة ما يوافق الطبع. والله الموفق.
Then if the intellect were independent of it, it would be permissible to send messengers by way of favor, for God is described and known by benevolence, in which His servants are immersed, and there is no blessing from God Almighty except that God Almighty bestows upon His servants favor: their adornment and beauty, such as the ears and eyes, and every numeral in the body, then in the abundance of blessings, then in the abundance of what He created of proofs of tawhid and prophethood, even if without that there would be sufficiency; then in the abundance of fruits and delights, even if little of that suffices. Moreover, even if the intellect were sufficient, it would suffice only the limit in that; and cooperation by types of consultation of the people of reflection in what God has specified for them and removed from them difficulty, then abundant striving in which one expends all effort. So in sending messengers there is facilitation for them and alleviation, and that is among the great favors; so ingratitude for such indicates the man's foolishness and ignorance of favors, even counting them as affliction, despite the occupations of intellects and the desires of souls that screen the intellects. So sending messengers is aid for them and guidance, and that is what intellects are created to love, along with what it contains of reminder, warning, and caution regarding the aspect of shortcoming, so that it becomes something that urges reflection and calls to thought and use of intellects, and that is well-known in all matters of the world and the policies of kings. Along with the fact that He made desire a contender against it, and He has appointed for desire helpers from wishes, lusts, and devils that embellish them, so how can one deny the appointment of helpers for intellects? The most deserving of that are the messengers. Moreover, all impulses of desire are witnessed sensibly, and all causes of acting rightly are absent; since the action of the reminder is the mention of reward and punishment and the command to abandon lusts and pleasures. And that is a difficult matter upon nature and desire, so one needs in that the assistance of seeing those whose sight reminds of the return, and they inform about the destination with what it contains of ease and hardship, so that becomes like direct observation, and thus becomes easy for nature with the ease of what accords with nature. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of sending messengers even if intellect were sufficient, based on God's abundant favor (الإفضال). Maturidi argues that even if intellect were enough, God still sends messengers as an extra favor, because God is known for His benevolence and pours blessings upon servants beyond mere sufficiency. He lists abundant blessings: physical features, proofs of tawhid and prophethood, fruits and delights—all more than necessary. So too, even if intellect sufficed, it only reaches a certain limit; cooperation through consultation and effort has limits, but messengers provide facilitation (تيسير) and alleviation (تخفيف). Rejecting such a favor shows foolishness and ignorance, especially when souls' desires screen intellects. Messengers aid and guide intellects, which are created to love this. Moreover, desire (الهوى) has helpers—wishes, lusts, devils—so why deny helpers for intellect? Messengers are most deserving. All impulses of desire are sensibly present, while causes of right action are unseen; the reminder of reward, punishment, and abandoning lusts is difficult by nature. So one needs to see those whose sight reminds of the hereafter and informs about the afterlife, making the unseen like direct observation, thus easing the burden. The passage concludes with 'والله الموفق'.

Key Terms
الإفضال
الإحسان
المنن
التيسير
التخفيف
الهوى
الشهوات
المعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if intellect can know God and divine truths, there is no need for messengers.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even if intellect sufficed for knowledge, messengers are needed as an additional favor from God and as an aid against the overwhelming helpers of desire; they provide facilitation and make the unseen tangible.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that sending messengers is justified even if intellect were sufficient?
A) Because intellect is inherently flawed.
B) Because God always gives more than what suffices, and messengers facilitate and aid the intellect against desire.
C) Because messengers are needed to create a political system.
D) Because intellect cannot know God at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends prophethood as both an expression of God's overflowing favor and a necessary aid to intellect overwhelmed by desire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life have you counted divine favor as affliction? When has the reminder of a prophet or a believer made you uncomfortable because it demanded you leave a pleasure? What does that reveal about the state of your intellect before your desire?
?
Maturidi says all impulses of desire are present before you, but the realities of the afterlife are absent. How have you allowed the sight of those who remind you of the return—through their words or their very presence—to make the unseen as if you see it? Or have you neglected this aid entirely?
?
If the most deserving helpers for your intellect are the messengers, then whose guidance—among the prophets or their true followers—do you need to invite into your heart today to fight a specific desire that is obscuring your reason? Will you seek that reminder now, or will you let desire screen it again?

Prophethood
beginner
4 min
Historical Proof of Prophethood from Messengers' Victory
Maturidi presents a historical proof: messengers' clear proofs and success despite opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues for the truth of prophethood by pointing to the historical fact that despite intense opposition, messengers ultimately prevailed, compelling even non-believers to acknowledge their authority.

Core Idea
The victory of messengers despite all opposition proves their prophethood.

Maturidi presents a unique historical argument: the messengers came with clear proofs, faced relentless hostility and slander, yet ultimately triumphed. This observable pattern of success, despite the greatest efforts to suppress them, is itself a sign of divine backing. In this lesson, we will examine how this historical evidence serves as a proof for prophethood, and how it connects to the necessity of divine law for society.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi structures the argument: he states the principle, describes the opposition, and then their failure.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the specific words for 'evidences and proofs', 'obstinate', 'tricks', 'sorcery', 'appearance and victory'. Reflect on how these terms build the proof.
3
Understand the historical proof
Grasp that the proof is not a miracle but the observable fact that despite all efforts, messengers won. This is an inductive, historical argument.
4
Connect to the societal analogy
Maturidi extends the argument: just as a society needs a law and foundation, humanity needs divine guidance, which is provided by messengers.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' reminds us that all power is from God, acknowledging that the success is ultimately divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوع آخر من الأصل في ذلك وجود الرسل بما معهم من الأدلة والبرهان مما يعلم جميع دليل يحقق تكذيبه أو يزيل عن نفسه صفة المتعنتين، أحد منهم منكري الرسل أن ليس مع مع كثرة حيلهم في مقابلات أدلتهم، وطعنهم مرة بالسحر وبوجوه أخرى. ثم مع بذلهم مجهودهم من دنياهم ومُهجهم في إطفاء نورهم فلم يروا غير الظهور والغلبة؛ حتى أحوج الله جميع الأنام إلى الذين يؤمنون بالرسل على تعرفهم بما علموا في الجملة أن لهم في أمورهم غنى، رجاءَ أن يصلح أمرهم وتتفق كلمتهم. وعلى ذلك سياسات ملوك الدنيا. ثم لا يقوم رعية لا تُجعل فيهم شريعةٌ يُلزمون القيام بها وأساس يبنون عليه. ولا بد لأمثال ذلك من تدبير ممن يعلم أنه إذا خلقهم جعل لهم وجها يصلحون عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And another type of the principle in that is the existence of messengers with what they have of evidences and proofs, so that every proof knows that it verifies his denial or removes from himself the attribute of the obstinate. One of them [is] that the deniers of messengers [find] that despite the abundance of their tricks in confronting their evidences, and their defaming them sometimes as sorcery and other forms, then despite their expending their utmost effort from their worldly goods and their lives in extinguishing their light, they saw nothing but appearance and victory; until God compelled all people to those who believe in the messengers, according to their knowledge in general that they have independence in their affairs, hoping that their affairs become right and their word becomes unified. And upon that are the policies of the kings of the world. Moreover, no community stands without a law (shari'a) imposed upon them to uphold and a foundation upon which they build. And there is no alternative for such [a thing] except a management from one who knows that when He created them, He made for them a way upon which they may become righteous. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents another type of proof for prophethood (نوع آخر من الأصل) based on the observable historical reality of messengers. Maturidi states that the existence of messengers with clear evidences and proofs (الأدلة والبرهان) is such that every proof confirms the truth of the messenger or removes the excuse of obstinacy. Specifically, the deniers of messengers, despite their numerous tricks, defamation (calling them sorcerers, etc.), and expending their utmost effort and lives to extinguish the messengers' light, saw nothing but the messengers' appearance and victory (الظهور والغلبة). This overwhelming success forced even those who did not believe to acknowledge, in general, that the messengers had a certain independence and authority, hoping for social harmony. Maturidi then draws an analogy with worldly politics: no community can function without a shari'a (law) and a foundation to build upon. Such order requires management from one who knows that in creating humans, God provided a way for their righteousness. The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (And there is no power except with God).

Key Terms
الأدلة
البرهان
المتعنتين
السحر
الظهور
الغلبة
الشريعة
الأساس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prevalence of denial and hostility disproves prophethood.
Correction: Maturidi shows that despite all opposition, messengers always prevail, proving their truth.

Checkpoint
What is the 'other type of proof' that Maturidi presents in this passage?
A) The miracles of the prophets.
B) The historical fact that despite intense opposition, messengers ultimately prevailed and their message spread.
C) The logical necessity of a lawgiver.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The historical success of messengers despite overwhelming opposition is a decisive sign of their prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that despite all opposition, the messengers' light could not be extinguished. In what area of your life are you still holding back from trusting that God's truth will prevail? What would change if you fully believed that His proof is sufficient?
?
The passage says that even those who did not believe were compelled to acknowledge the messengers' authority. Is there a truth about God or His prophets that you intellectually accept but have not allowed to transform your actions? What is holding you back from letting that truth govern your decisions?
?
Maturidi argues that every community needs a law from God. Are you living as though God's shari'a is the foundation of your life, or are you building on something else? What foundation are you relying on, and what would it cost you to shift to the divine foundation?

Messengers as Aids to Intellects
Maturidi explains that messengers aid intellects against desires by reminding of reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that messengers are necessary aids to intellects, helping them overcome the opposition of desires by making the unseen realities of the afterlife tangible.

Core Idea
Messengers are the intellect's essential helpers against the army of desires.

Maturidi continues his proof of prophethood by addressing a subtle objection: if intellect is sufficient, why send messengers? He points out that desires have many helpers (passions, devils) that cloud reason, so intellect needs its own helpers—messengers—who remind us of the unseen consequences of our actions. This passage deepens the rational argument for revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from the benefits of messengers to the contrast with desires.
The passage is a continuous argument; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the two sides of the helpers analogy
Maturidi compares helpers of intellect (messengers) to helpers of desire (wishes, passions, devils). Find these in the text.
Desire's helpers are described as 'wishes, passions, and devils that embellish them'.
3
Understand the visible vs hidden distinction
Desire's impulses are visible and immediate; right action's causes are hidden. Messengers make the hidden (afterlife) visible through reminders.
The difficulty of obedience is eased when the unseen becomes as if witnessed.
4
Reflect on the necessity of messengers
Ask yourself: why can't intellects get there alone? The passage shows the struggle against visible desires and the need for tangible reminders.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقول، فإرسال الرسل معونة لهم وإرشاد، وذلك هو الذي جبلت العقول على حبّه . مع ما فيه تذكير وتنبيه وتحذير لوجه التقصير، فيكون ذلك مما يحث على النظر ويدعو إلى الفكر واستعمال العقول، وذلك معروف في جميع أمور الدنيا وسياسات الملك . مع ما جعل الهوى منازعا له، وقد جعل للهوى أعوان من الأماني والشهوات وشياطين مزينة لها، فكيف ينكر جعل أعوان للعقول ؟ أحقهم بذلك الرسل. وبعد، فإن جميع نوازع الهوى شاهدة حسية، وجميع أسباب عمل الحق غائبة؛ إذ فعل المذكر هو ذكر الثواب والعقاب والأمر بترك الشهوات والملاذ. وذلك أمر عسير على الطبع والهوى، فيحتاج في ذلك إلى الاستعانة برؤية من تُذكر رؤيتهم المعاد، ويخبرون عن المنقلب بما فيه من اليسر والعسر ، ليصير ذلك بحق العيان فيسهل على الطبع سهولة ما يوافق الطبع. والله الموفق.
The intellects: sending messengers is an aid and guidance for them, and that is what intellects are naturally disposed to love. Along with what it contains of reminder, warning, and caution against the aspect of falling short. So that becomes something that encourages reflection, invites thought, and the use of intellects. That is known in all worldly affairs and policies of kings. Along with what desire has been made as an opponent to it, and desire has been given helpers from wishes, passions, and devils that embellish them. So how can one deny making helpers for the intellects? The most deserving of that are the messengers. Moreover, all impulses of desire are sensibly witnessed, while all causes of acting right are hidden; for the action of the reminder is the mention of reward and punishment and the command to abandon passions and pleasures. That is difficult for nature and desire, so there is need in that to seek help from seeing those whose sight reminds of the return (Afterlife), and they inform about the final outcome with what it contains of ease and hardship, so that becomes as if witnessed, making it easy for nature with the ease of what agrees with nature. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage continues the argument for the necessity of messengers. Maturidi explains that messengers are sent as aids and guidance for intellects, because intellects are naturally inclined to love truth but are hindered by desires and worldly distractions. The messengers provide reminders, warnings, and exhortations that stimulate reflection and proper use of reason. Just as kings have policies and worldly affairs require helpers, so too do intellects need external support against the forces of desire—which have their own helpers: false hopes, passions, and embellishing devils. If desire has armies, why should intellect not have its own helpers? The most worthy for that role are the messengers. Maturidi then contrasts the visible nature of desires (sensual and immediate) with the hidden nature of the causes of right action (which require faith and knowledge of the afterlife). The work of the reminder— mentioning reward and punishment and commanding abandonment of pleasures—is hard on human nature and desire. Therefore, people need to see individuals who remind them of the Day of Judgment and inform them of the consequences in the Hereafter. This makes the unseen tangible and easy to follow, just as if it were witnessed. Maturidi concludes with "And Allah is the granter of success."

Key Terms
الرسل
العقول
الهوى
الشهوات
الثواب والعقاب
المعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellect alone is enough to guide humans to truth and right action.
Correction: Maturidi argues that while intellect is noble, it faces strong opposition from desires which have many helpers; thus intellect requires external aids—messengers—who provide reminders of the afterlife to overcome the difficulty of acting right.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do intellects need the help of messengers?
A) Because intellects are naturally weak and cannot know anything.
B) Because desires have many helpers that oppose reason, and messengers provide reminders and warnings.
C) Because messengers force people to obey against their will.
D) Because intellects are created evil and need correction.

Key Takeaway
Messengers are necessary because they make the hidden realities of the afterlife perceptible, helping intellects overcome the visible temptations of desire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In moments of temptation, do you actively remind yourself of the reward and punishment of the afterlife, or do you rely solely on your own reasoning? What specific reminder from this lesson can you use today?
?
Desire has many helpers—wishes, passions, and even devils that embellish sin. Who or what are the 'helpers' for your intellect? Are you seeking out reminders (like this lesson) or neglecting them?
?
The passage says messengers make the unseen afterlife as if witnessed so that obedience becomes easy. What is one hidden reality of the afterlife that you struggle to feel real? How can you practically bring it to mind today through the teachings of the messengers?

Refuting al-Warraq's Denial of Prophetic Miracles
Maturidi refutes al-Warraq's claim that prophets cannot perform true miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key arguments Maturidi uses to refute the claim that prophetic miracles are mere tricks.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles are genuine divine signs, not clever tricks.

This lesson presents a critical exchange from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. Al-Warraq, a skeptic, argues that prophets could not have known all natural powers, so their 'miracles' might be ordinary deceptions. Maturidi responds with a rhetorical counter and a deeper theological argument about the nature of extraordinary events. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Encounter al-Warraq's Objection
Read the opening lines where al-Warraq argues that prophets could not have known all natural powers, so their 'miracles' might be tricks.
Notice his example of the magnetic stone.
2
Examine Maturidi's First Response
Maturidi turns the argument: he asks if al-Warraq himself has attained that knowledge to make his judgment.
This is a powerful rhetorical move—it exposes the skeptic's own ignorance.
3
Consider the Second, Substantive Response
Maturidi argues that even if the magnet stone's attraction is natural, its specific property does not allow generalization. Prophetic miracles are extraordinary and depart from natural essences, making them unique signs.
The text cuts off, but the point is clear: miracles are divinely specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما ذكره الوراق، فقال فيما جاء به الرسل من الآيات المعجزات التي بمثلها يثبت القول بالتوحيد إنهم لم يمتحنوا قوى الخلق، ولا وقفوا على طبائع العالم التي يستعان بها في الأفعال، بل لم تبلغ علم أكثرهم . فكيف يعرفون بذلك مبالغ الحيل؟ وهل الذي رأوا إلا كلعب أتى العجب ؟ وهل حدث السحر إلا لجذب حجر المغناطيس الحديد؟ فيقال له أبلغت انت الذي ذكرت لتعلم أنت الذي قلته طعن أو تمويه؟ فمهما قال من شيء فهو له جواب في الأول. وجواب آخر أنه لو كان في جوهر العالم الذي ذكر لم يحتمل ظهور ما ذكر من الحجر؛ لأن الخاص إنما يُحفظ باسمه، لما يخرج من الاحتمال والبعد من الآيات، وتخصيص ذلك من جوهره في الأعجوبة، فأوجب ذلك أمر ما جاء به الرسل خصوصاً لهم، ليكون له دليلاً على أنه في الخروج عن جوهره بالذي
Then we mention a portion of what al-Warraq mentioned. He said concerning the miraculous signs brought by the messengers, by which the statement of tawhid is established: "They (the messengers) did not test the powers of creation, nor did they come to know the natures of the world by which one seeks help in actions; rather, most of them did not reach such knowledge. So how can they know through that the extremes of tricks? And what did they see except like a play that brought wonder? Did sorcery occur except for the attraction of a magnetic stone to iron?" So it is said to him: Have you, yourself, reached what you mentioned, so that you know whether what you say is criticism or obfuscation? For whatever he says, it has an answer already in the first [argument]. And another answer: if it were within the essence of the world that he mentioned, it would not allow the appearance of what was mentioned from the stone; because the particular is only preserved by its name, due to what comes out of possibility and distance from miracles, and the specification of that from its essence in the wonder, so that necessitates the matter that the messengers brought specifically for them, to be for him a proof that it is, in departing from its essence, that by which...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an argument from al-Warraq, a critic of prophethood. Al-Warraq claims that the 'miracles' of prophets are not genuine; he argues that prophets have not fully examined the hidden powers of nature and so cannot know the limits of trickery. What they saw, he says, could be mere sleight of hand, like the wonder of a magician or the attraction of a magnetic stone—implying that prophetic miracles are just clever tricks. Maturidi responds with two answers. First, he turns the question back on al-Warraq: 'Have you yourself attained such comprehensive knowledge of all natural powers to be able to judge that the prophets' signs are tricks? If not, your objection is either empty criticism or deliberate obfuscation.' Second, he offers a more detailed rebuttal: even if such phenomena were within the natural order, the specific nature of the stone's attraction is confined to that particular object (the magnetic stone), and this specificity does not allow generalization. The prophetic miracles are extraordinary events that exceed the ordinary capacities of natural essences. They are deliberately specified by God as signs for the prophets, and this very specification—that they depart from the normal course of nature—serves as a proof of their divine origin. The passage is cut off at the page, but Maturidi's point is clear: the uniqueness and extraordinary nature of prophetic miracles distinguish them from mere magic or natural phenomena.

Key Terms
الوراق
الآيات المعجزات
التوحيد
الحيل
السحر
الجوهر
الاعجوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi denies the possibility of supernatural events, but he affirms miracles as extraordinary acts of God within creation.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny that natural phenomena occur; he argues that prophetic miracles are exceptional and specifically ordained by God, different from ordinary magic or natural events.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's second refutation?
A) The magnet stone's attraction is a miracle.
B) Prophetic miracles are beyond natural essences and are divinely specified.
C) Al-Warraq should study more about natural powers.
D) Miracles are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends the authenticity of prophetic miracles by turning the skeptic's ignorance back on him and arguing that the extraordinary specificity of miracles points to divine intervention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter objections to Islamic teachings, do you first examine your own ignorance like Maturidi does to al-Warraq? Be honest: are you willing to admit you may not have full knowledge, or do you reject outright? Will you humble yourself to seek truth from God's signs, even when they seem extraordinary?
?
Al-Warraq dismissed miracles as tricks. In your life, do you treat the signs of God—whether in the Quran, in nature, or in your own experiences—as trivial coincidences, or as extraordinary proofs of His existence and guidance? What would change if you saw each sign as a divine call to faith?
?
Maturidi argues that miracles are not like natural wonders but are unique proofs for prophets. Do you believe that God still sends signs for those who seek Him today? Are you looking for them, or have you become desensitized to the miraculous? What one step can you take this week to actively notice and reflect on God's signs?

Prophethood

Al-Warraq's Objection and the First Refutation
Maturidi presents al-Warraq's objection and the first part of his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Warraq's criticism of prophetic miracles and Maturidi's initial counter-argument that the objection is self-refuting.

Core Idea
To judge prophets, you must know their knowledge; otherwise, you condemn yourself.

Al-Warraq claimed that prophets cannot be trusted because they lack exhaustive knowledge of natural powers. Maturidi begins his refutation by turning the question back on the critic: if you claim to know the limits of prophecy, you must have attained that knowledge yourself. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read al-Warraq's statement carefully. He argues that prophets did not test the powers of creation nor know the natures of the world, so their miracles might just be tricks.
Al-Warraq compares miracles to magic, implying they are not divine.
2
Notice the first response
Maturidi asks: 'Have you yourself reached that knowledge?' This forces al-Warraq into a dilemma: either he admits ignorance (and thus cannot critique) or claims exhaustive knowledge (which is impossible for a human).
3
Analyze the trap
Whatever al-Warraq answers, his objection collapses. If he says he knows, he must prove it; if not, his criticism is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما ذكره الوراق، فقال فيما جاء به الرسل من الآيات المعجزات التي بمثلها يثبت القول بالتوحيد إنهم لم يمتحنوا قوى الخلق، ولا وقفوا على طبائع العالم التي يستعان بها في الأفعال، بل لم تبلغ علم أكثرهم. فكيف يعرفون بذلك مبالغ الحيل؟ وهل الذي رأوا إلا كلعب أتى العجب؟ وهل حدث السحر إلا لجذب حجر المغناطيس الحديد؟ فيقال له أبلغت أنت الذي ذكرت لتعلم أنت الذي قلته طعن أو تمويه؟ فمهما قال من شيء فهو له جواب في الأول. وجواب آخر أنه لو كان في جوهر العالم الذي ذكر لم يحتمل ظهور ما ذكر من الحجر؛ لأن الخاص إنما يُحفظ باسمه، لما يخرج من الاحتمال [و] البعد من الآيات، وتخصيص ذلك من جوهره في الأعجوبة، فأوجب ذلك أمر ما جاء به الرسل خصوصاً لهم، ليكون له دليلاً على أنه في الخروج عن جوهره بالذي يدعى. مع ما قد بينا فيها تقدم أنه نشأ بين قوم على طبع علموا أن مثله لا يحتمل بجوهر بشره بالذي جاء به الأرض. وبعد، فقد كثر عنهم الآيات من أنواع ما لا يحتمل ذلك بالاطلاع على جوهر إلا أن يُطلعه من علم جواهرها، وفي ذلك الذي ذكر. على أنه ما من نبي صحت نبوته إلا وقد شهد قومه منه من أعلام الصدق ما يجب به قبول قوله لولا الآيات. ثم يقال: أنت ممن يقبل خبرا في الدنيا؟ فإن قال: نعم، كلّف دليلاً على صدقه أوضح من أدلة الرسل؛ وفي ذلك وجوب القول بالذي ذكر؛ وإن قال: لا، يشهد عليه العقل وكل شيء جعله حجة، بالكذب.
Then we mention a portion of what al-Warraq mentioned, for he said concerning the signs and miracles with which the messengers came and by which the statement of tawhid (tawḥīd) is established: 'They (the messengers) did not test the powers of creation, nor did they come to know the natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) of the world by which help is sought in actions; rather, the knowledge of most of them did not reach that. So how can they know by that the limits of stratagems? And what did they see except a play that caused wonder? And did magic arise except for the attraction of the magnet stone to iron?' So it is said to him: 'Have you yourself reached that which you mentioned, so that you know whether what you said is criticism or obfuscation?' For whatever he says of something, there is for it an answer in the first [case]. And another answer: that if it were in the essence (jawhar) of the world which he mentioned, it would not admit the appearance of what was mentioned of the stone; because the specific (al-khāṣṣ) is only preserved by its name, due to what departs from probability and remoteness from miracles, and the specification of that from its essence in the wonder. So that necessitated the matter which the messengers brought, specifically for them, so that it would be for him a proof that he, in departing from his essence, is as claimed. Along with what we have already clarified therein, that he arose among a people of natural disposition who knew that the like of it is not admissible by the essence of his human nature (bashar) by which he came to the earth. After that, the signs from them multiplied of types that do not admit that by the inspection of essence, unless He who knows their essences makes it known, and in that is what was mentioned. Moreover, there is no prophet whose prophethood is confirmed except that his people witnessed from him such signs of truth that make it obligatory to accept his statement, even apart from the miracles. Then it is said: 'Are you among those who accept a report in the world?' If he says 'Yes,' he is required to produce a proof of his truthfulness clearer than the proofs of the messengers; and in that is the necessity of accepting what was mentioned. And if he says 'No,' then reason and everything He made a proof testify against him of lying.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by al-Warraq against the validity of prophetic miracles as proofs for tawhid. The structure is: (1) al-Warraq's objection is presented, (2) a first response shows the objection self-destructs, (3) a second response demonstrates the distinct nature of miracles due to their specificity. Maturidi begins by summarizing al-Warraq's claim: the prophets did not exhaustively examine the powers of creation or the natures of the world, so how could they know the limits of what is possible through trickery? Al-Warraq compares miracles to magic and tricks. Maturidi then turns the question back on al-Warraq: have you yourself attained such exhaustive knowledge to make this accusation? Whatever al-Warraq answers, he is trapped. Then Maturidi provides a second, more substantive answer: even if the phenomenon of the stone (magnetism) were part of the world's essence, it would not admit the miraculous appearance because specific properties are defined by their names and depart from normal probability. The miracles of the prophets are specified and unique, serving as proofs that the prophet is outside normal human capability. Maturidi adds that prophets arise among people who know their nature and recognize the impossibility of such acts from a human. After establishing that miracles are distinct, Maturidi argues that even without miracles, prophets bring clear signs of truthfulness that necessitate acceptance. He concludes with a dilemma: if the opponent accepts reports in worldly affairs, they must demand from the informant a proof clearer than that of the prophets—which is impossible; if they reject all reports, then reason accuses them of lying.

Key Terms
وراق (al-Warraq)
معجزات (miracles)
طبائع العالم (natures of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are merely unknown natural tricks.
Correction: Al-Warraq's objection reduces miracles to magic, but Maturidi shows this critique is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's first response to al-Warraq?
A) He argues that prophets do have complete knowledge of nature.
B) He asks whether al-Warraq himself has the knowledge to make such a claim.
C) He says miracles are beyond human understanding.
D) He ignores the objection entirely.

Key Takeaway
Al-Warraq's objection collapses because it assumes the very knowledge it denies the prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim that seems too extraordinary to be true, do you immediately look for natural explanations, like al-Warraq did, to avoid the responsibility of believing? What would it cost you to take that claim seriously and examine it with an open heart?
?
Maturidi asks al-Warraq: 'Do you know all that you speak of?' If this question were put to you about your own doubts regarding matters of faith, would you have a satisfying answer? How does your pride in 'knowing' actually blind you to the truth?
?
If you have ever dismissed a miracle or a sign of God as mere coincidence or trickery, ask yourself: was it because you truly understood the limits of nature, or because you were afraid of what belief would demand of you? What step can you take today to lower that shield and submit to the overwhelming evidence of prophecy?

The Specificity of Miracles as Proofs
Maturidi gives a second, more substantive refutation and concludes with a universal dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument: miracles are distinguished by their specificity and serve as unique divine proofs, even apart from other signs of truth.

Core Idea
Miracles are not generic wonders; they are specific acts that break known patterns, pointing directly to divine origin.

After the initial refutation, Maturidi provides a deeper argument: miracles are essentially different from natural phenomena because they are specific (khāṣṣ) and depart from the normal essence of things. This lesson explores that distinction and the final dilemma that forces acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Maturidi argues that even if magnetism were inherent in the world, the specific miracle of a prophet is unique and not just a natural event.
The example of the magnet stone shows that natural events are bound by essence; miracles transcend that.
2
Understand 'specificity'
The term 'al-khāṣṣ' (the specific) means that miracles are preserved by their name and depart from probability. This sets them apart from ordinary acts.
Miracles are 'specifically' tied to the prophet's claim, not to natural laws.
3
Notice the broader argument
Maturidi adds that even without miracles, prophets bring clear signs of truthfulness that compel acceptance. He ends with a dilemma: either you accept reports and must demand a proof clearer than the prophets' (impossible), or you reject all reports and reason accuses you of lying.
This dilemma leaves no escape for the skeptic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما ذكره الوراق، فقال فيما جاء به الرسل من الآيات المعجزات التي بمثلها يثبت القول بالتوحيد إنهم لم يمتحنوا قوى الخلق، ولا وقفوا على طبائع العالم التي يستعان بها في الأفعال، بل لم تبلغ علم أكثرهم. فكيف يعرفون بذلك مبالغ الحيل؟ وهل الذي رأوا إلا كلعب أتى العجب؟ وهل حدث السحر إلا لجذب حجر المغناطيس الحديد؟ فيقال له أبلغت أنت الذي ذكرت لتعلم أنت الذي قلته طعن أو تمويه؟ فمهما قال من شيء فهو له جواب في الأول. وجواب آخر أنه لو كان في جوهر العالم الذي ذكر لم يحتمل ظهور ما ذكر من الحجر؛ لأن الخاص إنما يُحفظ باسمه، لما يخرج من الاحتمال [و] البعد من الآيات، وتخصيص ذلك من جوهره في الأعجوبة، فأوجب ذلك أمر ما جاء به الرسل خصوصاً لهم، ليكون له دليلاً على أنه في الخروج عن جوهره بالذي يدعى. مع ما قد بينا فيها تقدم أنه نشأ بين قوم على طبع علموا أن مثله لا يحتمل بجوهر بشره بالذي جاء به الأرض. وبعد، فقد كثر عنهم الآيات من أنواع ما لا يحتمل ذلك بالاطلاع على جوهر إلا أن يُطلعه من علم جواهرها، وفي ذلك الذي ذكر. على أنه ما من نبي صحت نبوته إلا وقد شهد قومه منه من أعلام الصدق ما يجب به قبول قوله لولا الآيات. ثم يقال: أنت ممن يقبل خبرا في الدنيا؟ فإن قال: نعم، كلّف دليلاً على صدقه أوضح من أدلة الرسل؛ وفي ذلك وجوب القول بالذي ذكر؛ وإن قال: لا، يشهد عليه العقل وكل شيء جعله حجة، بالكذب.
Then we mention a portion of what al-Warraq mentioned, for he said concerning the signs and miracles with which the messengers came and by which the statement of tawhid (tawḥīd) is established: 'They (the messengers) did not test the powers of creation, nor did they come to know the natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) of the world by which help is sought in actions; rather, the knowledge of most of them did not reach that. So how can they know by that the limits of stratagems? And what did they see except a play that caused wonder? And did magic arise except for the attraction of the magnet stone to iron?' So it is said to him: 'Have you yourself reached that which you mentioned, so that you know whether what you said is criticism or obfuscation?' For whatever he says of something, there is for it an answer in the first [case]. And another answer: that if it were in the essence (jawhar) of the world which he mentioned, it would not admit the appearance of what was mentioned of the stone; because the specific (al-khāṣṣ) is only preserved by its name, due to what departs from probability and remoteness from miracles, and the specification of that from its essence in the wonder. So that necessitated the matter which the messengers brought, specifically for them, so that it would be for him a proof that he, in departing from his essence, is as claimed. Along with what we have already clarified therein, that he arose among a people of natural disposition who knew that the like of it is not admissible by the essence of his human nature (bashar) by which he came to the earth. After that, the signs from them multiplied of types that do not admit that by the inspection of essence, unless He who knows their essences makes it known, and in that is what was mentioned. Moreover, there is no prophet whose prophethood is confirmed except that his people witnessed from him such signs of truth that make it obligatory to accept his statement, even apart from the miracles. Then it is said: 'Are you among those who accept a report in the world?' If he says 'Yes,' he is required to produce a proof of his truthfulness clearer than the proofs of the messengers; and in that is the necessity of accepting what was mentioned. And if he says 'No,' then reason and everything He made a proof testify against him of lying.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by al-Warraq against the validity of prophetic miracles as proofs for tawhid. The structure is: (1) al-Warraq's objection is presented, (2) a first response shows the objection self-destructs, (3) a second response demonstrates the distinct nature of miracles due to their specificity. Maturidi begins by summarizing al-Warraq's claim: the prophets did not exhaustively examine the powers of creation or the natures of the world, so how could they know the limits of what is possible through trickery? Al-Warraq compares miracles to magic and tricks. Maturidi then turns the question back on al-Warraq: have you yourself attained such exhaustive knowledge to make this accusation? Whatever al-Warraq answers, he is trapped. Then Maturidi provides a second, more substantive answer: even if the phenomenon of the stone (magnetism) were part of the world's essence, it would not admit the miraculous appearance because specific properties are defined by their names and depart from normal probability. The miracles of the prophets are specified and unique, serving as proofs that the prophet is outside normal human capability. Maturidi adds that prophets arise among people who know their nature and recognize the impossibility of such acts from a human. After establishing that miracles are distinct, Maturidi argues that even without miracles, prophets bring clear signs of truthfulness that necessitate acceptance. He concludes with a dilemma: if the opponent accepts reports in worldly affairs, they must demand from the informant a proof clearer than that of the prophets—which is impossible; if they reject all reports, then reason accuses them of lying.

Key Terms
خاص (specific)
جوهر (essence)
أعلام الصدق (signs of truth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are just extraordinary natural events that can be explained by unknown natural laws.
Correction: Maturidi argues that miracles are distinct because they are 'specific' and depart from the essence of things, making them direct divine interventions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second answer, what makes a miracle a proof of prophethood?
A) It is a very rare natural event.
B) It is a specific act that departs from the natural essence of things.
C) It requires extensive study of the world's natures.
D) It is only accepted by the prophet's community.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are unique divine proofs because they are specific acts that depart from the natural essence, and even without miracles, prophets offer undeniable signs of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim that seems impossible, do you immediately dismiss it as false, or do you consider that it might be a sign from Allah? How does your reaction compare to the way you accept ordinary news from people you trust?
?
If you were to demand a miracle before believing a prophet, what kind of proof would satisfy you? Would that proof itself need to be verified? Reflect on whether your standards are higher than what Allah has provided through His prophets.
?
The passage ends by saying that if you reject all reports, reason itself accuses you of lying. Is there an area of your life where you accept reports without evidence but reject divine revelation? What would it take for you to apply the same standard to both?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Objection on Extraordinary Claims
Ibn al-Rawandi presents a hypothetical counterexample to challenge the proof of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Ibn al-Rawandi's objection to the proof of miracles and how it uses hypothetical examples to challenge the criteria for prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Rawandi challenges miracles by comparing them to any extraordinary claim.

This lesson presents a critical objection raised by Ibn al-Rawandi against the standard argument for prophetic miracles. He proposes that if someone claims to have extraordinary abilities—like moving planets or extinguishing the sun—such claims would be subject to the same skepticism as prophetic miracles. This forces us to consider what distinguishes true miracles from mere false claims. The objection is incomplete here, as Maturidi's response follows later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: 'if someone claimed... then he would need to...'
The passage is a single long sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the three hypothetical examples
Ibn al-Rawandi lists three extraordinary claims: communicating with planets, extinguishing the sun by placing something opposite it, and causing the sea to cast out its contents by touching it. Identify each one in the text.
3
Consider the force of the objection
Ask yourself: How do these examples mirror the challenge to prophetic miracles? Ibn al-Rawandi is suggesting that if such claims were made, they would be dismissed as lies unless proven. This parallels the skeptic's dismissal of miracles.
The objection is a dialectical move, not a full argument.
4
Reflect on the missing response
Note that Maturidi's response to this objection is not included in this lesson. Consider how the objection might be answered—what makes a prophetic miracle different from a mere extraordinary claim?
The full argument is incomplete without the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارضه ابن الروندي أن أحدًا لو ادعى طبيعة يُحدّث بها الكواكب، أو ادعى شيئًا لو نصبه مقابل الشمس يذهب ضوؤها، أو أنه إذا مس البحر لفظ البحر جميع ما فيه،
And Ibn al-Rawandi opposed him [saying] that if someone claimed a nature by which he communicates with the planets, or claimed something that if he placed it opposite the sun would extinguish its light, or that if he touched the sea would cause the sea to cast out all that is in it,
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage introduces a new objection from Ibn al-Rawandi. It is presented as a counterargument to Maturidi's previous refutation of al-Warraq. Ibn al-Rawandi suggests that if someone makes an extraordinary claim—such as communicating with the planets, extinguishing the sun, or causing the sea to vomit its contents—this claim would be subject to the same kind of challenge as prophetic miracles. The objection implies that such claims could be dismissed as lies unless proven, similar to how al-Warraq dismissed prophetic miracles. However, the response to this objection is not provided in the available pages; it likely follows in the subsequent part of the text. The incomplete nature of this passage requires caution; it is presented here only as the objection, and the reader should expect the author's rebuttal in the continuation.

Every Prophet Has Signs of Truthfulness
Maturidi states the principle and presents a dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that every true prophet has clear signs of truthfulness that make acceptance of his message obligatory, even before considering miracles.

Core Idea
True prophets always come with undeniable signs of truthfulness.

Maturidi begins by laying a foundational argument for prophethood. He asserts that no true prophet lacks evident signs of truthfulness witnessed by his people. This principle is crucial for distinguishing genuine prophets from impostors. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence and note Maturidi's universal statement about prophets and signs of truthfulness.
The phrase 'a'lam al-sidq' is key.
2
Understand the dialectical question
Maturidi then asks whether the opponent accepts news in worldly matters. This sets up a dilemma.
3
Explore both answers
If yes, they must provide clearer evidence than prophets; if no, their own intellect and proofs testify against them.
This forces the opponent to either accept prophetic evidence or be self-refuting.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The outcome is that accepting prophetic truthfulness becomes necessary.
This conclusion is drawn from the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه ما من نبي صحت نبوته إلا وقد شهد قومه منه من أعلام الصدق ما يجب قبول قوله لولا الآيات. ثم يقال : أنت ممن يقبل خبرا في الدنيا؟ فإن قال: نعم، كلف دليلا على صدقه أوضح من أدلة الرسل؛ وفي ذلك وجوب القول بالذي ذكر؛ وإن قال: لا، يشهد عليه العقل وكل شيء جعله حجة بالكذب. وعارضه ابن الروندي بأن أحدا لو ادعى طبيعة يحدث بها الكواكب، أو ادعى شيئا لو نصبه مقابل الشمس يذهب ضوؤها، أو أنه إذا مس البحر لفظ البحر جميع ما فيه، وإذا مسح به قدمه طار في الهواء وارتفع إلى السماء ويصير سحابا يمطر، فإذا لزم تكذيبه بما ادعى الخروج عن طبائع معروفة فمثله الأول. مع ما كان المكذب ليس معه شيء، ومع الآخر شيء بالظنون يرد وبالاحتمال، وما به قد يمكن غيبا، والحجة ظاهرة، فلزم القول به.
Indeed, there is no prophet whose prophethood is proven except that his people witnessed from him signs of truthfulness that oblige acceptance of his words, were it not for the miracles. Then it is said: Are you among those who accept news in this world? If he says yes, he is required to provide evidence of his truthfulness more clear than the evidences of the messengers; and in that is obligation of the statement which was mentioned. And if he says no, the intellect and everything He made a proof bear witness against him of lying. And Ibn al-Rawandi objected to him that if someone claimed a nature by which he influences the stars, or claimed something that if he places it facing the sun it would extinguish its light, or that if he touches the sea it would expel all that is in it, and if he wipes with it his foot he would fly in the air and ascend to the sky and become a rain-bearing cloud, then if his rejection is necessary due to his claim of departing from known natures, then the first (the prophet) is similar. But the rejecter has nothing with him, while with the other (the prophet) there is something that is rejected by conjectures and by possibility, and that by which something unseen might be possible, while the proof is apparent, so acceptance of him is necessary.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Maturidi first establishes that every true prophet has clear signs of truthfulness from his people that make acceptance of his words obligatory, even without considering the miracles. He then presents a dialectical question: if someone accepts news in worldly matters, he must demand from the prophet evidence clearer than that of other messengers; if he does not accept any news, then his intellect and every proof testify that he is lying. Then Ibn al-Rawandi objects with a hypothetical: if someone claimed extraordinary powers like controlling stars, extinguishing the sun, making the sea eject its contents, or flying and becoming a cloud, then if we reject him because he departs from known natures, we should similarly reject the prophet. But Maturidi answers that the difference is that the objector has no evidence at all, while the prophet has evidence that is only dismissible by conjectures and possibilities, whereas the proof is apparent. Therefore, acceptance of the prophet is necessary.

Key Terms
نبي
نبوة
أعلام الصدق
آيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are only known through miracles.
Correction: Maturidi shows that before miracles, there are signs of truthfulness that oblige acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does every true prophet have?
A) A large following
B) Signs of truthfulness witnessed by his people
C) Immediate miracles
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The presence of clear signs of truthfulness makes a prophet's message obligatory to accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time when you saw clear signs of Allah's guidance—perhaps through a verse, a lecture, or a situation—but you hesitated to change your ways. What held you back, and how does this lesson's claim that signs demand acceptance challenge your hesitation? Will you commit to acting on the next clear sign you see?
?
Maturidi's argument forces you to examine whether you accept news from others inconsistently. Are there areas of your life where you accept worldly reports without question but reject divine messages? If you were to apply the same standard, what would you need to change starting today?
?
The lesson says that rejecting all news—including prophetic news—causes your own intellect to testify against you. Have you ever dismissed Islamic teachings because they seemed inconvenient or difficult? How can you ensure your intellect is used to seek truth rather than to avoid accountability? What is one step you will take this week to align your intellectual acceptance with the signs you have already seen?

Refuting Ibn al-Rawandi's Objection
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's analogy between false claimants and true prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection that extraordinary claims are all equally rejectable, by highlighting the presence of evidence in the prophet's case.

Core Idea
Extraordinary claims backed by evidence are not comparable to baseless ones.

Ibn al-Rawandi raised a clever objection: if someone claims supernatural powers, we reject them as unnatural. Why should prophets be different? Maturidi's answer highlights the presence of evidence. Let's see his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Ibn al-Rawandi gives examples of extraordinary claims (controlling stars, extinguishing the sun, etc.) that would be rejected as unnatural.
He argues that prophetic claims are similar.
2
Understand the analogy
The objector claims that if we reject such claims due to departing from known natures, we should also reject prophets.
3
See Maturidi's distinction
Maturidi points out that the objector has no evidence whatsoever, while the prophet has apparent proof that can only be dismissed by conjectures and possibilities.
The key phrase: 'al-mukadhdhib laysa ma'ahu shay'.
4
Reach the conclusion
Because the prophet's proof is apparent and the evidence is there, acceptance of him is necessary.
