Consensus on Universal Death as Prophetic Proof
Maturidi presents the consensus argument and three rebuttals against skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the consensus that all humans die to argue that this knowledge must come from prophets.

Core Idea
Universal death is known by consensus through prophetic reports, not observation.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument: all humans, regardless of time or place, agree that every human dies. Yet no one has witnessed every death. This universal agreement, he argues, could only come from the reports of prophets. In this lesson, we explore this argument and Maturidi's three rebuttals against a skeptic's objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument from consensus
Read how Maturidi reports that people agree on the death of all humans, even though they haven't witnessed every death. This agreement is said to come from messengers.
Consensus here is not a logical proof but a transmitted fact.
2
Follow the three rebuttals against al-Warraq
Maturidi gives three reasons why al-Warraq's skepticism fails: (1) his knowledge is limited, (2) his proof depends on a past trial, (3) the knowledge cannot be empirical so it must be from prophets.
3
Reflect on why this matters for prophethood
Consider how this argument establishes that certain universal beliefs are only possible through divine communication.
This is a key method in proving the necessity of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتتج على الوراق بما أجمع الناس على موت البشر كلهم وإن لم يشهدوا الكل بالرسل فقال فيه الإجماع. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وقد علم أنه لم يشهد، بل لم يبلغ علمه شيء. والثاني أنه علق دليله في ذلك بالمحنة وقد زال. والثالث أن ذلك إذ لا يبلغ بالتدبير ثبت أنه قيل بالرسل. وقال في قول الفلسفة: إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت. تأملوا حماقته، بعد قول قوم: لو أدركوه لأدركوه بالرسل، ثم ينكر قول الرسل مع البرهان. والثاني أنه لم يمتحن عقول الفلاسفة ولا هم امتحنوا طبائع الجميع. والثالث أنه لو كان بالتركيب لما اختلف قدر جميع الحياة. وقال: بالطباع إن النفس لا تطمع في دفعه ولا ترجو الظفر به. فجوابه أنه لم يمتحن طبائع الكل. والثاني أنها سكنت إلى هذا بالتوارث من قول الرسل. والثالث كذلك آيات الرسل لم تطمع إلا بعسر إتيان مثلها، وهي بحيث تحتمل الطمع، مع ما كانت فيها ما لا يطمع مع التقريع والتحدي، وفيها ما لا يحتمل الطمع ألبتة نحو انشقاق القمر.
And he argued against al-Warraq by what people have agreed upon concerning the death of all humans, even if they did not witness all, through the messengers, so he said: there is consensus in it. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: And he knew that he did not witness, rather his knowledge did not reach anything. Second, that he based his proof in that on trial which has passed. Third, that that, since it is not attained by planning, it is established that it was said by the messengers. And he said concerning the saying of philosophy: 'The composition of the animal is a composition that dies.' Consider his foolishness, after the saying of a people: 'If they had perceived it, they would have perceived it through the messengers,' then he denies the saying of the messengers along with the proof. Second, that he did not test the intellects of the philosophers, nor did they test the natures of all. Third, that if it were by composition, then the capacity of all life would not differ. And he said: by nature, the soul does not covet its repulsion nor hope to overcome it. So his answer: he did not test the natures of all. Second, that it settled into this by inheritance from the saying of the messengers. Third, likewise the miracles of the messengers are not coveted except by difficulty of producing their like, and they are such that coveting is possible, while among them there is what is not coveted at all with rebuke and challenge, and among them what does not admit coveting at all, like the splitting of the moon.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a series of proofs and refutations. Maturidi reports an argument against al-Warraq (a skeptic) based on the consensus of people that all humans die, even though no one has witnessed every death—this knowledge comes from the reports of messengers. So consensus exists. Then Maturidi (Abu Mansur) gives three rebuttals: (1) al-Warraq did not personally witness all deaths, so his knowledge is limited; (2) his proof is based on a trial that has passed; (3) since this knowledge is not attained through empirical planning, it must come from messengers. Next, he refutes the philosopher's claim that animal composition necessarily leads to death: (a) it is foolish to deny the messengers while relying on a proof that actually came from them; (b) the philosophers did not test all intellects or natures; (c) if death were due to composition, then all living beings would have the same lifespan. Finally, he refutes the argument from 'nature' that the soul does not covet avoiding death: (1) the nature of all things is not tested; (2) the soul's settlement with death is inherited from the messengers' reports; (3) the miracles of the messengers are of two types: some are so difficult to imitate that they inspire covetousness, and others like the splitting of the moon are impossible to covet at all.

Key Terms
إجماع
موت البشر
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that universal death is known purely through empirical observation of many deaths.
Correction: Maturidi argues that no one has witnessed all deaths, so the consensus must come from prophetic reports.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Maturidi argue that the knowledge of universal death must come from messengers?
A) Because everyone has personally witnessed death.
B) Because empirical planning cannot achieve such knowledge.
C) Because philosophers proved it.
D) Because it is a logical necessity.

Key Takeaway
The consensus that all humans die, unattainable by observation, is evidence that this knowledge came from prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know for certain that you will die—but is this knowledge shaping how you live today? What would change if you truly internalized that this certainty came not from watching others die, but from the word of prophets? What would you do differently starting tomorrow?
?
Consider something you believe about the unseen—like the reality of death or the afterlife. Is your belief resting on empirical observation or on the authority of revelation? If it's the former, how can you be sure you haven't missed something? If the latter, are you living as if it's absolutely true?
?
Al-Warraq rejected the prophetic origin of universal death, insisting on his own limited observation. In what area of your life are you acting like al-Warraq—trusting your own experience over the clear reports of the prophets? Name one thing you will change today to align your trust with revelation.

Refuting Philosophical and Naturalist Denials of Prophetic Knowledge
Maturidi refutes philosophical and naturalist theories and uses miracles to affirm prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the philosophical claim that death is due to composition and the naturalist claim that the soul's nature precludes revelation.

Core Idea
Philosophical and naturalist explanations for death fail, pointing back to prophetic reports.

After establishing that the consensus on death comes from prophets, Maturidi tackles alternative theories: the philosopher's claim that death results from bodily composition, and the naturalist's claim that the soul naturally accepts death. He refutes both, showing their weaknesses and reinforcing that knowledge of life and death ultimately relies on prophetic miracles—some of which are beyond any human capacity to imitate.

Guided Path
1
Examine the philosophical argument on composition
Read Maturidi's refutation of the idea that death is caused by the animal's composition. He points out three flaws: it foolishly denies messengers, relies on untested intellects, and fails to account for varying lifespans.
The 'composition' theory is from Greek philosophy.
2
Examine the naturalist argument on the soul
Maturidi refutes the claim that the soul does not covet avoiding death by nature. He argues: (a) nature is not tested universally, (b) the soul's acceptance is inherited from prophets, (c) miracles prove prophetic authority.
3
Appreciate the miracle argument
Note how Maturidi concludes that prophets brought miracles that inspire awe, some like the splitting of the moon are utterly inimitable, proving their divine origin.

The Analogy of Universal Death
Maturidi argues by analogy from universal death to miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the consensus on universal death to argue for the acceptance of miracles based on prophetic testimony.

Core Idea
If you accept universal death without witnessing it, you can accept miracles too.

Maturidi presents a clever refutation: the very critic who denies miracles because they go against known nature accepts the universal death of all humans, despite not having witnessed every death. He does so based on reports from messengers. This shows that accepting reliable testimony about extraordinary events is rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, then Maturidi's analogy.
Pay attention to the word 'طبائع' (natures) and 'إجماع' (consensus).
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent denies a miracle because it violates known natural laws (طبائع). Maturidi says the same objection applies to the opponent's own acceptance of universal death.
3
Understand the analogy
The opponent accepts that all humans die even though he hasn't witnessed every death. He relies on reports from messengers (رسل). Maturidi argues that the same reasoning should lead him to accept miracles.
Consensus (إجماع) here means agreement based on prophetic reports.
4
Reflect on the proof
Maturidi says the proof is manifest (حجة ظاهرة) and it is necessary to affirm the miracle. Consider why he says this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا مسح به قدمه طار في الهواء وارتفع إلى السماء ويصير سحابا يمطر، فإذ لزم تكذيبه بما ادعى الخروج عن طبائع معروفة فمثله الأول. مع ما كان المكذب ليس معه شيء، ومع الآخر شيء بالظنون يُردّ وبالاحتمال ، وما به قد يمكن غيبا ، والحجة ظاهرة، فلزم القول به واحتج على الوراق بما أجمع [ الناس ] على موت البشر كلهم - وإن لم يشهدوا الكل - بالرسل ، فقال : فيه الإجماع. قال / أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد علم أنه لم يشهد، بل لم يبلغ علمه شيء. والثاني أنه علق دليله في ذلك بالمحنة، وقد زال . والثالث أن ذلك إذ لا يبلغ بالتدبير ثبت أنه قيل بالرسل.
And if he wiped with it his foot, it flew in the air and rose to the sky and became a rain-cloud. So since it is necessary to deny him because of what he claimed of departure from known natures, the example is the same as the first. Since the denier has nothing with him, and the other has something that is rejected by conjectures and by possibility, and what by it may be possible in the unseen, and the proof is manifest, so it is necessary to affirm it, and he argued against al-Warraq by what [people] agreed upon concerning the death of all humans – even if they did not witness all – by the messengers, so he said: ‘There is consensus on it.’ Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: And it is known that he did not witness, rather his knowledge did not reach anything. Second, that he based his proof in that on trial, and it has passed. Third, that since that cannot be attained by planning, it is established that it was said by the messengers.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against miracles. The opponent argues that a claimed miracle (turning a touch into a flying rain-cloud) is impossible because it contradicts known natural laws. Al-Maturidi responds by drawing an analogy: the opponent himself accepts the universal death of all humans even though he has not witnessed every human die. He accepts this based on reports from messengers (consensus). This shows that accepting something beyond immediate experience based on reliable testimony is rational. Al-Maturidi then critiques the opponent’s argument in three points: (1) The opponent did not witness every human death, so his knowledge is incomplete. (2) He based his evidence on a trial (i.e., empirical testing) that is no longer possible. (3) Since such universal knowledge cannot be obtained through personal planning or experience, it must have been conveyed by messengers. Thus, the opponent’s denial of miracles is inconsistent with his own acceptance of reported facts.

Key Terms
طبائع
إجماع
رسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are impossible because they violate the laws of nature.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same reasoning would also deny the universal death of all humans, which is accepted through testimony. Thus, miracles can be accepted on the basis of reliable reports.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, on what basis does the opponent accept that all humans die?
A) Personal observation
B) Philosophical deduction
C) Reports from messengers
D) Scientific experiment

Key Takeaway
Accepting universal death through prophetic testimony is analogous to accepting miracles through similar testimony, so the opponent's denial is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You accept that every human dies without having witnessed every death—solely based on reports. Is your acceptance of the Prophet's reports about the unseen (like miracles, the afterlife) as firm as that? If not, what is holding you back? Today, commit to treating prophetic testimony with the same certainty you give to universal death.
?
Consider an area where you demand direct evidence before believing something in your faith (e.g., a miracle, a divine decree). Are you inconsistent like the opponent? Where in your heart do you resist testimony? Ask Allah to grant you the humility to accept His proofs even when they challenge your expectations.
?
If you were the denier in this passage, would you have the integrity to admit your inconsistency? What would it cost you? Are you willing to surrender your intellectual pride to the truth, even if it overturns what you thought was 'natural'? Make a concrete decision today to follow evidence wherever it leads.

Three Critiques of the Denier
Maturidi lists three logical flaws in the denier's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three specific critiques of the opponent's argument and how they undermine the denial of miracles.

Core Idea
The denier's own knowledge is incomplete, his proof is obsolete, and his method impossible.

After establishing the analogy, Maturidi delivers three pointed critiques. He shows that the opponent did not actually witness universal death, that his empirical test is no longer available, and that such comprehensive knowledge can only come from messengers. These critiques dismantle the basis of the denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it begins with 'قال أبو منصور رحمه الله' and lists three points: أولاً, ثانياً, ثالثاً.
2
Understand the first critique
Maturidi states that the opponent did not witness every death, and his knowledge did not reach that. This undermines the opponent's claim to certainty.
3
Understand the second critique
The opponent based his argument on a 'trial' (محنة) that is no longer possible. This means his proof is not verifiable now.
4
Understand the third critique
Since such universal knowledge cannot be attained through personal planning (تدبير) or experience, it must have been conveyed by messengers.

Refuting the Nature Argument Against Miracles
Maturidi refutes the nature-based objection to miracles with three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's threefold refutation of the claim that human nature precludes hope in overcoming death through miracles.

Core Idea
Nature does not dictate impossibility; prophets' teachings and miracles themselves show otherwise.

This passage tackles a skeptical objection: that by natural disposition humans cannot hope to escape death, so miracles that defy nature are impossible. Imam Maturidi dismantles this with three arguments—questioning the generalization, tracing the belief to inherited tradition, and distinguishing types of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Let the structure of the objection and responses become clear.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the objection and three responses
The objector claims that by nature the soul does not hope to overcome death. Find how Maturidi counters this with three distinct points.
The responses are numbered implicitly: 'فجوابه أنه لم يمتحن... والثاني... والثالث'
3
Analyze the third response on miracles
Focus on the types of miracles: some are difficult but admit hope, others like the splitting of the moon are utterly beyond imitation. Why does this matter?
This distinction shows that hope is not ruled out by nature, but by the degree of inimitability.
4
Connect to the purpose of proving prophethood
Reflect on how this argument defends the possibility of miracles, which are central to establishing the truth of prophets.
Maturidi's goal is to uphold the rational basis for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: بالطباع إن النفس لا تطمع في دفعه ولا ترجو الظفر به. فجوابه أنه لم يمتحن طبائع الكل. والثاني أنها سكنت إلى هذا بالتوارث من قول الرسل. والثالث كذلك آيات الرسل لم تُطمع إلا بعسر إتيان مثلها ، وهي بحيث تحتمل الطمع، مع ما كانت فيها ما لا يُطمع مع التقريع والتحدي، وفيها ما لا يحتمل الطمع ألبتة نحو انشقاق القمر.
And he said: ‘By nature, the soul does not hope to repel it (death) nor does it hope to overcome it.’ The answer to him is that he did not test all natures. Second, that it (the soul) settled upon this by inheritance from the saying of the messengers. Third, likewise, the signs (miracles) of the messengers are not hoped for except with difficulty of producing their like, and they are such that they admit of hope, along with what was in them that is not hoped for despite rebuke and challenge, and among them are those that do not admit of hope at all, such as the splitting of the moon.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that by natural disposition the human soul does not hope to overcome death, implying that miracles that overcome natural processes are impossible. Al-Maturidi gives three responses: (1) The objector has not examined all natures, so he cannot generalize. (2) The acceptance of death as inevitable is not from nature alone but inherited from the teachings of messengers. (3) Miracles are of two types: some are difficult but not impossible to imitate (they admit hope), while others like the splitting of the moon are utterly inimitable and thus exclude hope entirely. Thus, the fact that people do not hope to overcome death is due to transmitted belief, not an inherent limitation of nature.

Key Terms
طباع
طمع
توارث
رسل
آيات
انشقاق القمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human nature inherently prevents anyone from hoping to overcome death, making miracles that counteract death impossible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that hope is not ruled out by nature; rather, it is often based on inherited teachings, and some miracles are so unique that they transcend any natural hope, proving divine intervention.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Maturidi give of a miracle that does not permit any hope of imitation?
A) Raising the dead.
B) Splitting of the moon.
C) Turning a staff into a snake.
D) Causing rain.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the impossibility of imitating some miracles, like the splitting of the moon, proves they are beyond natural human capacity and thus divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face something that seems impossible by nature (like changing a deep habit or overcoming a fear), do you secretly believe that change is impossible? What would it mean for you to truly accept that some limitations are inherited beliefs, not fixed realities?
?
This passage argues that even the hope to imitate miracles like the moon splitting is impossible. Have you ever witnessed or heard of something that you knew with certainty could only come from Allah? How did that shape your reliance on Him versus your own efforts?
?
Maturidi says the belief that death is inevitable is 'inherited from the messengers,' not an instinct. What are you passively accepting as true because it has been passed down—perhaps without questioning whether it aligns with the deeper purpose of faith? Will you examine one such belief this week?

The First Sign of a Messenger: Moral Character
Maturidi describes the first sign of a messenger: his known moral purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first sign of a true messenger according to Imam al-Maturidi: his manifest moral character that eliminates doubt and compels acceptance by nature and intellect.

Core Idea
The messenger's moral purity, witnessed by his community, is a compelling sign that nature and intellect accept.

Imam al-Maturidi presents the first of two signs of prophethood: the messenger's known moral character. This sign is rooted in the community's direct observation of the messenger's life from childhood to adulthood, which leaves no room for doubt. As you read, notice how al-Maturidi argues that such purity cannot be attained by ordinary upbringing, pointing to divine protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage ends abruptly; it covers only the first sign.
2
Identify the key elements of the sign
Notice that the sign is based on community observation: the messenger's purity, piety, and trustworthiness from childhood to adulthood, which are unparalleled.
This observation eliminates both doubt (ريبة) and suspicion (ظنة).
3
Understand the inference and the rejecter's position
Al-Maturidi concludes that such character is from divine protection for a noble mission. He then explains that rejection after such evidence is due to obstinacy, not valid doubt.
Consider why nature and intellect both approve this sign.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل عندنا في أعلام الرسل وجهان. أحدهما ظهور أحوالهم على جهة تدفع العقول عنه الريبة وتأبى فيه توهّم الظنة، بما صحبوه في الصغر والكبر فوجدوه طاهرًا صفيًا تقياً بين أظهر قوم ما احتمل التسوية بينهم على ذلك، ولا تربيتهم تبلغ ذلك، على ظهور أحواله لهم وكونه بينهم في القرار والانتشار. فيُعلَم باحاطةٍ أن ذلك حِفْظُ مَن يعلم أنه يقيمه مقاماً شريفاً، ويجعله أميناً على الغيوب والأسرار. وهذا مما تميل إلى قبوله الطبيعة، ويستحسن جميع أموره العقل . فيكون الرّاد عليه يردّ بعد المعرفة ردَّ تعنّت له ، إما لإلف وعادة على خلاف ذلك، أو لشرف ونباهة في العاجل، أو لمطامع ومنال، وإلا فما
Then the principle with us concerning the signs of the messengers is two aspects. One of them is the appearance of their states in a manner that drives away doubt from intellects and prevents imagining suspicion, because they (the people) accompanied them in childhood and adulthood and found him pure, clear, pious among his people, such that equality with them (others) in that is not possible, nor does their upbringing reach that, given the appearance of his states to them and his being among them in settlement and travel. So it is known comprehensively that that is the protection of One who knows that He will appoint him to a noble station and make him a trustee over the unseen and secrets. And this is something that nature inclines to accept, and intellect approves all of his affairs. So the rejecter of him rejects after knowledge out of obstinacy, either due to custom and habit contrary to that, or for immediate honor and prominence, or for desires and gains, and if not then...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two signs of a true messenger according to al-Maturidi. The sign is the messenger's manifest moral character: from childhood to adulthood, his community witnesses his purity, clarity, and piety, which is unparalleled by others. This observation eliminates doubt and suspicion. The fact that such a person exists among them, both in settled life and travel, and that his character is consistently virtuous, indicates that God is preserving him for a noble mission and entrusting him with unseen knowledge and secrets. Both human nature and intellect incline to accept such a person as a messenger. Therefore, anyone who rejects him after such clear evidence does so not from genuine doubt but from obstinacy, whether due to custom, desire for worldly status, or personal gain. Otherwise, there is no valid reason to reject.

Misconception: The only proof of a true messenger is through physical miracles, not moral character.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that the messenger's moral purity, witnessed by his community, is a primary sign that nature and intellect accept, and it eliminates doubt without requiring miracles.

Key Takeaway
The first sign of a true messenger is his impeccable character, witnessed by his community, which compels rational and natural acceptance and leaves rejection as mere obstinacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-Maturidi says nature inclines to accept the prophet, but we can suppress that inclination. What deeply rooted custom or personal gain has dulled your natural sensitivity to truth? Will you actively fight that corruption or let it win?

Proof of Prophethood: Moral Character
Maturidi explains the first proof of prophethood through the prophet's moral character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first proof of prophethood based on the prophet's lifelong moral character and how it rationally compels acceptance.

Core Idea
A prophet's flawless character, witnessed by his people, is a rational proof of his prophethood.

Maturidi begins his discussion of prophethood by grounding the proof in observable reality. Rather than relying solely on miracles, he points to the prophet's lifelong moral excellence, which his own community could not deny. This passage explains why such character cannot be a product of human upbringing and must come from divine preservation, making the prophet a trustee of divine secrets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi structures the argument from observation to conclusion.
The passage mentions two aspects but only covers the first; focus on the moral character proof.
2
Identify the key elements of the proof
Look for: (1) the prophet's states repelling doubt, (2) the community's lifelong witness, (3) the inference of divine preservation, and (4) the acceptance by nature and reason.
These elements form a logical chain.
3
Understand the rejection of obstinacy
Maturidi explains that rejection of such an evidently pure prophet can only stem from stubbornness, habit, or worldly motives—not rational grounds.
This reinforces the strength of the proof.
4
Reflect on the role of community testimony
Consider why the witness of the prophet's own people is epistemically strong: they knew him intimately over decades.
This is a historical argument, not abstract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل عندنا في أعلام الرسل وجهان. أحدهما ظهور أحوالهم على جهة تدفع العقول عنه الريبة وتأبى فيه توهّم الظنة، بما صحبوه في الصغر والكبر فوجدوه طاهرا صفيا تقيا بين أظهر قوم ما احتمل التسوية بينهم على ذلك، ولا تربيتهم تبلغ ذلك، على ظهور أحواله لهم وكونه بينهم في القرار والانتشار. فيُعلم باحاطة أن ذلك حفظ من يعلم أنه يقيمه مقاما شريفا، ويجعله أمينا على الغيوب والأسرار. وهذا مما تميل إلى قبوله الطبيعة، ويستحسن جميع أموره العقل. فيكون الراد عليه يرد بعد المعرفة رد تعنت له، إما لإلف وعادة على خلاف ذلك، أو لشرف ونباهة في العاجل، أو لمطامع ومنال، وإلا فما من قلب إلا ويميل إلى من دون هذا رتبته ومحله ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the principle with us concerning the proofs of the messengers is two aspects. One of them is the manifestation of their states in a manner that repels doubt from the intellects and prevents suspicion, by what they accompanied him in (i.e., observed him) from childhood to old age, and they found him pure, clear, and pious among a people who could not be equaled in that, nor could their upbringing attain that, given the manifestation of his states to them and his being among them in settledness and travel. So it is known with certainty that that is the guarding of One who knows that He will appoint him to a noble station and make him a trustee over the unseen and secrets. And this is something that nature inclines to accept, and reason approves all his affairs. So the one who rejects him rejects after knowledge, a rejection of obstinacy, either due to custom and habit contrary to that, or for worldly honor and prominence, or for gains and aspirations. Otherwise, there is no heart but it inclines to one of lower rank and station than this. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines the first of two proofs of prophethood: the moral character of the prophet. Maturidi states that the prophet's conduct, observed by his people from childhood to old age, is so pure and perfect that it repels any doubt. They saw him as pure, clear, and pious, and they themselves could not reach his level despite living alongside him. This moral excellence indicates that God has preserved him for a noble mission and made him a trustee of divine secrets. Both human nature and reason incline to accept such a person. Those who reject him do so not due to rational objection but because of stubbornness, habit, worldly ambition, or desire. In fact, any person would naturally incline to a prophet of lower rank than this one, implying the prophet's superiority is evident.

Key Terms
أعلام الرسل
ريبة
ظنة
طبيعة
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood can only be proven through miracles.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the prophet's lifelong moral character, witnessed by his community, is a rational proof of his prophethood, independent of miracles.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the prophet's moral character prove his prophethood?
A) Because he performed miracles.
B) Because his people observed him from childhood and found him impeccable, indicating divine care.
C) Because he was wealthy and powerful.
D) Because he defeated his enemies in battle.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's impeccable character, observed by his people from childhood, proves his prophethood because it cannot be attained by human effort alone, indicating divine preservation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believe that the prophet's character is a sign from God, then your response to his teachings is not just intellectual—it is a matter of your deepest loyalty. What is one specific area of your life where you are currently resisting his character, and what would it cost you to align with it fully?

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood & Five Signs
Maturidi enumerates five signs distinguishing miracles from magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof of prophethood according to Imam Maturidi: miracles, and the five signs that distinguish prophetic miracles from magic.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles are real, enduring, and selfless, unlike transient and selfish magic.

Imam Maturidi now presents the second proof of prophethood: miracles. He not only explains that miracles are beyond human ability, but also provides five precise criteria to distinguish true prophetic miracles from mere magic. This passage grounds the authenticity of prophethood on rational and observable distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the overall structure: first the nature of miracles, then a discussion of magic, then the enumeration of five signs.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the nature of miracles
Notice that Maturidi emphasizes that miracles are unlearned and come directly from God, making prophets trustworthy trustees of revelation.
He contrasts this with magic which can be learned.
3
Identify the five signs
Locate the five enumerated signs. Each sign contrasts prophets with magicians. Try to summarize each in your own words.
The signs are introduced after 'يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون'.
4
Reflect on the final point
After the fifth sign, Maturidi adds that the prophets' message aligns with reason, good governance, and welfare. This is an additional argument for their truthfulness.
5
Apply the criteria
Think of a hypothetical claim to prophethood. How would you use these five signs to evaluate whether the claimant is a true prophet or a false one?
This helps internalize the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني مجيء الآيات الخارجة عن طبائع أهل البصر في ذلك النوع، الممتنعة عن أن يطمع في مثلها أو يبلغ بكنهها التعلم مع ما لو احتمل أن يبلغ أحد ذلك بالتعلم والاجتهاد فإن الرسل بما نشأوا لا في ذلك، وربوا لا به يظهر أنهم استفادوه بالله، أكرمهم بذلك؛ لما يجعلهم أمناء على وحيه. ولهم أيضاً معان فاقوا بها السحرة. على أن علم السحر أصله من السماء، لكن الناس نسوا أصله وتوارثوه بالتعلم، وكذلك المكاسب والحرف والصناعات كلها. فمن أكرم لا بالوجه الذي هو طريقه في المعارف علم أن ذلك تخصيص لأمر عظيم. مع ما كان معهم معان يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون؛ أحدها أنها تخرج حقيقة وتبقى ببقاء الخلقة، والسحر هو شيء يأخذ البصر ثم يضمحل. والثاني أن آية الرسل تمنع أن يدعيها من ليس برسول فتبقى معه بأن كانت في جهة سحرا وما كان. والثالث أن أولئك الذين تكلفوا استخراج العجائب بالتعلم فهم قد مالوا إلى شيء لو كان حقا لكان به غنى من عرض الدنيا، فكان معهم دليل الكذب. والرابع أن الرسل حملوا ما في الأنفس إنكاره، ذلك من كفها عن الملاذ والشهوات وحفظها عن الذين بهم عز الدنيا وشرفها ودعاء أمثالهم إلى ترك ذلك الله. والخامس مخاطرتهم بالأنفس وبذلها في وقت ضعفهم وقلة أنصارهم من الخلق، والتعرض للجبارين بتنغيص ما هم فيه عليهم، وإظهار القوة لهم من عند العزيز. على ما علموا من سوء صنيعهم بالمخالفين لهم وبخاصة من يخافون منهم تفريق جمعهم وتشتيت أمورهم. وأيضا إنهم دعوا إلى ما في العقول بيانه، وفي سياسات الملك حسنه، وبما في توقيف الخلق عليه صلاحهم دينا ودنيا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second is the coming of signs that depart from the natures of the people of insight in that type, which are beyond hope of being like them or of their true nature being reached by learning, even if it were possible for someone to attain that through learning and effort, for the messengers were not raised in that nor trained in it, so it appears that they obtained it from Allah; He honored them with that, to make them trustees of His revelation. And they also have meanings by which they surpassed the magicians. Although the knowledge of magic originally came from the heaven, but people forgot its origin and inherited it by learning, and likewise earnings, crafts, and all industries. So whoever is honored not by the way that is the path of knowledge, knows that that is a specification for a great matter. And there were with them meanings by which it is known that they are sent: First, that they come forth as reality and remain with the continuation of creation, while magic is something that takes the eyesight then vanishes. Second, that the sign of the messengers prevents it from being claimed by one who is not a messenger, so it remains with him only if it were in the manner of magic, but it was not. Third, that those who labor to produce wonders through learning have inclined to something that, if it were true, they would have sufficed with it instead of worldly goods, so with them is evidence of falsehood. Fourth, that the messengers bore what souls deny, restraining them from pleasures and desires, and protecting them from those through whom worldly honor and glory come, and calling their likes to abandon that for Allah. Fifth, their risking and sacrificing their lives at times of their weakness and few supporters, and confronting tyrants by spoiling what they have, and manifesting power from the Almighty. Based on their knowledge of the evil treatment of their opponents, especially those they fear will split their community and scatter their affairs. And also, they called to what reason clarifies, and in the policies of kings is its goodness, and in the instruction of creation is their welfare in religion and world. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of prophethood: miracles, and then distinguishes them from magic through five signs. Maturidi begins by noting that miracles are beyond human ability to produce, even through learning, and the prophets were not trained in them, so they come from God. Miracles make prophets trustworthy. He then discusses magic, acknowledging its divine origin but noting it was corrupted and passed down by learning. However, prophets surpass magicians in several ways: First, prophetic miracles are real and enduring, whereas magic is illusory and fleeting. Second, a true prophetic sign cannot be falsely claimed; if a liar claimed it, it would not persist as a miracle. Third, magicians seek worldly gain, but prophets do not; their lack of profit-seeking proves their truthfulness. Fourth, prophets call people to restrain desires and abandon worldly honors, which is contrary to what a fraud would do. Fifth, prophets risk their lives despite weakness, confronting tyrants, which shows their sincerity. Finally, their message aligns with reason and good governance, and brings welfare in both religion and this world.

Key Terms
آيات
سحر
حقيقة
تخصيص
مبعوثون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are simply extraordinary events that can be performed by anyone with special knowledge.
Correction: According to Maturidi, prophetic miracles are distinct: they are unlearned, enduring, not for personal gain, and accompanied by moral struggle.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes prophetic miracles from magic according to Maturidi's third sign?
A) Miracles are visible while magic is hidden.
B) Magicians seek worldly gain, while prophets do not, proving their truthfulness.
C) Magic only appears in certain places.
D) Prophets have more followers.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi provides five rational criteria to distinguish true prophetic miracles from magic, grounding prophethood in observable, self-evident signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The prophets risked their lives despite being weak and few. What is one situation in your life where you have avoided speaking truth or taking a stand because you feared the loss of safety or reputation? How can you imitate the prophets' courage today?
?
Magic promises immediate, dazzling effects that vanish. What 'magical' quick-fixes—relationships, achievements, distractions—are you clinging to instead of pursuing the enduring, selfless path of the prophets? Will you choose to abandon one of them this week?

Miracles Cannot Be Learned
Maturidi presents the second proof: miracles cannot be learned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophets' miracles cannot be learned and how this proves they are from God.

Core Idea
Prophets' miracles come from God, not from training.

Maturidi presents a second argument for prophethood: the miracles of prophets are beyond human learning. Given that prophets were not trained in sorcery or magic, their ability to perform such signs can only come from God, establishing them as trustworthy messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read Maturidi's original argument carefully. Pay attention to the flow from the impossibility of learning miracles to the conclusion about divine origin.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Understand the key claim
Identify Maturidi's central claim: miracles are inherently unattainable through learning or effort. They are 'impossible to aspire to their like or to reach their depth through learning.'
This distinguishes prophets from sorcerers.
3
Connect upbringing to proof
Notice how Maturidi uses the prophets' upbringing—they were not raised in the arts of sorcery—to argue that their miracles must come directly from God. This makes them trustworthy as messengers.
The argument relies on background knowledge of the prophets' lives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني مجيء الآيات الخارجة عن طبائع أهل البصر في ذلك النوع، الممتنعة عن أن يُطمع في مثلها أو يبلغ بكنهها التعلم مع ما لو احتمل أن يبلغ أحد ذلك بالتعلم والاجتهاد فإن الرسل بما نشأوا لا في ذلك، وربوا لا به يظهر أنهم استفادوه بالله، أكرمهم بذلك؛ لما يجعلهم أمناء على وحيه.
And the second: the coming of signs that are outside the natures of people of insight in that type, impossible to aspire to their like or to reach their depth through learning, with what if it were possible for someone to reach that through learning and exertion, then the messengers, by virtue of how they were raised not in that, and nurtured not by it, it becomes apparent that they acquired it from God, He honored them with that in order to make them trustworthy over His revelation.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of prophethood. Maturidi argues that prophetic miracles are inherently unattainable through human learning or effort. If they could be learned, then given the prophets' upbringing—which was not in the arts of sorcery or magic—their possessing such signs would be suspicious. However, since they were not trained in such fields, their ability to perform these miracles clearly comes from God, establishing their trustworthiness as messengers.

Key Terms
آيات
التعلم
الاجتهاد
أمناء على وحيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets learned their miracles through diligent study or practice.
Correction: Maturidi argues that prophets were not raised in the arts of sorcery or magic, so their miracles cannot come from learning; they must be divine gifts.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the second proof demonstrate about prophets?
A) They learned miracles from earlier masters.
B) Their miracles come directly from God, not through learning.
C) They used sorcery to convince people.
D) They were born with all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Prophets' miracles cannot be learned; their lack of training proves divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something significant, do you first attribute it to your own effort or to God's gift? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider your gratitude? What is one thing you can do today to actively shift your acknowledgment to God?
?
The prophets were not trained in miracles, yet God enabled them. Where in your life do you feel untrained or inadequate, but sense God might still use you? What step of trust can you take this week relying on His power, not your preparation?
?
If God honored prophets with miracles to establish trustworthiness, how might He be signaling His presence in your life through unexpected provisions? Are you paying attention, or dismissing them as coincidence? Commit to writing down one such divine sign this week and thank Him for it.

Five Signs Distinguishing Prophets from Sorcerers
Maturidi enumerates five qualities that differentiate prophetic miracles from sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five criteria that distinguish prophetic miracles from sorcery, and why these criteria demonstrate the truth of prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles are real, enduring, and selfless; sorcery is illusory, fleeting, and worldly.

Imam Maturidi provides a powerful set of criteria to distinguish between the miracles of prophets and the tricks of sorcerers. This passage is crucial for defending prophethood against skeptics who might confuse the two. Let's explore each of the five signs carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the structure
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing the introductory remark and the enumeration of five signs.
The author uses 'أحدها', 'والثاني', etc.
2
Understand the first two signs
Focus on the reality and endurance of prophetic miracles versus the illusory nature of sorcery.
Contrast permanence with fading.
3
Examine the third and fourth signs
Note the worldly motives of sorcerers versus the self-denial of prophets.
The fourth sign involves calling others to abandon pleasures for God.
4
Reflect on the fifth sign
Consider the risk of life and weakness, demonstrating sincerity.
This sign shows that prophets act against their own worldly interests.
5
Apply the criteria
Think about how these criteria help us evaluate claims of prophethood.
These are rational checks, not just blind faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم أيضاً معان فاقوا بها السحرة. على أن علم السحر أصله من السماء، لكن الناس نسوا أصله وتوارثوه بالتعلم، وكذلك المكاسب والحرف والصناعات كلها. فمن أُكرِم لا بالوجه الذي هو طريقه في المعارف علم أن ذلك تخصيص لأمر عظيم. مع ما كان معهم معان يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون؛ أحدها أنها تخرج حقيقة وتبقى ببقاء الخلقة، والسحر هو شيء يأخذ البصر ثم يضمحل. والثاني أن آية الرسل تمنع أن يدعيها من ليس برسول فتبقى معه بأن كانت في جهة سحرًا، وما كان. والثالث أن أولئك الذين تكلفوا استخراج العجائب بالتعلم فهم قد مالوا إلى شيء لو كان حقا لكان به غنى من عرض الدنيا، فكان معهم دليل الكذب. والرابع أن الرسل حملوا ما في الأنفس إنكاره، ذلك من كفها عن الملاذ والشهوات وحفظها عن الذين بهم عز الدنيا وشرفها ودعاء أمثالهم إلى ترك ذلك الله. والخامس مخاطرتهم بالأنفس وبذلها في وقت ضعفهم وقلة أنصارهم من الخلق، والتعرض للجبارين بتنغيص ما هم فيه عليهم، وإظهار القوة لهم من عند العزيز. على ما علموا من سوء صنيعهم بالمخالفين لهم وبخاصة من يخافون منهم تفريق جمعهم وتشتيت أمورهم.
And they also have meanings by which they surpassed the sorcerers. That the science of sorcery originally came from heaven, but people forgot its origin and inherited it through learning, and likewise all acquisitions, crafts, and industries. So whoever is honored not through the way that is the path in knowledge knows that that is a specification for a great matter. Along with that, they had meanings by which it is known that they are sent: First, that they come forth as reality and remain with the permanence of creation, while sorcery is something that seizes the sight then fades away. Second, that the sign of the messengers prevents anyone who is not a messenger from claiming it, so it remains with him only if it were in the direction of sorcery, and it was not. Third, that those who undertook to extract wonders through learning inclined to something that, if it were true, would be sufficient for the goods of this world, so with them was the evidence of falsehood. Fourth, that the messengers bore what is inherently denied, that is restraining it from pleasures and desires and preserving it from those by whom the honor and glory of this world are maintained, and calling their like to abandon that for God. Fifth, their risking and sacrificing their souls at a time of their weakness and few supporters among people, and exposing themselves to the tyrants by spoiling what they have, and manifesting power to them from the Almighty, according to what they knew of the evil treatment of those who opposed them, especially those whom they feared would disperse their assembly and scatter their affairs.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage lists five distinguishing qualities (معان) of prophetic miracles. Maturidi first notes that sorcery is originally from heaven but was forgotten and passed down through learning. Prophets, however, receive their signs directly from God. The five signs are: (1) prophetic miracles are real and enduring, unlike sorcery which is illusory; (2) a prophetic sign cannot be successfully claimed by a false prophet, whereas sorcery can; (3) sorcerers seek worldly gain, while prophets do not; (4) prophets call to rejection of worldly pleasures, not to indulgence; (5) prophets risk their lives despite weakness, showing their sincerity.

Key Terms
معان
السحرة
آية الرسل
مخاطرتهم بالأنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All extraordinary acts are the same, so prophets and sorcerers cannot be distinguished.
Correction: This passage provides clear criteria: prophetic miracles are real, enduring, not falsely claimable, selfless, and involve sacrifice, unlike sorcery which is illusory, fleeting, and worldly.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five signs distinguishing prophets from sorcerers?
A) Prophetic miracles are enduring, sorcery is fleeting.
B) Prophets never perform miracles in public.
C) Sorcerers seek worldly gain.
D) Prophets risk their lives.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic miracles are genuine, enduring, and selfless, setting them apart from all forms of sorcery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The prophets abandoned pleasures and called others to do the same for God. What specific pleasure or desire are you unwilling to give up, and why? Will you commit to at least reducing its hold on you this week?
?
Imagine you are weak and few support you, yet you must stand against tyrants for the truth—as the prophets did. What fear is currently preventing you from speaking up for truth in your own life? Write down one concrete step you will take this month to overcome that fear for God's sake.

Moral Excellence of the Prophets
Maturidi enumerates prophetic virtues and argues from their perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the prophets' perfect moral character and virtues as a proof of their prophethood.

Core Idea
The prophets' complete virtues and patience make falsity inconceivable.

Maturidi continues his proofs for prophethood by examining the character of the prophets themselves. He argues that their flawless virtues, combined with their sincere performance for God and patience in hardship, make it impossible that they would resort to flattery or falsehood. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two 'also' statements that introduce the proof.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the prophetic virtues listed
The passage lists several virtues: generosity, courage, noble morals, mercy, asceticism, and others. Make note of each one.
These are the 'complete' qualities.
3
Understand the argument from combination
Maturidi argues that one who combines all these virtues, performs for God, and patiently endures harm cannot be suspected of seeking deliverance by flattery. Why is that?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the prophets' character alone proves their truthfulness. Consider how this argument works.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنهم دعوا إلى ما في العقول بيانه، وفي سياسات الملك حسنه، وبما في توقيف الخلق عليه صلاحهم دينا ودنيا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إنهم لم يقصروا في شيء دعوا إليه اجتهادا، ولا روى في شيء من أمورهم هوادة، ولا عرف في شيء من أخلاقهم نكير، ولا في شيء من الأسباب التي بكل واحد - مما فيها بعد الناس بذلك - ما يوصف بالتمام: من السخاء والشجاعة ومكارم الأخلاق والرحمة بالخلق والإشفاق عليهم، ومن الزهادة في الدنيا وتحمل مؤن الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يحق الميل إلى كل من فيه خصلة منها والتعظيم له لمكان ذلك؛ فكيف لمن جمع الخصال المعروفة في المكارم، مع حسن الأداء عن الله جل ثناؤه، والصبر له فيما يصيبه من المكروه مما لا يُحتمل أن يكون شيء من ذلك يحتمل على تمكين الخلاص منه ببعض المداهنة. وفيهم أيضاً وعد العواقب ورجوع الأمر إليهم، فخرج الأمر على ذلك. وفيهم أنه لم يُذكر عن أحد نظر إليهم بعين التبجيل واستمع إليهم بالنصح لأنفسهم إلا أبصر الحق في مقالتهم، ولا اتبعهم أحد فخالفهم إلا بعد العلم منه بإيثاره الدنيا على الآخرة والباطل على الحق.
And also, they called to what is clear in intellects, good in policies of kings, and what by instructing creation brings about their well-being in religion and world. And there is no power except with God. And also, they did not fall short in any matter they called to through exertion, nor was leniency reported in any of their affairs, nor was any blame known in any of their morals, nor in any of the causes that are in each one—among which people differ thereby—what is described as complete: from generosity, courage, noble morals, mercy to creation, compassion towards them, and from asceticism in the world and bearing the burdens of creation, and other things that make it right to incline to everyone in whom there is a quality from them and to glorify him for that reason; so how about the one who combined the well-known qualities in noble traits, with good performance on behalf of God, glory be to His praise, and patience for Him in what befalls him of harm, such that it is not conceivable that any of that could be tolerated to enable deliverance from it by some flattery? And in them also is the promise of consequences and the return of the matter to them, and the matter came out accordingly. And in them is that it was not reported about anyone who looked at them with an eye of veneration and listened to them with sincere advice for themselves except that he saw the truth in their words, and no one followed them then opposed them except after knowing from himself that he prefers the world over the hereafter and falsehood over truth.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents additional proofs of prophethood from the prophets' character and the response of their followers. First, they call to rational truths and good governance, which are beneficial for religion and world. Second, they are perfect in every virtue: generosity, courage, noble morals, mercy, asceticism, etc. Their combination of all virtues, along with their patience and performance for God, makes it impossible that they would seek deliverance through flattery. Third, they promise that they will ultimately prevail. Fourth, sincere observers see the truth in their words, while those who oppose them do so knowingly, preferring the world.

Key Terms
السخاء
الشجاعة
مكارم الأخلاق
الزهادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophets are ordinary people with no special moral character.
Correction: This passage shows that prophets possess complete and unparalleled moral virtues, making them extraordinary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what makes it inconceivable that prophets would resort to flattery?
A) Their miracles
B) Their combination of all virtues, performance for God, and patience
C) Their large number of followers
D) Their political power

Key Takeaway
The prophets' complete virtues and patience prove that they cannot be false messengers of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Testimony of Sincere Followers
Maturidi presents the promise of victory and the testimonial response of followers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the response of sincere followers as evidence for prophethood.

Core Idea
Sincere listeners see the truth; opposers knowingly choose falsehood.

Maturidi also appeals to history: the prophets were given the promise of ultimate success, and those who truly listened to them recognized the truth. Those who opposed did so knowingly, preferring the world over the hereafter. This lesson examines that testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two observations: the promise of victory and the response of people.
2
Understand the promise of consequences
The passage states that the prophets were given the promise that the final outcome and authority would return to them.
3
Analyze the response of sincere listeners
Anyone who looked at the prophets with veneration and listened sincerely saw the truth in their words.
This is a historical testimony.
4
Contrast with the opposers
Those who followed them then opposed did so only after knowing that they preferred the world over the hereafter and falsehood over truth.
Opposers are not ignorant; they knowingly reject.

The Quran as a Lasting Miracle of Muhammad
Maturidi highlights the Quran as a lasting miracle for Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran serves as a permanent miracle for Muhammad's prophethood, both through its inimitability and its comprehensive guidance for all future events.

Core Idea
The Quran's challenge and its comprehensive rulings prove it is from the All-Knowing God.

Maturidi now applies the general framework of prophethood to the specific case of Muhammad. He points to the Quran as a miracle that remains until today, unlike the temporary miracles of earlier prophets. This lesson examines two key aspects of the Quran's miraculous nature: its challenge to mankind and its inclusion of rulings for all future events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the two main evidences Maturidi presents: the Quran's challenge and its comprehensive rulings.
The passage is part of a larger argument about prophethood.
2
Understand the challenge (tahaddi)
Focus on the phrase where the Quran challenges all disbelievers, jinn and humans, to produce something like it. Reflect on why this challenge is considered a miracle.
No one succeeded except a fool, as Maturidi says.
3
Recognize the comprehensive rulings
Maturidi states the Quran contains judgment for all events until the Day of Resurrection. This shows it comes from the One who knows the unseen and the future.
This is a second, often overlooked aspect of the miracle.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these two aspects together establish Muhammad as the seal of the prophets and the Quran as a divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل الذي ذكرت كان لمحمد صلى الله عليه مع غير ذلك من الآيات التي دامت له مما فيه إظهار نبوته، وأنه خاتم الأنبياء. منها هذا القرآن الذي تحدى به جميع الكفرة أن يأتوا بمثله وأن يعينهم على ذلك الجن والإنس، فما طمع في ذلك إلا سفيه أخرق هجره قومه لسخفه. وفيه أيضا بيان الحكم لجميع النوازل التي تحدث إلى يوم القيامة، ليُعلم أنه جاء من عند من يعلم الغيب وما يكون أبدا؛ وبيان ما جاء له من البشارات في فتح
And all that I mentioned was for Muhammad, peace be upon him, along with other signs that remained for him, in which is the manifestation of his prophethood, and that he is the seal of the prophets. Among them is this Quran, with which he challenged all the disbelievers to bring something like it and to be helped in that by jinn and humans, but no one aspired to that except a foolish, clumsy person whom his people abandoned for his foolishness. And in it also is the explanation of the judgment of all events that occur until the Day of Resurrection, so that it might be known that he came from the One who knows the unseen and what will be forever; and the explanation of the glad tidings that came to him concerning the conquest...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
Maturidi applies the general proofs of prophethood specifically to Muhammad. The Quran is a lasting miracle: it challenged all humans and jinn to produce its like, and no one succeeded except a fool. Additionally, the Quran contains rulings for all future events, indicating it comes from the All-Knowing God. These points together demonstrate that Muhammad is the seal of the prophets.

Key Takeaway

The Perfect Character of the Prophets as Proof
Maturidi enumerates prophetic virtues and argues for their evidential value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the flawless character of the prophets, combined with their divine mission and patience, as a powerful proof of their prophethood.

Core Idea
A prophet's moral perfection points to divine truth.

In this passage, Maturidi presents an argument based on the character of the prophets. He lists the virtues that make a person worthy of veneration and then shows that the prophets possessed all these virtues in the highest degree, especially in their service to Allah and their endurance of hardship without resorting to flattery.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the listed virtues
Note each virtue Maturidi mentions: generosity, courage, noble character, mercy, compassion, asceticism, bearing burdens.
3
Understand the rhetorical question
Maturidi asks: if one virtue merits respect, how much more the combination of all virtues with divine mission and patience? This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنهم لم يقصروا في شيء دعوا إليه اجتهادا، ولا روى في شيء من أمورهم هوادة، ولا عرف في شيء من أخلاقهم نكير، ولا في شيء من الأسباب التي بكل واحد مما فيها بعد الناس بذلك ما يوصف بالتمام: من السخاء والشجاعة ومكارم الأخلاق والرحمة بالخلق والإشفاق عليهم، ومن الزهادة في الدنيا وتحمل مؤن الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يحق الميل إلى كل من فيه خصلة منها والتعظيم له لمكان ذلك؛ فكيف لمن جمع الخصال المعروفة في المكارم، مع حسن الأداء عن الله جل ثناؤه، والصبر له فيما يصيبه من المكروه مما لا يُحتمل أن يكون شيء من ذلك يحتمل على تمكين الخلاص [منه] ببعض المداهنة. وفيهم أيضاً وعد العواقب ورجوع الأمر إليهم، فخرج الأمر على ذلك. وفيهم أنه لم يُذكر عن أحد نظر إليهم بعين التبجيل واستمع إليهم بالنصح لأنفسهم إلا أبصر الحق في مقالتهم، ولا اتبعهم أحد فخالفهم إلا بعد العلم منه بإيثاره الدنيا على الآخرة والباطل على الحق.
And also, they (the prophets) did not fall short in anything they called to with diligence, and no leniency was reported in any of their affairs, and no disapproval was known in any of their character, nor in any of the qualities that each one has—among which people differ—is described as complete: generosity, courage, noble character, mercy to creation, compassion for them, asceticism in the world, bearing the burdens of creation, and other things which make it right to incline towards anyone in whom there is one of these traits and to venerate him because of it. So how about one who combines all the known qualities of noble deeds, along with good performance on behalf of Allah, glory be to His praise, and patience for Him in what befalls him of hardship that cannot be borne to be delivered from it by some flattery? And also among them (the prophets) is the promise of good outcomes and the return of authority to them, so the matter turned out accordingly. And among them is that it is not reported that anyone looked at them with veneration and listened to them with sincere advice for themselves except that he perceived the truth in their statement, and no one followed them and then opposed them except after knowing that he prefers this world over the Hereafter and falsehood over truth.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the unimpeachable character of the prophets. Maturidi lists the perfect qualities that the prophets possessed: generosity, courage, noble character, mercy, compassion, asceticism, and bearing others' burdens. He argues that if a single one of these traits merits respect, then how much more does a person who combines them all, especially when he performs his duty from Allah and endures hardships without resorting to flattery? Furthermore, the prophets also had the promise of ultimate victory and the return of authority to them, which indeed occurred. Notably, anyone who looked at them with genuine veneration and listened sincerely always recognized the truth of their message. Opposition only came from those who consciously preferred this world over the afterlife.

Key Terms
السخاء
الشجاعة
مكارم الأخلاق
الرحمة
الزهادة
المداهنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Character alone cannot prove prophethood.
Correction: Maturidi does not rely on character in isolation; he combines it with divine mission and patience, making it a strong indicator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what makes a prophet deserving of respect?
A) Miracles only
B) Perfect character combined with divine mission and patience
C) Wealth and power
D) Eloquence

Key Takeaway
The prophets' perfect character, combined with their unwavering commitment to Allah's message, serves as a strong sign of their truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophet endured severe hardship without resorting to flattery or compromise. Think of a situation in your own life where you were tempted to compromise your faith to avoid difficulty. What would it mean for you to stand firm like the prophet?
?
If a single noble trait in a person earns respect, how much more does the prophet deserve your complete trust and obedience? Is there an area of your life where you hold back from fully following the prophet's example? What would it take to surrender that area?
?
Maturidi argues that the prophet's perfect character, combined with his mission, is undeniable proof of truth. When you reflect on the life of the Prophet Muhammad, what evidence of his character strikes you most personally? How does that evidence challenge your own doubts or negligence in following him?

The Response of People to the Prophets
Maturidi presents the fulfilled promise and the witness of sincere listeners as further proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's observation that sincere listeners always recognize the truth of the prophets, and that opposition comes only from those who prefer this world.

Core Idea
Sincere seekers find truth in the prophets; rejection stems from worldly attachment.

After presenting the character argument, Maturidi adds two more pieces of evidence: the promised good outcome for the prophets and the historical record of how people responded to them. This passage shows that anyone who looked at the prophets with genuine veneration recognized their truth, while opposition came only from those consciously choosing worldly gains over the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the additional proofs.
2
Identify the two additional points
First, the promise of good outcomes and return of authority to the prophets. Second, the observation about people's response.
3
Reflect on the implication
Maturidi uses these to show that the prophets' truth is self-evident to sincere observers, and opposition is not due to any flaw in their message.

The Qur'an as the Everlasting Miracle of Muhammad
Maturidi presents the Qur'an as the primary miracle of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi presents the Qur'an as the paramount miracle proving Muhammad's prophethood, through its challenge, content, and fulfillment of prophecies.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's challenge and content prove it is from Allah.

Maturidi begins by highlighting the Qur'an as the most enduring sign of prophethood. He emphasizes its challenge to all of humanity and jinn to produce a similar book, which they failed to meet. The Qur'an also contains rulings for all future events, prophecies of conquests, and news of the past—all of which could not have come from an unlettered man. Additionally, it was foretold in earlier scriptures, and the Prophet boldly challenged Jews and Christians, but they could not deny him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall sense. Notice the multiple angles Maturidi uses to argue for the Qur'an's divine origin.
The passage is dense with arguments.
2
Identify the main arguments
List the specific proofs: the challenge (tahaddi), the comprehensive knowledge of past and future, the foretelling in previous scriptures, and the Prophet's daring debates with the People of the Book.
Each of these is a separate line of evidence.
3
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
Consider why Maturidi presents multiple arguments together. How does each strengthen the case for the Qur'an's authenticity?
The combination makes the proof overwhelming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل الذي ذكرت كان لمحمد صلى الله عليه مع غير ذلك من الآيات التي دامت له مما فيه إظهار نبوته، وأنه خاتم الأنبياء. منها هذا القرآن الذي تحدى به جميع الكفرة أن يأتوا بمثله وأن يعينهم على ذلك الجن والإنس، فما طمع في ذلك إلا سفيه أخرق هجره قومه لسخفه. وفيه أيضا بيان الحكم لجميع النوازل [التي] تحدث إلى يوم القيامة، ليُعلم أنه جاء من عند من يعلم الغيب وما يكون أبدا؛ و[بيان] ما جاء له من البشارات في فتح البلدان، وإظهاره دينه بين أهل الأديان، وما فيه من الأنباء عما كان؛ مما يعلم الخلق أنه لم يكن اختلف إلى أحد ممن يعلم ذلك، ولا نظر في كتاب قط لتبقى له تلك الآيات. مع ما ذكر شأنه في الكتب السماوية [و] حاج أهل الكتاب فلم يمكنهم إنكاره إشفاقا على أنفسهم. بل قد باهلهم مباهلته اليهود بقوله: فتمنوا الموت [البقرة، 94]، والنصارى بقوله: تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم [آل عمران، 61] الآية، والجميع بقوله: فكيدوني جميعا ثم لا تنظرون، وإظهاره الأمن عنهم والثقة بالله بقوله: والله يعصمك من الناس [المائدة، 67]. مع ما له آيات في الخلق، وهو النور الذي انتقل من ظهر إلى ظهر حتى خرج هو؛ وما كان من الخاتم بين كتفيه؛ وما وصف بالربعة، ثم كان لا يزاحم طويلين إلا فاقهما؛ ثم من السحاب الذي يظله قبل أن يوحي إليه، ثم كان من شق بطنه وغسل ما فيه - معلوم ذلك - وردّه إلى موضعه؛ ثم كان من هجر عبادة الأوثان في صغره، مع حرص قومه.
And all that I have mentioned was for Muhammad, peace be upon him, along with other signs that remained for him, which manifest his prophethood and that he is the seal of the prophets. Among them is this Qur'an, with which he challenged all the disbelievers to produce something like it, and to seek help from jinn and men for that, but none aspired to that except a foolish, clumsy person whose people shunned him for his foolishness. And in it is also the explanation of the ruling for all incidents that occur until the Day of Resurrection, so that it might be known that he came from One Who knows the unseen and what will ever be; and [the explanation] of what came to him of glad tidings of the conquest of lands, and the manifestation of his religion among the people of religions, and what is in it of news of what has passed; such that the creation knows that he did not resort to anyone who knows that, nor did he ever look into a book so that those signs would remain for him. Along with what was mentioned of his mention in the heavenly books, and he argued with the People of the Book, and they could not deny him, out of concern for themselves. Rather, he challenged them: he challenged the Jews with His saying, 'So wish for death' [al-Baqara 2:94], and the Christians with His saying, 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons' [Aal 'Imran 3:61] the verse, and all with His saying, 'Then plot against me all together and give me no respite' [Hud 11:55], and his showing of safety from them and trust in Allah with His saying, 'And Allah will protect you from the people' [al-Ma'ida 5:67]. Along with what he has of signs in creation: the light that transferred from back to back until he emerged; and the seal between his shoulders; and he was described as of medium height, but he would not stand beside tall people except that he would surpass them; and then the cloud that shaded him before revelation came to him; and then the splitting of his chest and washing of what was inside—that is known—and its return to its place; and then his abandonment of idol worship in his childhood, despite his people's eagerness.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the specific proofs for Muhammad's prophethood. First and foremost is the Qur'an, which challenged all of humanity and jinn to produce a similar book, yet none succeeded except to expose their own folly. The Qur'an contains knowledge of all events until Judgment Day, showing it is from the Knower of the Unseen. It also contains prophecies about the conquest of lands and the spread of Islam, which were fulfilled. Moreover, previous scriptures had mentioned his coming, and he argued with the People of the Book, who could not deny him out of fear. He even challenged them openly. Additionally, there are physical and historical signs: the light that passed through noble generations until he was born, the seal of prophethood between his shoulders, his physical superiority, the cloud that shaded him before revelation, the miraculous opening of his chest, and his innate avoidance of idolatry from childhood. All these constitute cumulative evidence.

Key Terms
القرآن
التحدي
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'anic challenge was only rhetorical and not a serious test.
Correction: Maturidi treats it as a real challenge that no one could meet, exposing the disbelievers' inability.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does Maturidi NOT mention as a proof from the Qur'an?
A) The challenge to produce a similar book.
B) The Qur'an's predictions about future conquests.
C) The Qur'an's detailed descriptions of the Prophet's physical appearance.
D) The Qur'an's mention in earlier scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an serves as an everlasting miracle through its inimitability, comprehensive knowledge, and fulfillment of prophecies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Qur'an challenges you directly to bring something like it. Have you ever taken this challenge seriously, or do you read it casually without confronting its divine claim? What would it take for you to truly examine it as the ultimate proof?
?
You face countless incidents and decisions every day. The Qur'an claims to explain rulings for 'all incidents until the Day of Resurrection'. Do you turn to it first for guidance, or do you consult other sources more? What does your daily habit reveal about your belief in its comprehensiveness?
?
The Prophet put his full trust in Allah's protection while challenging powerful enemies. In which area of your life are you afraid to stand for the truth because you fear people's reactions? Will you genuinely rely on Allah's promise of protection and speak boldly, as the Prophet did?

The Personal and Physical Signs of Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates personal and physical signs of Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the personal and physical signs that Maturidi lists as additional evidence of Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
Even the Prophet's physical traits and life events testify to his prophethood.

After discussing the Qur'an, Maturidi turns to personal signs witnessed in the Prophet himself. These include the light of prophethood passed through generations, the seal of prophethood on his back, his physical superiority, the cloud that shaded him, the miraculous cleansing of his chest, and his innate abandonment of idolatry. These signs, known to his companions, reinforce the cumulative proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note how each sign is presented succinctly as an established fact.
These signs are meant to be known and accepted.
2
List each sign
Identify each sign: the light, the seal, his stature, the cloud, the chest splitting, and his avoidance of idolatry.
Each is a distinct miracle.
3
Consider the cumulative effect
Reflect on how these personal signs, together with the Qur'an, form a strong case for prophethood. Why does Maturidi mention them all?
They cover different aspects of his life.

?
