Prophethood
beginner
5 min
The Signs of Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates the many miraculous signs supporting the Prophet's truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the many miraculous signs that prove the truthfulness of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) as enumerated by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
The Prophet's truthfulness is confirmed by a multitude of signs, from scriptural prophecies to physical miracles and noble character.

Imam Maturidi presents a comprehensive list of proofs for the Prophet's prophethood. These include challenges to the People of the Book, physical signs like the seal between his shoulders, and his impeccable character. This passage shows that the evidence is not limited to one type but is abundant and diverse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the variety of signs mentioned.
Pay attention to the structure: it starts with scriptural challenges, then lists physical and historical signs, then character, then reactions.
2
Identify the categories of signs
The signs fall into groups: challenges to Jews and Christians, physical signs (light, seal, stature, cloud, chest), actions (abandoning idols, prayer for rain), character (no flattery, no obscenity), and the testimony of enemies that he never lied.
Make a mental note of each category; they are all separate proofs.
3
Understand why each sign is a proof
For example, the fact that he challenged the People of the Book to wish for death shows he had divine backing. The physical signs like the seal are well-known traditions. His character was spotless even in the eyes of enemies.
These are not just stories; they are historical attestations (Maturidi says 'معلوم ذلك' – that is known).
4
Analyze the reaction of the opponents
Maturidi notes that the signs were so abundant that opponents accused him of sorcery, soothsaying, and poetry. This itself is a sign of the many miracles.
The abundance of signs caused confusion among them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما ذكر شأنه في الكتب السماوية، وحاج أهل الكتاب فلم يمكنهم إنكاره إشفاقا على أنفسهم. بل قد باهلهم مباهلته اليهود بقوله: فتمنوا الموت [البقرة، 94/2]، والنصارى بقوله: تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم [آل عمران، 61/3] الآية، والجميع بقوله: فكيدوني جميعا ثم لا تنظرون [هود، 55/11]، وإظهاره الأمن عنهم والثقة بالله بقوله: والله يعصمك من الناس [المائدة، 67/5]. مع ما له آيات في الخلق، وهو النور الذي انتقل من ظهر إلى ظهر حتى خرج هو؛ وما كان من الخاتم بين كتفيه؛ وما وصف بالربعة، ثم كان لا يزاحم طويلين إلا فاقهما؛ ثم من السحاب الذي يظله قبل أن يوحي إليه، ثم كان من شق بطنه وغسل ما فيه - معلوم ذلك - ورده إلى موضعه؛ ثم كان من هجر عبادة الأوثان في صغره، مع حرص قومه على ذلك، وما استسقى به العباس فسقوا. ثم ما وصف من معاملته الكفرة أنه لم يكن يدارى ولا يمارى؛ ولم يكن فحاشاً ولا صحابًا. ثم ما لم يأخذوا عليه كذبًا قط، وبذلك وصفه أعداؤه. ثم ما جاء من الآيات التي صارت سببًا لما اختلفوا فيه، فعرفوه بالسحر والكهانة والشعر ونحو ذلك، فما كان إلا لكثرة آياته. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Along with what mentioned his mention in the heavenly books, and he argued with the People of the Book, and they could not deny him out of fear for themselves. Indeed, he challenged them with his challenge to the Jews by his saying: 'So wish for death' [2:94], and to the Christians by his saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons' [3:61] the verse, and to all by his saying: 'So plot against me, all of you, then do not give me respite' [11:55], and his showing security from them and trust in Allah by his saying: 'And Allah will protect you from the people' [5:67]. Along with what he has of signs in creation: the light that transferred from loin to loin until he came out; and what was of the seal between his shoulders; and what was described of moderate stature, then he would not compete with tall men except he surpassed them; then the cloud that shaded him before revelation came to him; then the splitting of his belly and washing what was in it – known that – and returning it to its place; then his abandonment of idol worship in his youth, despite his people's zeal for it; and his prayer for rain through al-Abbas and they were watered. Then what was described of his dealing with the disbelievers: that he would neither flatter nor dispute; he was not obscene nor a companion of evil. Then that they never accused him of lying, and even his enemies described him thus. Then the signs that came which became a cause for what they differed about, so they recognized him as sorcery, soothsaying, poetry, and the like – that was only because of the abundance of his signs. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the miraculous signs (آيات) that prove the truthfulness of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Maturidi begins by noting that the Prophet's mention occurred in the heavenly scriptures, and he challenged the People of the Book (Jews and Christians) to accept him, but they could not deny him out of fear. He then describes specific challenges: the challenge to wish for death (for Jews), to call their sons (for Christians), and to plot against him (for all). These show his confidence in divine protection. Next, Maturidi lists physical and historical signs: the light that passed through generations until his birth, the seal of prophethood between his shoulders, his moderate stature that outmatched tall men, a cloud shading him before revelation, the splitting of his chest and washing of his heart (a known event), his abandonment of idolatry despite his people's pressure, his prayer for rain through his uncle al-Abbas. Then he describes his noble character: he did not flatter or argue needlessly, was not obscene or evil. Even his enemies never accused him of lying. Finally, the signs were so abundant that his opponents attributed them to sorcery, soothsaying, or poetry, but that very attribution shows the excessive number of his signs. The passage concludes with a reliance on Allah's power.

Key Terms
آيات (signs)
النور (the light)
الخاتم (the seal)
الربعة (moderate stature)
السحاب (the cloud)
شق بطنه (splitting of his belly)
هجر عبادة الأوثان (abandonment of idol worship)
استسقى (prayer for rain)
كذب (lying)
آياته (his signs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these signs are merely legendary or unverified stories.
Correction: Maturidi presents them as well-known and attested facts (e.g., 'معلوم ذلك' – 'that is known').

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the People of the Book find it difficult to deny the Prophet?
A) Because they had no knowledge of him.
B) Because they feared for themselves (إشفاقا على أنفسهم).
C) Because they were convinced by his miracles.
D) Because they wanted to avoid conflict.

Key Takeaway
The multitude and diversity of the Prophet's signs—from scriptural prophecies to physical miracles and impeccable character—collectively establish his truthfulness beyond doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the Prophet's signs—scriptural, physical, or character—moves you most? How will you keep this sign in your mind and heart starting today?
?
The Prophet's enemies never accused him of lying, yet you may struggle to speak truth in difficult situations. When is the next time you will choose honesty at personal cost?
?
The Prophet abandoned idol worship despite his people's pressure. What social pressure do you face, and what 'idol' of status, approval, or comfort are you called to abandon for Allah? What is your first step?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Argument for Prophethood
Ibn al-Rawandi's proof for prophethood using tawatur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that the necessity of accepting mutawatir reports leads to accepting prophetic reports.

Core Idea
To avoid ignorance, we must accept tawatur, which entails prophethood.

Ibn al-Rawandi presents a foundational epistemological argument: without accepting tawatur, we would be ignorant of past events. But once we accept it, we must also accept the reports of messengers. This is the backbone of his proof for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how Ibn al-Rawandi uses an analogy from food and poisons.
2
Identify the two-part dilemma
Ibn al-Rawandi presents a dilemma: either reject all reports (leading to ignorance) or accept tawatur (which implies prophethood). Identify both horns.
3
Consider the refutation context
Think about why Maturidi would find this argument insufficient. The next lesson will show his refutation based on the Qur'an.
This connects to the broader discussion of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج ابن الروندي في إثبات الرسالة ورده على الورّاق
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : احتج ابن الروندي بما تقدم من الأغذية والسموم في إثبات الرسالة. ثم قال: لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر إما أن لا يثبت ألبتة، فيجب الجهل بالأيام الماضية والأماكن النائية والوقائع السالفة، أو نقبل التواتر وما يُضطر إليه فيجب به أخبار الرسل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم نذكر جمل ما يبين فساد طعنه من وجوه الحجج بالقرآن، إذ هي من وجوه. أحدها بنظمه من غير أن كان فيه غريب مبتدع يخرج ذلك عن عرف العرب، بل هو بأعذب لفظ وأملح نظم، وقد احتملت العرب المؤن التي هلكوا فيها ولا يحتمل ترك الأمر اليسير مع التحدى والتقريع، مع سلامة أحب الأشياء إليهم وهي الحياة، وتبذل المهج مع ضنّهم بها إلا عن عجز ظهر لهم من أنفسهم طباعًا أو امتحانًا
The argument of Ibn al-Rawandi in proving the message and his refutation of al-Warraq
The shaykh said, may God have mercy on him: Ibn al-Rawandi argued on the basis of what preceded about foods and poisons in proving the message. Then he said: The matter of report is not free from either not being established at all, resulting in ignorance of past days, distant places, and previous events, or we accept the mutawatir (tawātur) and what is necessary, and thus the reports of the messengers must be accepted. And there is no power except with God. Then we will mention the sum of what shows the corruption of his criticism from the aspects of proofs by the Qur'an, for they are many. One of them: by its composition, without there being in it any strange innovation that takes it outside the custom of the Arabs; rather it is in the sweetest expression and most pleasant composition. And the Arabs bore hardships in which they perished, but they could not bear to abandon the easy matter despite the challenge and rebuke, while saving the dearest thing to them, which is life, and sacrificing lives despite their stinginess with them except due to an incapacity that appeared to them from themselves by nature or by testing.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage introduces a section on Ibn al-Rawandi's argument for prophethood and Maturidi's refutation. First, Maturidi reports that Ibn al-Rawandi used an analogy from food and poisons to prove prophethood—presumably that just as we accept knowledge of harmful foods through tradition, we should accept prophetic reports. Ibn al-Rawandi then presents a dilemma: either we accept no reports at all (leading to ignorance), or we accept mutawatir reports and thus must accept the reports of messengers. Maturidi then begins his refutation by stating he will show the corruption of this criticism through proofs from the Qur'an. He mentions one such proof: the Qur'an's composition is uniquely eloquent and follows Arabic custom, yet the Arabs, despite their love of life and willingness to sacrifice everything, could not produce a similar text, proving their incapacity.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawātur)
إثبات الرسالة (ithbāt al-risālah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that accepting tawatur is optional or that it can be rejected without consequence.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi argues that rejecting tawatur leads to absurd ignorance, so rationality demands its acceptance.

Checkpoint
What is the core structure of Ibn al-Rawandi's argument as presented by Maturidi?
A) The Qur'an is unique in style.
B) We must accept mutawatir reports, so we accept prophethood.
C) The Arabs were unable to produce like the Qur'an.
D) Prophethood is proven by miracles.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi argues that rationality demands accepting tawatur, which in turn necessitates acknowledging prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly open to accepting the truth of prophethood based on the overwhelming evidence of tawatur, or do you allow personal biases to blind you to the clear signs?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth simply because it came through transmission, without examining the reliability of the chain? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on your own limited experience?
?
If you accept that tawatur is necessary for knowledge of history, why do you hesitate when it comes to the reports of the messengers? What fear holds you back from surrendering to the truth?

Maturidi's Refutation: The Qur'an's Inimitability
Maturidi begins his refutation using the Qur'an's composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi by pointing to the Qur'an's miraculous composition as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's incomparable eloquence proves the incapacity of the Arabs and thus the truth of Muhammad's prophethood.

After presenting Ibn al-Rawandi's argument, Maturidi begins his refutation by highlighting the Qur'an itself as a miracle. He argues that the Arabs, despite their love of life and willingness to sacrifice, could not produce a text like the Qur'an, indicating divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Qur'an's composition
Focus on how Maturidi describes the Qur'an's style and the Arabs' reaction.
Note that the Qur'an follows Arabic custom but is uniquely eloquent.
2
Understand the challenge and incapacity
The Arabs were challenged (tahaddi) to produce something like it, but they couldn't, despite their eloquence and sacrifices.
This shows their incapacity was not due to lack of effort.
3
Connect to refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi
Maturidi uses the Qur'an's inimitability to ground prophethood in a concrete, undeniable proof.
Thus, the proof for prophethood does not solely rely on tawatur but on direct miraculous evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج ابن الروندي في إثبات الرسالة ورده على الورّاق
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : احتج ابن الروندي بما تقدم من الأغذية والسموم في إثبات الرسالة. ثم قال: لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر إما أن لا يثبت ألبتة، فيجب الجهل بالأيام الماضية والأماكن النائية والوقائع السالفة، أو نقبل التواتر وما يُضطر إليه فيجب به أخبار الرسل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم نذكر جمل ما يبين فساد طعنه من وجوه الحجج بالقرآن، إذ هي من وجوه. أحدها بنظمه من غير أن كان فيه غريب مبتدع يخرج ذلك عن عرف العرب، بل هو بأعذب لفظ وأملح نظم، وقد احتملت العرب المؤن التي هلكوا فيها ولا يحتمل ترك الأمر اليسير مع التحدى والتقريع، مع سلامة أحب الأشياء إليهم وهي الحياة، وتبذل المهج مع ضنّهم بها إلا عن عجز ظهر لهم من أنفسهم طباعًا أو امتحانًا
The argument of Ibn al-Rawandi in proving the message and his refutation of al-Warraq
The shaykh said, may God have mercy on him: Ibn al-Rawandi argued on the basis of what preceded about foods and poisons in proving the message. Then he said: The matter of report is not free from either not being established at all, resulting in ignorance of past days, distant places, and previous events, or we accept the mutawatir (tawātur) and what is necessary, and thus the reports of the messengers must be accepted. And there is no power except with God. Then we will mention the sum of what shows the corruption of his criticism from the aspects of proofs by the Qur'an, for they are many. One of them: by its composition, without there being in it any strange innovation that takes it outside the custom of the Arabs; rather it is in the sweetest expression and most pleasant composition. And the Arabs bore hardships in which they perished, but they could not bear to abandon the easy matter despite the challenge and rebuke, while saving the dearest thing to them, which is life, and sacrificing lives despite their stinginess with them except due to an incapacity that appeared to them from themselves by nature or by testing.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage introduces a section on Ibn al-Rawandi's argument for prophethood and Maturidi's refutation. First, Maturidi reports that Ibn al-Rawandi used an analogy from food and poisons to prove prophethood—presumably that just as we accept knowledge of harmful foods through tradition, we should accept prophetic reports. Ibn al-Rawandi then presents a dilemma: either we accept no reports at all (leading to ignorance), or we accept mutawatir reports and thus must accept the reports of messengers. Maturidi then begins his refutation by stating he will show the corruption of this criticism through proofs from the Qur'an. Key Terms
نظم (naẓm - composition)
تحدى (tahaddī - challenge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'an's inimitability is a matter of subjective preference.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is objectively proven by the historical fact that the Arabs, despite intense motivation, could not imitate it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's refutation, why could the Arabs not produce a text like the Qur'an?
A) They lacked motivation.
B) The Qur'an contained strange innovations.
C) They were inherently incapable, as shown by their failure despite the challenge.
D) They were forbidden by their leaders.

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' failure to produce a text like the Qur'an, despite their best efforts, demonstrates its divine origin and thus validates prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Qur'an, do you truly sense its inimitability, or has familiarity dulled your awareness of its miracle? What would it cost you to treat every verse as a direct, unprecedented revelation?
?
Maturidi mentions that the Arabs sacrificed their lives rather than face the 'easy matter' of imitating the Qur'an. What possessions, relationships, or comforts are you holding onto that prevent you from fully submitting to the Qur'an's authority?
?
The proof from incapacity demands that you either accept the Qur'an as divine or acknowledge your own inability to produce anything like it. If you truly believe it is from God, what specific change in your daily life will reflect that conviction starting today?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Argument: Tawātur as Proof of Prophethood
Maturidi presents Ibn al-Rawandi's logical argument for prophethood through tawātur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Rawandi's logical argument that accepting tawātur for historical knowledge necessitates accepting prophetic reports.

Core Idea
We cannot consistently reject prophethood without rejecting all historical knowledge.

Maturidi introduces Ibn al-Rawandi's argument which uses the principle of tawātur (mass transmission) to establish prophethood. Ibn al-Rawandi presents a dilemma: either we reject all reports about the past, leading to absurd ignorance, or we accept tawātur, which includes the reports of messengers. This section shows that denying prophethood is logically inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the two options presented by Ibn al-Rawandi.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر' – it sets up the dilemma.
2
Identify the two options
List the two possibilities: (1) reject all reports leading to ignorance, (2) accept tawātur and thus prophetic reports.
This is a classic logical dichotomy.
3
Understand tawātur
Recognize that tawātur refers to mass transmission that compels certainty. Maturidi uses it as a foundation for knowledge of the past.
Tawātur is a key concept in Islamic epistemology.
4
Connect to prophethood
See how accepting tawātur forces acceptance of prophetic reports since they are transmitted via tawātur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله على البديهة، فقد أمهلوا، مع ما لم يحتمل أن يكون من البشر أحد يعلم بفضل القوة ما تُسأل عنه. وقد تكلفوا الأشعار، ثم نصب الحروب وجمع الأعوان وبذل الأعيان ثم اقتتال الأقران والمبادرات الفظيعة، فلو كان وسعهم يحتمل القيام بذلك لكان أيسر عليهم. ثم قد دعوا إلى إتيان السورة نحو ثلاث آيات، لو احتملها وسع البشر لكان ساعة من النهار كافية لذلك. احتجاج ابن الروندي في إثبات الرسالة ورده على الوراق. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: احتج ابن الروندي بما تقدم من الأغذية والسموم في إثبات الرسالة. ثم قال: لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر إما أن لا يثبت ألبتة، فيجب الجهل بالأيام الماضية والأماكن النائية والوقائع السالفة، أو نقبل التواتر وما يُضطر إليه فيجب به أخبار الرسل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نذكر جمل ما يبين فساد طعنه من وجوه الحجج بالقرآن، إذ هي من وجوه. بنظمه من غير أن كان فيه غريب مبتدع يخرج ذلك عن عرف العرب، بل هو بأعذب لفظ وأملح نظم، وقد احتملت العرب المؤن التي هلكوا فيها ولا يحتمل ترك الأمر اليسير مع أحدها التحدي والتقريع، مع سلامة أحب الأشياء إليهم وهي الحياة، وتبذل المهج مع ضنّهم بها إلا عن عجز ظهر لهم من أنفسهم طباعًا أو امتحانًا.
And His saying about the improvising, they were given respite, along with what it is inconceivable that any human knows by virtue of power what he is asked about. And they undertook the composing of poems, then the setting up of wars, gathering supporters, sacrificing lives, then the fighting of peers and terrible confrontations. So if their capacity could bear undertaking that, it would have been easier for them. Then they were called to produce a single sūra of about three verses – if the capacity of humans could bear it, an hour of the day would have sufficed for that. The argument of Ibn al-Rawandi in establishing prophethood and his refutation of al-Warraq. The shaikh, may God have mercy on him, said: Ibn al-Rawandi argued with what was mentioned earlier about foods and poisons in establishing prophethood. Then he said: The matter regarding [prophetic] reports cannot be: either they are not established at all, in which case ignorance of past days, distant places, and past events becomes necessary; or we accept tawātur (mass transmission) and what is compelled [by necessity], and thus the reports of the messengers become necessary. And there is no power except with God. Then we will mention a summary that shows the corruption of his criticism from the various arguments of the Qur'an, for they are multiple. In its composition, there is no strange or innovated thing that takes it out of the customary usage of the Arabs; rather, it is in the sweetest wording and most beautiful composition. And the Arabs endured hardships in which they perished but could not bear to leave a small matter? The first: the challenge and rebuke, along with the safety of the most beloved things to them – life – and sacrificing lives despite their miserliness with them, except out of an inability that appeared to them from themselves, whether due to nature or trial.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is both a continuation of the argument about the Quranic challenge and a new section introducing Ibn al-Rawandi's argument for prophethood and its refutation. It begins by referencing the Arabs' inability to respond to the challenge despite their efforts in poetry and war. Then Maturidi quotes Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that either we accept nothing about the past, which is absurd, or we accept tawātur reports, which includes the reports of prophets. Maturidi then states he will refute al-Warraq's criticism using Quranic arguments. He first describes the Quran's composition as within Arab norms but unsurpassable. Then he presents the first proof: the taḥaddī (challenge) itself, noting that the Arabs valued life and were miserly with it, yet they faced death rather than produce something like the Quran, which shows their inability was genuine.

Key Terms
التواتر
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawātur only applies to secular history, not religious reports.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi argues that if we accept tawātur for historical events, we must also accept it for prophetic reports since they are transmitted in the same manner.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Rawandi's argument, if we accept tawātur reports about the past, what follows?
A) We must also accept reports of the messengers
B) We must reject all reports
C) We can accept some reports but not others arbitrarily
D) Only reports about food and poisons are valid

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi's dilemma shows that rejecting prophethood would require rejecting all historical knowledge based on tawātur, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you doubt the truth of prophethood, do you also doubt that George Washington existed? Why or why not? Be honest about your inconsistency.
?
Think of a time when you accepted a historical fact based on testimony. How is that different from accepting the reports of the prophets? Are you applying a double standard?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi's argument forces a choice. Have you truly accepted the tawātur of the prophetic message, or do you still harbor intellectual reservations? Commit to investigating one prophetic report today.

Prophethood

The Quranic Challenge: Proof from Arab Incapacity
Maturidi presents the first proof of prophethood: the Quranic challenge and the Arabs' inability to respond.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabs' inability to produce a sūra like the Quran, despite their efforts and sacrifices, proves the Quran's divine origin.

Core Idea
The Arabs' willingness to die rather than produce a text like the Quran proves their genuine inability.

Maturidi now presents the first Quranic argument against al-Warraq's criticism: the challenge (taḥaddī). He describes how the Arabs exerted immense efforts in poetry, wars, and sacrifices, yet could not produce a single sūra. Their failure, despite risking their lives, demonstrates that the Quran is beyond human capacity, confirming its divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the description of Arab efforts and the challenge.
Notice the contrast between their ability to wage wars and their inability to produce a sūra.
2
List the Arab efforts
Identify the activities Maturidi mentions: composing poems, waging wars, gathering supporters, sacrificing lives.
These show their capacity for difficult tasks.
3
Understand the challenge
The challenge was to produce a sūra of about three verses. Maturidi says if humans could do it, it would have been easy.
The brevity of the sūra emphasizes the ease of the task if it were humanly possible.
4
Analyze the significance of sacrifice
Maturidi notes that the Arabs were miserly with their lives, yet they sacrificed them rather than face the challenge, indicating their inability was real.
This is strong evidence because they would not have chosen death if they could have produced a sūra.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله على البديهة، فقد أمهلوا، مع ما لم يحتمل أن يكون من البشر أحد يعلم بفضل القوة ما تُسأل عنه. وقد تكلفوا الأشعار، ثم نصب الحروب وجمع الأعوان وبذل الأعيان ثم اقتتال الأقران والمبادرات الفظيعة، فلو كان وسعهم يحتمل القيام بذلك لكان أيسر عليهم. ثم قد دعوا إلى إتيان السورة نحو ثلاث آيات، لو احتملها وسع البشر لكان ساعة من النهار كافية لذلك. احتجاج ابن الروندي في إثبات الرسالة ورده على الوراق. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: احتج ابن الروندي بما تقدم من الأغذية والسموم في إثبات الرسالة. ثم قال: لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر إما أن لا يثبت ألبتة، فيجب الجهل بالأيام الماضية والأماكن النائية والوقائع السالفة، أو نقبل التواتر وما يُضطر إليه فيجب به أخبار الرسل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نذكر جمل ما يبين فساد طعنه من وجوه الحجج بالقرآن، إذ هي من وجوه. بنظمه من غير أن كان فيه غريب مبتدع يخرج ذلك عن عرف العرب، بل هو بأعذب لفظ وأملح نظم، وقد احتملت العرب المؤن التي هلكوا فيها ولا يحتمل ترك الأمر اليسير مع أحدها التحدي والتقريع، مع سلامة أحب الأشياء إليهم وهي الحياة، وتبذل المهج مع ضنّهم بها إلا عن عجز ظهر لهم من أنفسهم طباعًا أو امتحانًا.
And His saying about the improvising, they were given respite, along with what it is inconceivable that any human knows by virtue of power what he is asked about. And they undertook the composing of poems, then the setting up of wars, gathering supporters, sacrificing lives, then the fighting of peers and terrible confrontations. So if their capacity could bear undertaking that, it would have been easier for them. Then they were called to produce a single sūra of about three verses – if the capacity of humans could bear it, an hour of the day would have sufficed for that. The argument of Ibn al-Rawandi in establishing prophethood and his refutation of al-Warraq. The shaikh, may God have mercy on him, said: Ibn al-Rawandi argued with what was mentioned earlier about foods and poisons in establishing prophethood. Then he said: The matter regarding [prophetic] reports cannot be: either they are not established at all, in which case ignorance of past days, distant places, and past events becomes necessary; or we accept tawātur (mass transmission) and what is compelled [by necessity], and thus the reports of the messengers become necessary. And there is no power except with God. Then we will mention a summary that shows the corruption of his criticism from the various arguments of the Qur'an, for they are multiple. In its composition, there is no strange or innovated thing that takes it out of the customary usage of the Arabs; rather, it is in the sweetest wording and most beautiful composition. And the Arabs endured hardships in which they perished but could not bear to leave a small matter? The first: the challenge and rebuke, along with the safety of the most beloved things to them – life – and sacrificing lives despite their miserliness with them, except out of an inability that appeared to them from themselves, whether due to nature or trial.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is both a continuation of the argument about the Quranic challenge and a new section introducing Ibn al-Rawandi's argument for prophethood and its refutation. It begins by referencing the Arabs' inability to respond to the challenge despite their efforts in poetry and war. Then Maturidi quotes Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that either we accept nothing about the past, which is absurd, or we accept tawātur reports, which includes the reports of prophets. Maturidi then states he will refute al-Warraq's criticism using Quranic arguments. He first describes the Quran's composition as within Arab norms but unsurpassable. Then he presents the first proof: the taḥaddī (challenge) itself, noting that the Arabs valued life and were miserly with it, yet they faced death rather than produce something like the Quran, which shows their inability was genuine.

Key Terms
التحدي
النظم
الطعن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the challenge was only about literary eloquence and the Arabs could have attempted but chose not to.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that they did attempt but failed – they even risked death rather than admit failure, showing their inability was real.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi mention the Arabs' willingness to sacrifice their lives?
A) To show their bravery
B) To prove they were incapable of producing a sūra
C) To demonstrate their love for poetry
D) To criticize their miserliness

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' failure to produce a sūra, despite their efforts and sacrifices, proves the Quran is miraculous and divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Arabs chose death over admitting the Quran's divinity. Have you ever chosen comfort or reputation over surrendering to a truth you know? What would it cost you to fully submit to the Quran as divine?
?
If you were an Arab poet at the time of revelation, would you have recognized the Quran's miraculous nature, or would your pride have prevented you? Examine your own heart: where does your pride resist the clear signs of God in your life?
?
The Arabs' love for life was overcome by their stubbornness. What attachments in your life – status, possessions, relationships – keep you from responding to God's call with complete sincerity? What step can you take today to break that attachment?

Proof from the Prophet's Illiteracy
Maturidi states the second proof of prophethood based on the Prophet's illiteracy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's illiteracy and lack of contact with earlier scholars prove his knowledge came directly from Allah.

Core Idea
The Prophet's illiteracy proves his knowledge is from Allah, not human learning.

The second proof of prophethood from the Quran focuses on the Prophet's knowledge of things unknown to the scholars of the People of the Book. Since he was illiterate and never studied with them, his comprehensive knowledge could only have come from divine teaching. This lesson examines the logic of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof, then its translation. Notice the key claim: the Prophet's knowledge is not from human sources.
The passage references Quran 29:48 about the Prophet not reading books.
2
Identify the premises
The proof has two premises: (1) the scholars of the People of the Book knew certain matters, (2) the Prophet never studied with them nor wrote a book. The conclusion is that his knowledge was from Allah.
3
Understand the implication
This proof establishes the Prophet as a recipient of direct divine knowledge, which supports his prophethood.
Consider why illiteracy is a crucial factor here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني بيان جميع الأمور التي بها علم علماء أهل الكتاب مع العلم بمن شهد رسول الله أنه لم يكن اختلف إليهم ولا كان يخط كتابًا بيمينه فيحتمل استعادته، ثبت أن ذلك كان بتعليم الله إياه. والثالث الإخبار بما يكون له من الفتوح ودخول الخلق في دينه أفواجًا وإظهار دينه على الأديان في وقت ضعفه وقلة أعوانه وكثرة أعدائه فكان على ما أخبره القرآن. وبالله التوفيق. والرابع أن الله تعالى جمع في القرآن أصول جميع النوازل التي تكون إلى يوم القيامة، دل أنه عن عالم الغيب جاء حتى أعلمه أصول ذلك. وأيضًا ما أظهر من موافقة القرآن سائر كتب الله وبيان نعت محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وأمته كقوله: {الذي يجدونه مكتوبًا عندهم} الآية، وقوله: {محمد رسول الله} إلى آخره، وقوله: {يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم} الآية، من غير اجتراء أحد منهم على إنكار ذلك ودفعه. ثبت أن الذي أنزل هذه الكتب هو الله سبحانه فجعلها كلها متفقة على اختلاف الأزمنة وتباعد الأوقات؛ ليعلموا أن القرآن من عند من جاء منه الكتب، وأن الذي جاء منه الكتب قديم لم يزل ولا يزال حجته في الأولين والآخرين واحدًا. وأيضًا ما سبق من ذكر المباهلة وما كان من الأخبار أنه يُسأل كذا، فكان على ما ذكر على ما في القرآن من قصة الجن وتصديقهم وشهادتهم له بموافقة الكتب. وبالله العصمة.
And the second: the explanation of all the matters by which the scholars of the People of the Book came to know – along with the knowledge that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, never frequented them nor wrote a book with his right hand such that he could be suspected of learning from it – it is established that that was through the teaching of God, exalted is He. And the third: the informing of what would happen to him of conquests, the entering of people in multitudes into his religion, and the manifestation of his religion over other religions at a time of his weakness and few supporters and many enemies; it came about just as the Quran informed. And with God is success. And the fourth: that God, exalted, gathered in the Quran the principles of all the events that will occur until the Day of Resurrection; this indicates that it came from the Knower of the unseen, to the point that He informed him of the principles of that. And also what appeared of the Quran's agreement with the rest of God's books and the description of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and his community, such as His saying: 'whom they find written with them' [7:157], and His saying: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' [48:29] to its end, and His saying: 'they recognize him as they recognize their own sons' [2:146], with none of them daring to deny or reject that. It is established that the One who revealed these books is God, exalted, and He made them all consistent despite the difference of times and the distance of ages, so that they might know that the Quran is from the One from whom the [other] books came, and that the One from whom the books came is eternal, has not ceased and does not cease, His argument in the first and the last being one. And also what preceded of the mention of the mubāhala (mutual imprecation) and what was of reports that he would be asked such and such, and it was as mentioned, and what is in the Quran of the story of the jinn and their confirmation and their testimony for him confirming the books. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the second, third, and fourth proofs of prophethood from the Quran. The second proof is based on the Prophet's illiteracy: the scholars of the People of the Book knew things unknown to others, but the Prophet never studied with them nor wrote, so his knowledge must have come from divine teaching. This references Quran 29:48. The third proof is the accurate prophecy of future events: the conquests, people entering Islam in multitudes, and the domination of Islam over other religions despite initial weakness – all came true as predicted. The fourth proof is that the Quran contains the principles of all future events, indicating it came from the Knower of the unseen. Then Maturidi adds an argument from the agreement of the Quran with previous scriptures and the recognition of the Prophet's description in them, with none of the People of the Book daring to deny it. This consistency across time shows they are from the same divine source. He concludes with a reference to the story of the jinn who testified to the truth of the Quran, confirming the message.

Key Terms
أهل الكتاب
تعليم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Prophet acquired knowledge from earlier scriptures through study.
Correction: The passage emphasizes his illiteracy and lack of contact with scholars, so study is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what proves that the Prophet's knowledge was from Allah?
A) He memorized many books
B) He was illiterate and never studied with scholars
C) He traveled widely to learn
D) The People of the Book taught him

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's illiteracy, combined with his comprehensive knowledge, proves he received divine teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe the Quran is from Allah. But when your faith wavers, do you ever recall this proof – that an illiterate man produced knowledge no scholar could match? What keeps you from letting this evidence solidify your certainty?
?
Think of a time you doubted Islam's truth. Could this logical proof – the Prophet's illiteracy combined with his profound knowledge – have silenced that doubt? If you had truly internalized this argument, would you still struggle with uncertainty?
?
The scholars of the People of the Book spent years studying, yet the Prophet surpassed them without any human teacher. What does this say about the source of true knowledge? Are you seeking guidance from the same divine source, or are you relying solely on human intellect and books?

Proof from Fulfilled Future Prophecies
Maturidi presents the third proof: accurate future prophecies in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's accurate predictions of future conquests and the spread of Islam serve as a proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Fulfilled prophecies prove the Quran is from the Knower of the unseen.

The third proof of prophethood from the Quran involves predictions about future events. The Quran foretold conquests, mass conversions to Islam, and its domination over other religions – all while the Prophet was weak and outnumbered. These prophecies came true exactly as stated, demonstrating divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the third proof and its translation. Focus on the elements predicted: conquests, multitudes entering religion, and dominance of Islam.
Note the contrast between the initial weakness and the eventual victory.
2
Analyze the prediction vs reality
Consider that at the time of revelation, Muslims were few and weak. The prophecy of widespread conquests and mass conversions was unlikely by human estimation, yet it happened exactly as foretold.
This parallels the concept of i'jaz (miracle) in prophethood.
3
Draw the conclusion
The accurate fulfillment proves that the message came from Allah, who knows the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or does your despair override your certainty? What one concrete act of trust can you commit to today?
?
The Quran's prophecies came true exactly as stated, yet many Muslims today live as if the Quran's promises for this world and the next are uncertain. Where in your own life do you act as if Allah's words might fail? What would change if you lived with absolute certainty that every prediction in the Quran will come to pass?
?
This proof shows that Allah knows the future perfectly. But do you live like He knows your future and has already prepared success for you if you follow His path? Or do you plan your life as if you are in control? What practical step can you take tonight to surrender your future plans to His knowledge and wisdom?

Proof from the Quran's Comprehensiveness and Agreement
Maturidi enumerates the fourth proof: the Quran's comprehensiveness and agreement with earlier scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's inclusion of principles of all future events and its agreement with previous scriptures prove it is from Allah.

Core Idea
The Quran contains principles of all events and agrees with earlier scriptures, proving its divine source.

The fourth proof of prophethood from the Quran consists of two parts: first, the Quran contains the essential principles of all events until Judgment Day, which only the Knower of the unseen could provide; second, the Quran remarkably agrees with previous scriptures in describing the Prophet and his community, despite the time gap and without any dispute from the People of the Book. This lesson explores these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the fourth proof and its translation. Notice it includes two sub-arguments: comprehensiveness and agreement with earlier books.
The passage also mentions the story of the jinn and mubahala as further evidence.
2
Understand the comprehensiveness argument
The Quran states it contains principles of all occurrences up to the Day of Judgment. This implies it came from the Knower of the unseen.
This is a claim of comprehensiveness that human authorship cannot achieve.
3
Examine the agreement argument
The Quran matches the descriptions in previous scriptures (e.g., Torah and Gospel) about the Prophet and his nation, yet no one dared to deny it. This consistency across time shows a single divine source.
It also highlights the story of the jinn confirming the message.
4
Synthesize the proof
Both sub-arguments point to the same conclusion: the Quran is from God, the eternal, whose argument is one for all peoples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the second: the explanation of all the matters by which the scholars of the People of the Book came to know – along with the knowledge that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, never frequented them nor wrote a book with his right hand such that he could be suspected of learning from it – it is established that that was through the teaching of God, exalted is He. And with God is success. And the fourth: that God, exalted, gathered in the Quran the principles of all the events that will occur until the Day of Resurrection; this indicates that it came from the Knower of the unseen, to the point that He informed him of the principles of that. And also what appeared of the Quran's agreement with the rest of God's books and the description of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and his community, such as His saying: 'whom they find written with them' [7:157], and His saying: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' [48:29] to its end, and His saying: 'they recognize him as they recognize their own sons' [2:146], with none of them daring to deny or reject that. It is established that the One who revealed these books is God, exalted, and He made them all consistent despite the difference of times and the distance of ages, so that they might know that the Quran is from the One from whom the [other] books came, and that the One from whom the books came is eternal, has not ceased and does not cease, His argument in the first and the last being one. And also what preceded of the mention of the mubāhala (mutual imprecation) and what was of reports that he would be asked such and such, and it was as mentioned, and what is in the Quran of the story of the jinn and their confirmation and their testimony for him confirming the books. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the second, third, and fourth proofs of prophethood from the Quran. The second proof is based on the Prophet's illiteracy: the scholars of the People of the Book knew things unknown to others, but the Prophet never studied with them nor wrote, so his knowledge must have come from divine teaching. This references Quran 29:48. The third proof is the accurate prophecy of future events: the conquests, people entering Islam in multitudes, and the domination of Islam over other religions despite initial weakness – all came true as predicted. The fourth proof is that the Quran contains the principles of all future events, indicating it came from the Knower of the unseen. Then Maturidi adds an argument from the agreement of the Quran with previous scriptures and the recognition of the Prophet's description in them, with none of the People of the Book daring to deny it. This consistency across time shows they are from the same divine source. He concludes with a reference to the story of the jinn who testified to the truth of the Quran, confirming the message.

Key Terms
النوازل
موافقة الكتب
المباهلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the agreement of the Quran with previous scriptures indicate, according to Maturidi?
A) The Prophet copied from them
B) All scriptures come from the same divine source
C) The Quran is unnecessary
D) The People of the Book are the only authority

Key Takeaway
The Quran's comprehensiveness and its agreement with earlier scriptures show it is from the eternal God.

The Second Proof: Divine Teaching Through Illiteracy
Maturidi presents the second proof of prophethood based on the Prophet's illiteracy and the testimony of the People of the Book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Prophet's illiteracy and the testimony of the People of the Book as a second proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's knowledge without human teachers proves it came from Allah.

This passage presents Maturidi's second proof for the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). It highlights that the Prophet came to know matters that only the scholars of the People of the Book knew, yet he was unlettered and never studied with them. This establishes that his knowledge was directly from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi compresses the argument into a single sentence.
The phrase 'فيحتمل استعادته' may be ambiguous; the translation renders it as 'could be suspected of having retrieved it'.
2
Identify the two premises
Identify the two key facts Maturidi presents: (1) the Prophet knew matters known to the scholars of the People of the Book, and (2) he did not frequent them or write a book with his right hand.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, Maturidi concludes that the Prophet's knowledge must be from direct divine teaching. Reflect on why this is a strong proof for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني بيان جميع الأمور التي بها علم علماء أهل الكتاب. مع العلم بمن شهد رسول الله أنه لم يكن اختلف إليهم، ولا كان يخط كتابًا بيمينه فيحتمل استعادته، ثبت أن ذلك كان بتعليم الله تعالى إياه.
And the second: the explanation of all the matters by which the scholars of the People of the Book had knowledge, along with the knowledge of what the Messenger of God testified that he had not frequented them, nor would he write a book with his right hand so that he could be suspected of having retrieved it; it is established that that was by the teaching of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). Maturidi presents the second argument: the Prophet possessed knowledge of matters that only the scholars of the People of the Book (Jews and Christians) knew, yet he was illiterate and never studied with them. The phrase 'with the knowledge of what the Messenger of God testified' refers to the testimony of the Prophet himself that he did not attend their gatherings. Moreover, he could not write a book with his right hand, so it is impossible that he retrieved this knowledge from previous scriptures by his own effort. Therefore, it is established that this knowledge was given to him directly by God Almighty.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage proves that the Prophet was not a student of the People of the Book and could not have retrieved the information from written books, so his knowledge must be from God.

Key Takeaway

The Quran's Comprehensiveness and Agreement with Scriptures
Maturidi argues the Quran's comprehensiveness and agreement with previous scriptures prove its divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's all-encompassing principles and its agreement with earlier scriptures serve as evidence for its divine origin and the prophethood of Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Quran's universal scope and harmony with previous scriptures reveal its single divine source.

Maturidi now presents the fourth proof, which draws on two key features of the Quran. First, it contains the foundational principles for every event until the end of time, which only the Knower of the Unseen could reveal. Second, it agrees perfectly with all previous divine books in describing the Prophet and his community. Together, these show that the Quran comes from the same eternal God who sent the earlier scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on comprehensiveness
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the claim that the Quran contains principles for all events. Notice how Maturidi deduces that this knowledge must come from the Knower of the Unseen.
2
Examine the agreement with previous scriptures
Read the part about the Quran agreeing with earlier books and the citations about the Prophet. Consider why the People of the Book could not deny these descriptions.
Maturidi quotes verses that describe the Prophet in their scriptures.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence where Maturidi concludes that all scriptures come from one eternal God, whose proof is one. Reflect on how this unity across time supports prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أن الله تعالى جمع في القرآن أصول جميع النوازل التي تكون إلى يوم القيامة، دل أنه عن عالم الغيب جاء حتى أعلمه أصول ذلك. وأيضاً ما أظهر من موافقة القرآن سائر كتب الله وبيان نعت محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وأمته كقوله: الذي يجدونه مكتوبًا عندهم الآية، وقوله: محمد رسول الله إلى آخره، وقوله: يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم الآية، من غير اجتراء أحد منهم على إنكار ذلك ودفعه. ثبت أن الذي أنزل هذه الكتب هو الله سبحانه فجعلها كلها متفقة على اختلاف الأزمنة وتباعد الأوقات؛ ليعلموا أن القرآن من عند من جاء منه الكتب، وأن الذي جاء منه الكتب قديم لم يزل ولا يزال حجته في الأولين والآخرين واحدا. وأيضاً ما سبق من ذكر المباهلة وما كان من الأخبار أنه يُسأل كذا، فكان على ما ذكر على ما في القرآن من قصة الجن وتصديقهم وشهادتهم له بموافقة الكتب. وبالله العصمة.

The Quran's Inimitability and Consistency as Proofs of Divine Origin
Maturidi presents two arguments for the Quran's divine origin: its inimitability and its consistent harmony despite piecemeal revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Quran's inimitability and its consistent revelation over twenty years are decisive proofs that it comes from Allah.

Core Idea
The Quran's perfect harmony despite piecemeal revelation proves it is from the Knower of the Unseen.

Maturidi presents two complementary arguments for the Quran's divine origin. First, he asserts that the Quran's profundity surpasses all human capacity. Then, he highlights that although revealed over two decades, the Quran displays a flawless consistency that no human author could achieve. These points together establish the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) and its source as the Knower of the Unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the repetition of 'La quwwata illa billah'.
This is a short but dense passage.
2
Identify the two arguments
Maturidi presents two distinct proofs: (1) the Quran's inimitability even if all monotheists united, and (2) its consistency despite being revealed over twenty years. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the historical background
The Prophet was sent in a time of widespread idolatry. Maturidi emphasizes that not even one monotheist could be found. This makes the Quran's arrival even more miraculous.
This context strengthens the argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Quran's harmony indicates it is from 'Allam al-Ghuyub. Reflect on why consistency is a sign of divine origin.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا أن رسول الله بعث في عصر لم يُعرف فيه التوحيد، بل كان عباد الأوثان والأصنام والنيران فجميع ما أنزل عليه من القرآن هو من أنجح ما لو اجتمع موحدو العالم من مضى منهم ومَن يكون أبدا، على إظهار أدلته ما احتملت بلوغ عُشرها، فضلاً عن الإحاطة في ذلك الزمان الذي لا يُقدر على موحد واحد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن القرآن أنزل في عشرين سنة فصاعدا بالتفاريق؛ ما خرج كله على وزن واحد من النظم، على موافقة بعضه بعضاً مما لو احتمل كون مثله عن الخلق لم يمتنع من الخلق من الاختلاف في شيء من ذلك، دل أنه نزل من عند علام الغيوب. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the foundation of this is that the Messenger of God was sent in an age in which tawhid was not known; rather, they were worshippers of idols, statues, and fire. So all that was revealed to him of the Quran is among the most successful [proofs]; if all the monotheists of the world, past and future, were to gather to manifest its proofs, they would not be able to reach a tenth of it, let alone encompass it, in that time in which not even a single monotheist could be found. And there is no power except by Allah. Also, the Quran was revealed over twenty years or more in separate parts; it all came out on one pattern of harmony, agreeing with each other, such that if it were possible for the like of it to come from creation, the creation would not be prevented from differing in something of that. This indicates that it was sent down from the Knower of the Unseen. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Quran. Maturidi states that the Prophet was sent in an age dominated by polytheism, with no known monotheists. The Quran is so profound that even if all monotheists united, they could not produce its like. Additionally, although the Quran was revealed piecemeal over twenty years, it maintains a consistent harmony without contradiction. If it were from a human author, one would expect discrepancies. Therefore, the Quran must be from the Knower of the Unseen.

Key Terms
التوحيد
النظم
علام الغيوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the Quran could have been authored by the Prophet over time, with human imperfections.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the Quran's perfect consistency over 20 years defies human capacity; no human author could maintain such harmony over such a long period.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi use the revelation of the Quran over twenty years as a proof?
A) It shows the Prophet's patience
B) The Quran's consistency despite being revealed piecemeal indicates it is from God
C) It took a long time to complete
D) The people accepted it gradually

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability and its flawless consistency despite being revealed over twenty years prove that it is from the Knower of the Unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran's consistency over 20 years proves it is from the Knower of the Unseen, then how consistent is your own life in obeying it? Do your actions reflect the same unwavering harmony, or do you find contradictions between your beliefs and daily choices? Confess one area where you have been inconsistent and commit to aligning it with the Quran this week.
?
The passage says even all monotheists together cannot produce a tenth of the Quran's proofs. Yet how often do you seek validation and guidance from human opinions or societal trends rather than turning to the Quran as your ultimate source of truth? What specific change will you make today to prioritize the Quran over the world's voices?
?
The revelation came piece by piece over 20 years, yet it remained perfect. In your own journey of faith, do you trust that Allah's guidance is sufficient even when it comes gradually and in small doses, or do you grow impatient and seek shortcuts? What step can you take this week to embrace the process of gradual revelation in your own life?
